The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of the science of medicine, and having reached the peak of clarity and explanation in it, has seen fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of practical application.
Because practical application, in our country and in our time, is utterly lacking, almost to the point of the study of its knowledge being abandoned and its effects disappearing. And only a few traces remain in the books of the ancients, which have been corrupted by hands and tainted by error and confusion, until their meanings have become obscure and their benefit has receded.
Therefore, I have seen fit to revive it and compose this chapter on the path of explanation, clarity, and brevity, and to include depictions of cauterizing irons and other instruments of practice, since this is an addition to clarity and an aid to what is needed.
The reason there are no skilled practitioners with their hands in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first diligently study the anatomy as described by Galen. Until he grasps the benefits of the organs, their structures, humors, connections, and separations, and the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their number and exits, and the pulsating and still veins and their exit points.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the practical craft.  We have touched upon this point in the introduction to this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned will inevitably make a mistake that will kill people, as I have witnessed often in those who have imagined themselves experts in this field without knowledge or understanding.
For I have seen an ignorant physician, who incised a scrofulous tumor on a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, causing the woman to bleed to death in his hands.
And I saw another physician, while removing a stone from an elderly man, the stone being large, acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder's tissue. So the man died about three days later.  I was called to remove it, and upon seeing the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I judged it impossible.
And I saw another physician who earned his living by practicing medicine for some of the leading citizens of our town.
A dark-skinned boy had a fracture in his leg near the heel, accompanied by a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, quickly tightened the fracture with bandages and splints very tightly, leaving no breathing room for the wound.
Then he indulged his desires, and left the boy for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandage until his leg and foot were swollen and he was close to death.
I was called to him, and quickly loosened the bandage.  He gained relief and his pain subsided, but the damage had already taken hold in the limb, and I was unable to stop it.
The damage continued to affect the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a cancerous tumor.  After days, it ulcerated, and the patient's condition worsened. This is because if the cancer is purely of a dark, black humor, it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual treatment is divided into two categories: treatment accompanied by safety, and treatment often resulting in harm.
I have warned in every place in this book of work that involves risk and fear, and you should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to speak ill of you, be firm and cautious, and gentle and resolute in treating your patients.
Employ the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure illnesses.
Keep yourselves free from that which you fear might bring doubt upon your religion and worldly affairs; it is more enduring for your reputation and raises your status in this world and the hereafter.
Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad illness, and you will be known as a bad physician.
This article is divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization by fire and cauterization with a sharp medicine, arranged from the heel to the foot, the illustrations of instruments, cauterizing devices, and everything necessary for manual work.
And the second chapter.
In incision, lancing, venesection, cupping, surgeries, removal of arrows, and similar procedures, all arranged and illustrated with diagrams of the instruments.
And the third chapter.
In setting fractures, dislocations, and treatment of sprains, and similar procedures, arranged from the heel to the foot, and with illustrations of the instruments.
The first chapter.
In cauterization.
Before describing the procedure, it is necessary to discuss its benefits and drawbacks, and in which temperament it should be used.
I say that discussing the benefits and harms of cauterization is a lengthy and meticulous subject, a hidden secret, and many wise men have spoken upon it.
Their opinions differed, and I have summarized the essentials of their words for fear of being lengthy.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for every bad temperament, with or without matter, except for two temperaments: a hot temperament without matter and a dry temperament without matter.
Concerning a dry temperament with matter, they differed. Some said that cauterization is beneficial, while others said the opposite, that cauterization is unsuitable for an illness arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease by a hot, dry remedy.  The one who says the opposite claims that cauterization with fire may be beneficial for a hot, dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add the body of a person and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the person's body cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed this to me many times, but this should only be considered by someone who has practiced and trained extensively in the art of cauterization. And he must understand the diverse temperaments of people, the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold, moist diseases. All physicians agree on this, and they do not disagree about the benefit of cauterization in these cases.
Know, my children, that the secret of cauterization with fire, and its superiority over cauterization with a burning drug, is that fire is a simple element whose effect is limited to the organ cauterized, and it does not harm a connected organ except to a slight extent.
Cauterization with a burning drug may extend its effect to other organs, and may even cause a difficult-to-treat disease in the affected organ or even death.  Fire, due to its noble and pure nature, does not do this unless it is excessive.
This has been made clear to us through experience, due to long service, careful attention to the craft, and observing the true nature of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse.  Were it not inappropriate for this book, I would have presented you with a profound secret about fire, its action on bodies, and its ability to dispel diseases using a rigorous philosophical and demonstrative argument that would be too subtle for your understanding.
Know, my children, that they have differed regarding the appropriate time for cauterization, and they consider spring the best time.
I say that cauterization may be appropriate at any time, for any harm caused by the time will be overcome by the benefit derived from cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a strong, necessary and urgent ailment that cannot tolerate delay, for fear of a greater calamity following from it than the minor harm that arises from the time.
Do not, my children, consider the notion of the common people and ignorant physicians that a cauterization that cures a disease will never return and that you should regard it as a curse.
And the matter is not as you suppose, for cauterization is like a medicine that alters the temperament and dries the moisture that is the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is preferred to medicine for the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
The illness may return sometime in the future, according to the patient's temperament, the strength and severity of the disease, and the accumulation of humors in the body, and their negligence in acquiring suitable nourishment, and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild disease in a part of the body with few humors and moisture. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain, and similar cases, the pain may not return, and this will be the minimum.
Also, the common saying that cauterization is the last resort in medicine is correct, but not for the reason they believe, since they think that no treatment will be of benefit after cauterization, which is not the case.
The meaning of cauterization being the last resort in medicine is that, if various treatments are used for a disease and those remedies are ineffective, then cauterization, as the final measure, proves successful.  This is why cauterization is considered the last resort. Not in the sense that the common people and many ignorant physicians understand it.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron.  They said this because of gold's moderation and the nobility of its essence, and that it does not cause the cauterized area to fester.  However, this is not universally true.
For I have tested it and found that it only produces this result in some bodies, not in others.  Cauterization with gold is indeed better and superior to that with iron, as they said. However, if you heat a gold cautery in the fire, you cannot easily determine when it has reached the desired temperature for the gold's redness.
Because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and is distorted.  This results in the worker having difficulty with it. Therefore, cauterization by iron is, in our view, quicker, closer to correctness, and more suitable for practice, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization in sections, organized from the head to the foot, so that the student may easily find what he desires, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cauterization of the head: single cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excess moisture and cold in the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds from the head down to the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, toothache, and sore throat. And generally, for any illness arising from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, stupor, and similar ailments.
The procedure for this cauterization is that the patient should first be induced to vomit using a purgative medicine appropriate for the head, for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and habits.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, then have him sit before you, squatting with his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-oil cautery, which has this form.
If the bone is exposed to the extent of a bodkin's head or the size of a carbuncle, lift your hand; otherwise, reheat the same iron, or another, if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as I described to you, then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it.  Place it on the affected area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in clarified butter and leave it until the burning sensation subsides.  Then treat it with the four-part ointment until it heals, God willing.
They said that the longer a wound remains open, the more it discharges pus, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have said that the skin should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some of the bone's thickness is burned.  Then, what has been burned of the bone should be scraped away and subsequently treated.
Others said that the cauterization should be excessive, so as to affect the bone strongly, so that it falls away like a small piece of pottery or a small flake.  They claimed that this releases vapors from the head through that area and that the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not see these two types of cauterization at all, except in certain individuals, and by way of chance.  I believe leaving it alone is better.  With respect, the head weakens once its natural connections are broken, as we have observed in other organs.
Especially when the patient's head is naturally weak.  The first type of cauterization is safer and better, in my opinion.  Use it, and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
On cauterizing the head also.
If a chronic pain arises in the entirety of the head, and this affliction prolongs, and if the patient has tried applications, ointments, flogging, and dressings, and especially if the single cauterization described previously was performed, and none of these treatments worked, then consider... If the patient's head is strong constitutionally and not weak, and if they experience severe cold, cauterize again, slightly above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize a cauterization on each horn of his head until the thickness of the skin disappears, and the bone is exposed to the extent we described. And cauterize a cauterization at the back of his head, in the area known as the "axe" [or "the place of the axe"].  Be gentle in this one, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience intense pain unlike the pain of any other head cauterizations.  I will discuss this cauterization in its proper place.
The cautery used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head.  This is its form.
Chapter Three.
In the cauterization of the non-chronic fistula.
If, in a fissure of the head, pain occurs with a headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then the patient should be purged with medications that cleanse the head.  Use all other treatments that I have mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If this does not work, cauterization in this case has two approaches. Either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or by iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medicine, one takes one clove of garlic, peels it, and cuts its ends on both sides. Then, slice the site of the pain from the temple with a wide scalpel until a space under the skin forms that can accommodate the clove.
Place it under the skin until it disappears, then compress it firmly with a bandage for about fifteen hours, then remove it and take out the garlic.  Leave the wound for two or three days, then bandage it.
Place a cotton swab dipped in ghee on it until the area becomes suppurated, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. Or, if you wish, you may do this with some of the burning medicines that I have provided in the eighteenth article on burning medicines.
As for cauterizing with iron, it is done this way: heat the cauterizing iron, which is depicted as follows:
The cauterizing iron is called the nail-shaped iron because its head is shaped like a nail, with some indentations and a small protuberance in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while turning the iron a little bit at a time, the amount of skin burned should be about half its thickness. Then lift your hand so as not to burn the vein beneath, causing bleeding, then soak a cotton swab in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then apply the cotton swab with oil, then treat with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this abscess with the knife-like tip of the cauterizing iron, thereby avoiding cutting the vein in this chronic abscess, especially.
Chapter Four.
Treating chronic abscesses.
If you have treated the abscess with the aforementioned treatment and what we have described in the sections on diseases, and the treatment has not been successful, and you see a cause that the first cauterization, either with medicine or fire, cannot handle. Then you should heat the knife-like cauterizing iron until it whitens, after having marked the painful area with a line about half a finger in length or so.  Release your hand once, pulling until you cut the vein and reach the bone.
However, you should be careful not to touch the jaw that moves during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, causing spasms.
And be cautious and careful of the bleeding from the artery you cut, for there is risk in cutting it. Especially for those who do not know what to do and have not had proven experience; leaving the work alone is better.
A description of the treatment for accidental bleeding from an artery will come later in this book, God willing, if you see a condition for which this cauterization is not sufficient.
And if you find the patient's condition tolerable, cauterize a spot in the center of the head as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cauterization mentioned in the chapter on tying off arteries with the double-bladed cautery iron; it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing ear aches.
If an earache occurs due to cold and has been treated with laxatives and other treatments mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, then cauterize the ear with the cautery iron, which is shaped like a dot, as depicted here.
Then, after heating it, apply it around the entire ear, or around both of them if the pain is in both, in a circular motion. Move the cautery slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the area with ink. The cautery should be about ten dots in each ear, or approximately that amount. Then, treat the affected areas until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
In cauterizing the fistula.
The fistula treated by cauterization is of the type that arises from phlegm, as mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve spasm.
When you have treated this type of fistula with applications, poultices, and gargles, and your treatment has been unsuccessful, then the affected area should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be performed with one cauterization opposite the upper part of the ear, a little below the crown of the head, and another in the temple.  Its length should be equal to the length of the thumb.
Apply the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cautery iron, a type of knife previously shown, but slightly gentler as you see. The knife should have a slightly greater thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization of chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you have treated it as we described, and your treatment is ineffective and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one at the back of the head, as previously stated.
The cauterization method, as previously mentioned, and a cauterization may also be applied to the mouth of the stomach, which would be more effective, then treat as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
The one that is from phlegm, the patient should first be given large emetics and purging grains for the brain.
Then his entire head should be shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, should be applied to his back repeatedly, for it is a type of cauterization.
And do this in the same manner as mentioned there.  If he recovers with this, well and good; otherwise, cauterize three times at the back of his head, arranged in a row from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
Place the thickness of a finger between each cauterization.  Then treat the cauterization as previously described. If you wish to increase the treatment, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize at the temples, and then treat until he recovers.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and general relaxation of the body.
One should first cleanse the head with the methods mentioned previously.  Then shave the patient's head and cauterize with one cautery in the center of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck.  If, for a case of body relaxation, more than that is needed, and the patient can tolerate it, and the illness is strong and entrenched, then cauterize four times on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize deeply until most of the skin is burned, then raise your hand and treat it as previously described until it heals, God willing.  Use an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is from phlegm should be cauterized. First, his brain should be cleansed with large leeches, and with all the other treatments we have mentioned in the section, if the patient is large and able to take the medicines.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicine, then use gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head, and cauterize with one cautery in the center of the head, as previously described, and another in the back of the head. And cauterize one cautery on each temple.  If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, cauterize with the cauteries mentioned for paralysis and weakness of the body, on the cervical and lumbar vertebrae.  The cautery should be olive-oil based, according to the method previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this way.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupted humours and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauteries we have mentioned for paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge what cleanses his brain as previously described in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of flax like a circle, then place it on the center of his head.  The patient sits upright, holding it from each side. Then take one pound of old sheep's fat.
Then heat it moderately over the fire, as much as the finger can tolerate if inserted into it. Then pour it into the circle on his head and leave it to cool. Do this to the patient once a week with the rest of his good treatment until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small, scattered points, without holding the cautery in your hand, but rather by the smoke of it.  This type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton pad soaked in fat or chicken fat to it.
Chapter Twelve.
In the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
If you recognize the descending water in the eye by the signs mentioned in the classification, then immediately treat the patient, cleanse his head, and protect it from all moisture.  Have him sweat in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize a spot in the center of the head, and cauterize two spots on the temples if the water is descending into both eyes, or one spot on one temple if it is descending into one eye.
Then, with the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin, ensuring the cauterizations extend lengthwise and widthwise across the temples, and guard against blood loss.
If you see any of it, cut it at the site using whatever treatment is possible. We will later discuss the wisdom of clearing the arteries and cutting them while protecting against bleeding.
Two strong cauterizations may be performed at the nape of the neck, under the two bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic weeping sores.
If the weeping of the eye is chronic and persistent, and if it originates from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, externally, and you are certain that it is due to thick, cold, phlegmatic excess, then use the cauterization which I described in the beginning for the descending fluid. One cauterization in the middle of the head, two on the temples, and two at the nape of the neck, beneath the bones.  If further cauterization is needed, apply a cauterization on each side of the tear duct, at the edge of the eyebrow, with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
Concerning the cauterization of foul-smelling nasal discharge.
If the treatment mentioned in the section was not successful, then quickly apply cupping to the patient's temples for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the center with an olive oil cautery iron. Then cauterize with a nail cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, carefully avoiding the artery; do not cut it.  This is the image of the nail cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the cauterization of eyelid relaxation.
If the eyelid has relaxed due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, spaced apart from the temples, and each cauterization should be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization; it should only burn a third of the skin. The cautery iron should be of this shape.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its eyelashes turn inward and injure the eye, cauterization has two types. Either cauterization with fire or with a burning medicine.  If cauterizing with fire, first tell the patient to leave his eyelashes alone, if he is one who plucks them, so that they grow long and even.
If it injured it at the time of their growth, bind his eyes with a bandage so they don't move until they grow, and when they have grown and evened out, put the patient's head on your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of a leaf.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes, then place a cotton swab dipped in egg white or the juice of the squirting cucumber under the eyelid. Then heat the cautery iron—this is its form.
Then cauterize in the shape you marked, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin is burned, in the shape of the entire visible whole leaf of rue. A sign of successful work is to see the eyelid contracted and the hair raised from the eye's flesh.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, then apply a cotton swab with oil until the scab comes off. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair grows back later and the eyelid relaxes.
Then repeat the cauterization at that spot as you did at first. If the hair is on the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with burning medicine, tell the patient to let the eyelashes grow long and even.
Then make a shape of a rue leaf from paper, and take a weighed amount of ordinary soap and quicklime, one dirham or similar, pulverize them both thoroughly, doing this quickly so they do not dry out. Then spread it [the mixture] on the paper in the shape of a rue leaf and place it on the eyelid, one or both. Place a cotton swab soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and gently press it while moving it, whenever the patient feels the medicine burning, because he will feel a burning sensation like fire. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. If the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see, if you see the eyelid has risen, as it rises with cauterization by fire or by cutting, otherwise, apply more medicine to the spot where the medicine has not yet affected it and has not darkened, until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then apply cotton with oil to remove the burning sensation.
Then treat it with the date palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You should be extremely careful during the procedure to prevent any medicine from entering the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after several days and we fear something in the eye,
Then apply the medicine again to the relaxed part of the eyelid, specifically as you did at first, and treat it until it heals. This is a picture of a rue leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size and age, so adjust your cauterization accordingly. The correct method is not hidden from those experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the nasal polyp that appears in the eye socket.
If you have treated the polyp with the methods described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment is unsuccessful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and have a servant hold their head firmly, preventing any movement or shaking.
Then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or the juice of the mulberries on their eyes, and then heat the cautery iron, which is pictured here.
It should be hollow, like the shaft of a hawk's feather on one side where the cauterization will be performed, and if you wish, the other end can be pierced, or if you prefer, it can be solid like a rod. However, the hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, if the polyp is open, squeeze it and remove the pus, and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove the pus, then apply the very hot cautery to it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand slightly away from the eye, towards the nose, during the cauterization, so that you do not miss your mark or upset the patient, and the cautery does not fall on the eyelid and damage it. If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then if not, repeat the cauterization another time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton pad with oil and treat it with the drying ointment until it heals. If after forty days it does not heal, apply the corrosive, sharp medicine until the bone is exposed and remove it according to what will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the polyp, mentioned by some earlier scholars, is to carefully open the polyp, then insert a thin tube into the opening.  This is what it looks like.
Pour in molten lead weighing a dirham, holding the tube firmly in your hand, and ensuring the patient does not move at all, so that the molten lead does not run into their eye.  You should place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the polyp, curing it in a remarkable way. If the polyp heals with the cauterization and treatment described, then otherwise, the nasal perforation must be used and the polyp returned to the nasal passage as will be detailed in its dedicated section.
Chapter Eighteen.
Cauterization of cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, often called a hairlip, frequently occurs, especially in children's lips. If the cleft is treated as described in the classification and the treatment is unsuccessful, then a small, knife-like cautery iron should be applied in this manner.
Make the interior of the cautery as thin as a knife, then apply it to the cleft while hot and quickly until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft; then treat it with qirrot (a medicinal preparation) until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
Cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or the palate, or the roots of the teeth, then ulcerates and bursts, and a fistula develops from the discharge of pus, and treatment is ineffective, then a cautery should be heated to the size that will fit into the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the opening of the fistula, and hold it until the heated iron reaches its bottom and end. Do this once or twice, and then treat it afterward with the treatment we mentioned until it heals, God willing.
If the matter ceases and it heals, otherwise, the place must be examined, and the corrupted bone must be removed, as will be described in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Loose teeth and gums.
If the gums have become loose due to moisture, and the teeth have moved, and treatment with medication has been ineffective, place the patient's head on your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image will come after this, after placing the tube on the tooth.
And quickly insert the hot cautery iron into it, and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then remove your hand, then repeat the cautery iron several times as desired, then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water.
And he holds it for an hour and spits it out, then the loose tooth will become firm, and the relaxed gums will become firm and the bad moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is caused by cold or worms, and treatment with medication has not worked, cauterization can be done in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
For cauterization with oil, take cow's butter, boil it in an iron spoon or a shell, then take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it into the boiled butter, and place it on the aching tooth quickly, holding it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth; if desired, dip wool or cotton into cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then place the heated iron on top until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
For cauterization with fire, use a brass or iron tube, somewhat thick-walled so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.  Then heat the cautery iron, place it on the tooth itself, and hold it until the iron cools.  Repeat this several times.
The pain will disappear either on that same day or the next.  Following the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the shape of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer, as is possible.  This is the shape of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing swine.
If the swine are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and do not respond to maturation with medications, and you desire rapid maturation, then heat the hollow cautery, which has this shape, its ends perforated.
To allow smoke to emerge from the other end during cauterization, place it on the same tumor, once and again if necessary, until reaching the depth of the tumor.  If the tumor is small, adjust the cautery to the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days, and apply a cotton swab soaked in clarified butter to remove what was burned by the fire.  Then treat it with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.

Then treat him with what has been mentioned previously until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and coughs.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moisture, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, hollow, depressed areas, and once more in the center of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be in the form of a nail, as previously described.  If you prefer, the cauterization can be done by dripping with a cautery called the "point," whose form was previously described in treating earaches.  The points should be from about thirty to around that number.
Then treat him according to what was previously mentioned, until he is healed.  You can also use a cautery with three prongs, like this, and use it quickly, because you cauterize three times in a single application.  This is the shape of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
In cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moisture, or if it was not fixed when the dislocation was reduced so that it becomes a habit to relocate and then dislocate at the slightest movement as we have observed, then the joint must first be reduced. Then the patient lies on their back or on their right side, and the skin inside the armpit is pulled upward with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then the double-pointed cautery, whose image is this, is heated.
Then the skin is cauterized with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization is four cauterizations.  Sometimes it is cauterized with a three-pointed cautery, in which case the shape of the cauterization is six cauterizations, and the points are on the thinness of the tissue.
And this is the image of the three-pointed cautery.
And one more may be added to this number, making eight cauterizations. Then the crushed balls with salt are applied to the cauterization. The patient should remain at rest and not move the limb for a period of time until it strengthens. If the dislocation was upwards, this is rare.
Cauterize above the shoulder blade with one good nail-like cauterization, or many small cauterizations.  The joint will strengthen, the moisture will disappear, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If the stomach is affected by excessive cold and moisture, disrupting its balance, and frequent ailments occur despite various treatments, the patient should lie on their back, with legs and arms extended, and then be cauterized three times. Cauterize under the breastbone with a nail-shaped cautery to the extent of a finger.  Two additional cauteries should be placed below, one on each side, forming a triangular shape.  The cauteries should be spaced far enough apart so as not to coalesce if they become infected.  The depth of the cauterization should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin.  The shape of the cauteries should be [as described].
In this manner and to this extent, and no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cautery in the center of the stomach, in this manner.

Chapter Twenty-seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.
If a cold, damp, or excessively hot-wind affliction affects the liver, causing it to depart from its natural state, and the remedies described in the preceding classifications have failed. The patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries of this shape and size must be marked on the liver, situated below the clavicles, where the human forearm ends.
Each cautery should be about the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be applied along the body in a straight line. Do not press the cautery iron tightly.
The depth of the burn should be no more than half the thickness of the skin.  The patient should stand on their feet, or if unable, lie down with their legs extended and arms raised. This is the form of the cautery iron.
You may perform these cauteries with a sharp-pointed cautery iron, if you have skill and dexterity in the craft, taking care not to over-cauterize and burn all the skin, thus reaching the abdomen and the intestines, for the skin there is thin.  Be aware of this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
Cauterization of a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you want to determine whether the swelling is in the liver's flesh or its membrane, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and pain, but without intensity.
If it is in the liver's membrane, the pain will be intense and severe.  If the physicians have exhausted their treatment attempts, the patient should lie on their back, and the swollen area should be marked with ink.
Then, heat the cautery iron in the fire; it is a cautery iron resembling a plowshare, and this is its image. Cauterize it with a single cautery, until all the skin is burned and the cautery reaches the membrane, so that all the matter is expelled.  Then treat it as an abscess until it heals.
This type of cautery should only be performed by someone who has extensive experience in medicine and has treated these illnesses repeatedly by experience.  Only then should one undertake such a procedure; to avoid it is better.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing cold fistulas with the roots of the zarawand plant.  Take one long, dry root of the zarawand, as thick as a finger.  Dip it in oil and then hold it in the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the veins, sloping towards the area under the chin.  And two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, sloping slightly backwards.  Another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. Three cauterizations on the back, one between the shoulders, and two on the sides of the groin, below the cauterization between the collarbones.
One should not make the cauterization deep, but rather on the surface of the skin, lightly. Some of the ancients have mentioned that some people used an iron cautery, similar to a spatula, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reaches the affected area.
The pus will drain as described for liver tumors, and in this case, cauterizing the abscess carries the risk of the patient dying immediately, or of a persistent fistula forming at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If treatment of splenic disease, as described in the previous section, proves ineffective, cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all correct: one is to cauterize the spleen three or four times, in a line along its length.
In the same manner as cauterizing the liver, described previously, and with the same cauterizing instrument; the distance between each cauterization should be the thickness of a finger, or slightly more; the patient should lie on their back.
The other method is at the elbow; elevate the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends, raising the skin the width of the body, so the cauterizations run along the body's length.
Then, very carefully insert the cauterizing probes, so they pierce the skin on the opposite side; remove the cautery.  The cauterizations will thus be four.  If desired, a three-pronged cautery may be used.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing the pus to drain for many days; this is more effective than any other prior treatment.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is beneficial specifically in ascites. If, after treating the person with dropsy using the methods we have described in the sections, your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two behind his back—one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterization open to drain pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient's treatment after cauterization, providing the necessary care to bring the affected areas together, so that healing occurs quickly.  The cautery iron used for the back is pointed, as previously described, and the one for the other areas is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If the feet and legs swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot at the point between the little and ring fingers.
Hold your hand with the cautery iron straight, do not bend it. Then lift your hand and do not put it back down, for the yellow water will drain out, and the cautery iron will be in this shape.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg. The cauterization should be with the sharp end of the cautery iron, along the length of the leg: one below the knee, the other lower down towards the middle of the leg.  And two cauterizations on each thigh.
Leave the cauterizations open without treatment for a long time, allowing the water to drain.  Then treat them with the rest of the methods we have mentioned.
The thirty-third chapter.
On cauterizing diarrhea.

Chapter Thirty-five.

Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula in the perineum and its surrounding areas.

Insert it hot into the same fistula, aligning it with the depth of the fistula and the extent the probe entered. And be cautious of all these locations.  You should only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area, and you assess that it is not perforating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area seals shut, and the discharges stop, and it remains like this for a time, know that it has healed completely.
If the discharges do not stop, know that it is perforating, or there is a corrupted bone in its depth, or something similar, which will be discussed in its proper place.

Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous humidity may build up in the hip joint, causing dislocation.  A sign is that one leg is longer than the other when compared; and if the dislocated area is examined, a gap is found. Chapter Forty-one.
In cauterizing the sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint caused by cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient as described in the classification, but the treatment is ineffective and the condition persists, then the patient should be relieved of the thick humors by taking  rotten fruit or prickly pear fruit, or similar remedies.
Then cauterize.  The cauterization has two forms: either with burning medications, or with fire.  Cauterization with fire can be done in various ways, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
This produces a suitable depth.  The distance between each cauterization should be about the thickness of a finger. The cautery should be olive-colored.  A cauterization can also be made in the center, on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations in total. If desired, cauterize with the circular method previously mentioned for dislocated hip, using a single cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip and burns the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica, if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize with two cauterizations on the thigh in the location where the patient indicates pain, and a cauterization above the heel, about four fingers to the outer side.  The cautery should be bladed, and the depth of the cauterization should only be the thickness of the skin.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize there with the pointed cautery three, four, or more times, if needed.  If the pain is indicated below the knee, towards the leg, cauterize there with a single bladed cauterization.
In all your cauterizations, be careful not to reach a nerve or major artery, lest you inflict a grave injury or ailment upon the patient.
I have observed one and another who cauterized above the heel and overdid the cauterization, resulting in gangrene of the leg, reaching down to the foot and its complete ulceration, ruining the entire leg.  Subsequently, diarrhea and death ensued. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize them in precisely the same manner.
Some scholars among the wise have described a hip cauterization with these characteristics: a cup-like instrument is made of iron, with a diameter of half a span.  Its opening is the thickness of a date pit, or slightly less.  Inside this cup is another cup, and a third cup.
The distance between each cup is the width of a thumb.  All the cups are open on both sides and their height is approximately one or two thumbs.  A handle is fitted to the cups, made of iron. This is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and sparks fly.  It is then placed upon the affected hip, with the patient resting on the healthy side.  Three circular cauterizations are performed at once.  Then, the patient is left for three days, bandaged with clarified butter, and the wound is left open for several days.
Then it is treated with ointment until healed. The author of this book said that this type of cauterization is rarely used because of its unpleasantness and frightening appearance, and few can endure it.  However, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it and are afflicted in that location.
As for cauterization with burning medications, it is done by creating two cups resembling the throat where the latch of the door goes, made of copper or iron.
There will be a height of about two thumbs on each side of them, and the distance between them will be the thickness of a finger.  The bottom and top of the cups are open, and they are joined together in this manner.
Then, place them on the affected hip, with the patient lying on their healthy side.  Hold your hand gently, then pour a little heated, sharp water between the two circles.  Hold it for about the duration of an hour glass.  The patient must endure the sting and burn, as it will feel like fire.
Until the sting subsides. Then remove the instrument and wipe the affected hip with fresh water, completely removing the sharp water. Leave it for three days, applying grease, until the blackness of the burn disappears.  Leave it for some days, allowing pus to drain; it is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.  If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold that holds the water, in the same way as you did for the hip.  This is a description of sharp water.  It has been proven in my treatise on the preparation of remedies.
Take one part of caustic soda and one part of quicklime. Grind them together and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, large enough for a pestle to enter. Place another glazed pot underneath the bottom of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the soda and lime, enough to cover them by a finger's breadth, after pressing them well with your hand.  Let the pot stand until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below. Then collect all that water and pour it over fresh lime and fresh soda.
Then it will be extremely sharp and effective in many medical procedures and cauterization of other parts of the body, as it acts like fire itself.  There are also other medicines that can cauterize the hip, such as thafsīya, balaḏir honey, and lime mixed with soap, all ground up.
Galen recounted a story about an ancient physician's treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly praising it, claiming that it needed no other treatment and cured the condition in a single application.
Even, perhaps, a patient would be carried into a bath and emerge cured.  One takes fresh green shiṭraj, or if no green is available, fresh dried shiṭraj, grinds it with some fat, and applies it to the hip where it hurts, or to the leg or thigh.
Apply the compress and leave it on for three hours, or until the patient feels the burning subside.  Then, bathe the patient.  If the body is sweating, place them in a basin.  The pain will disappear and the patient will recover, God willing. If not, reapply the dressing after ten days.
Then the patient will recover, God willing.  Know that no one should use these medicines for cauterization unless the body is emptied.  Dioscorides has mentioned that goat's dung, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization is performed as follows:  Take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep area between the thumb and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat dung.
Set it ablaze with fire until it becomes ember, then place it on the wool and leave it until it extinguishes.  Repeat this until sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing.  This type of cauterization is called cauterization with dung.
Chapter Forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from many causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It may result from the accumulation of a cold, moist substance.  Cauterization is only effective in this particular type of pain.
After the patient has vomited, and after applying a rotten herb or similar remedies, one should cauterize the back where the pain is located in three rows, each the width of the table.  Mark the area with ink, and apply five cauterizations (or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and strength) to each row.
Use a pinpoint cautery iron, or if preferred, use a medium-sized nail-shaped cautery iron for three or four cauterizations, following this method.
Chapter Forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a swelling.
This ailment frequently affects young children.  A sign of its initial stage in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and at the end of the spine's vertebrae, a prominent swelling will appear, protruding beyond the other vertebrae.
If you see this and wish to stop its progress, cauterize it with a circular cautery iron, shaped like this. Apply the cauterization evenly to each side of the vertebra.  If you prefer, cauterize around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery iron, in rows of two or three, keeping the points close together.  Then treat the area until it heals as described.
Beware that cauterization should not be used on a swelling caused by nerve tension.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are caused by cold dampness affecting any part of the body, if the pain occurs in the legs, physicians customarily call it gout, particularly.
If cold gout, the cause, is treated with the methods we have mentioned in the classification and the pains do not subside, then cauterization will alleviate them.  Cauterize around the joints of the legs with many cauteries after vomiting. The cautery should be olive-oil based, of medium heat, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize on the face of the leg, use a point cautery.  If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, as often happens, cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries on each side using the same olive-oil based cautery.
