Cauterize both of them [the elbows or shoulders] from every side.  The patient must also take good care and use medicine; if he has a good diet and eliminates phlegm, he will recover with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some intestines and bowel contents descend to the testicle, and this is a recent onset, then the patient should be instructed to avoid food that day and use laxatives to evacuate the bowels.
Then, have the patient lie on their back before you, instructing them to hold themselves until the bowel contents or intestines emerge.  Then, push them back using your finger.  Then, mark the area under the hernia on the pubic bone with ink, a mark resembling a semicircle, its ends extending towards the upper body.
Then, heat a cautery iron, its image being like this.
Until it becomes white, throwing off sparks. Then, push the bowel or contents back into the abdomen.  Then, a servant should place their hand on the area to prevent the bowel from protruding.  The patient's legs should be spread apart, and a cushion placed underneath them. Another servant should tie their legs together.
Another servant should hold the patient's hands on their chest. Then, place the cautery iron on the same mark, your hand holding the cautery iron straight, until it reaches the bone, or repeat the process again if it does not reach the bone on the first attempt.
Maintain your effort to prevent the bowel from protruding while cauterizing, so as not to burn the patient, causing either death or a great calamity.  Know that if your cauterization does not reach the bone, your work will not be successful.  The cautery iron should be gentle for children, according to their size, and for adults, according to their size.
Then, treat the cauterized area with ghee after three days, to remove the harshness of the burn.  Then treat it with other ointments until it heals.  Let the patient lie on their back for forty days, until the wound is closed.
His food during his treatment should be soft, to prevent the bowel from protruding during bowel movements.  Then, if he wishes to stand up after forty days, a firm bandage should be used and kept on for another forty days.  Reduce the strain and fullness from food, drink, and loud noises.
If this treatment is applied as described, he will heal completely, God willing. I will discuss the treatment of hernias by incision in its appropriate section, God willing.  As for a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is recent, if you want it not to worsen, cauterize the hernia with a circular cautery, to the extent of its size. Let the amount of skin burned be about a third of it, then treat it as described.  It will not worsen, God willing. Some earlier physicians mentioned that the hernia should be cauterized with a triangular cautery iron in this manner.
After marking the hernia with ink, make a line across the top of the hernia, and another line down below it.  Then, use a single, nail-like cautery in the middle. The initial cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If a part of the body suffers from a fall or blow, causing pain that persists for a long time and is not relieved by medication, it should be cauterized with a pinpoint cautery. The cauterization of each body part should be in accordance with its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
This will relieve the pain and the fistula. If the patient is cured by the first cauterization, then it is good. Otherwise, the cauterization should be repeated, because it is the nature of these ailments to spread from the affected part to those nearby. Therefore, cauterization should be applied until the patient is cured.
Chapter Forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for those with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that results from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, examine him.  If the leprosy is in its early stages and you have treated it as described in the classification, and it has not declined or stopped, and you fear that the corruption will spread to the patient's entire temperament, cauterize the head five times. One in the usual center of the head, a second below it, towards the forehead near the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind on the nape of the neck.  Apply the cautery somewhat firmly until it affects the bone slightly.
Scales will peel away, making it easier for thick vapors to escape.  Cauterize the spleen with a cauterization as previously described.  However, if the leprosy has persisted and become clearly evident, it is appropriate to cauterize the head in the manner described above. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the cervical vertebrae, six on the thoracic vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine and another above it on the same surface, and two on each thigh, one on each hip. Two on each knee, one on each, two on each shoulder, two on each elbow, two on the ribs of the chest, and a cauterization on each joint of his fingers and toes, and on each heel of his feet and on each wrist.
Preserve the nerve behind the heels so as not to burn them.  A cauterization may be applied to the pubic bone, another to the stomach opening, and another to the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations you apply, the more effective and beneficial they will be.
Know that the patient feels less pain from the cauterization than a healthy person, because his body has become numb.  The cauterizing tools should vary in size according to the organs and joints, as previously described regarding the properties of the instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with fine crushed cumin seeds, honey, and other remedies until he is healed, God willing.
The forty-eighth chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness. When a part of the body has become numb and has been treated with medicines, ointments, and bandages, but has not healed, cauterize the same numb part with cauterizations according to the size of the affected body part, and let your cauterizations be slightly deeper in thicker skin.
Then treat it with the ointment until it heals.  Cauterization may be used for some numbness that affects the hand or foot, in the spinal vertebrae at the exit point of the nerve that moves that limb, to relieve the numbness.
Only those who are knowledgeable about the anatomy of organs and the exits of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
The forty-ninth chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it with a moderate depth, according to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil, and the leaves of the horsetail plant, and dove's blood or the blood of vultures (each in equal parts). Mix them together, apply to a cloth, and keep the area covered until it heals.
The fiftieth chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular iron around it, as it revolves. Some scholars have suggested a severe cauterization in the middle, but I do not agree with this, as I anticipate that it will ulcerate, and I have witnessed this several times.
Therefore, the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we stated, or with many small cauterizations.
The fifty-first chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of boils.
If a boil has occurred and is slow to mature, either due to the agent that caused it, or because of the patient's age (if the patient is elderly and anemic), or due to time, and you wish to hasten its maturation, then cauterize around it with many small, puncturing cauterizations.
Then leave it, for this will hasten its maturation.  If you wish to further cauterize it, heat the cautery iron, which is depicted in this image.
And place it in the center of the boil until the skin is pierced.  Let the cauterization be on the underside, so that the pus may drain easily.  Then treat it as appropriate until healed.
The twenty-fifth chapter.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are nothing but corruption that consumes the tissue, just as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place where cauterization with fire is appropriate, heat a cautery iron with many small and large nails, according to what is suitable for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing of it remains. Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scab and corruption are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments. If you see that after three days the flesh grows healthy and without corruption, then leave it be; otherwise, repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas. Ulcers can also be treated with strong medication.
For it is faster and more effective than using fire, and I have already mentioned the treatment of ulcers with strong medication in the classification, so refer to it there when needed.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a rough sore that causes pain to the foot, often occurs in the soles of the feet.  The cauterization can be performed in two ways: either by fire or by a strong caustic liquid.
As for cauterization by fire, heat a hollowed-out cautery iron, resembling an eagle's beak, made of iron, that is sized to encompass the nail on all sides and thin at the edges. Then, bring the hot cautery down onto the nail and turn your hand with the cautery around the nail until the cautery reaches the nail's depth.
Leave it for three days, allowing pus to form, then bandage it with crushed wild barley mixed with salt, leaving the dressing on overnight. It will detach from its roots. Then, treat the wound with a healing ointment until it is healed, if the nails are not inverted.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  A tube of copper, iron, or eagle's beak should be used to press onto the nail or wart.  Then, a small amount of strong caustic liquid is poured into the tube.
Hold the tube with your hand, gently rotating it to ensure that the edge of the tube affects the base of the nail and the strong caustic liquid penetrates to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the strong caustic liquid for a while.
Then, leave it; the nail will detach from its roots.  Do this with each one until you have dealt with all of them on the body.  Then treat the sites where they detached with ointments that promote healing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On cauterization.
If a person has a painful condition due to cold in the nerve, or fever, or other causes, one should cauterize four or five times on the back of the spine, one cauterization between each bead, and one on the chest. And on his stomach, one cauterization with the olive oil cautery, for the painful condition will subside and quickly mature the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-five.
On cauterizing a wound occurring on the body.
Ugly boils may arise on the body from thick, corrupt cold matter.  Upon their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a heated stick, having its end ignited with fire, or with a long piece of willow, or with a lens cautery.
Boils may be cauterized in their initial stages in this manner.  The beneficial effect will not increase, and the illness will not subside unless the patient has been bled beforehand.
Chapter Fifty-six.
On cauterizing bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Bleeding frequently occurs from an artery severed during an external wound, or when incising a tumor, or cauterizing a limb, or similar procedures, and it is difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly apply your index finger to the mouth of the artery, pressing firmly until the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing escapes. Then, place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are extremely hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery ruptured, and apply the cautery to the same vein after quickly removing your finger, holding the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, when you remove your finger from the artery's opening and extinguish the cautery, the blood spurts out again, quickly take another cautery from the prepared fire and continue this process with one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve, lest another affliction befall the patient. Know that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be stopped, especially a large one, except by one of four methods: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop. Or, it can be tightly tied with threads, or the drugs that stop the flow and bind it tightly can be applied.
As for those who attempt to stop it with a bandage, or by tightening with cloths, or by applying burning substances, and the like, it will not stop in this way, except rarely.  If someone experiences this and is not attended by a physician or by medicine, then let him immediately place the index finger over the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should press it firmly until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound and onto the artery and finger.  The finger should not be removed from there with constantly very cold water until the blood solidifies and thickens and stops.  Meanwhile, consider what cauterization or medicine is needed.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, and wounds, and similar things.
Khalaf said: We have already discussed in the first chapter all diseases treatable by cauterization with fire and burning medications, and their causes, reasons, and instruments, and the different kinds of cauteries, arranging this from the front to the back as chapters.
And I will follow the same path in this chapter so that the student may readily grasp what they seek.
Before I begin with that, you children, it is necessary to know that this chapter contains more uncertainties than the first chapter on cauterization.
For this reason, the warnings in this chapter must be more severe, because in this chapter, the practice often involves expelling the blood, on which life depends, when opening a vein or cutting a tumor, puncturing an abscess, treating a wound, extracting an arrow, cutting a stone, and similar situations, all of which are accompanied by uncertainty and fear, and in most of them, death occurs.
And I advise you to avoid what is questionable.  For in this craft, there may come before you people of various kinds with many illnesses. Among them, some will have grown weary of their disease, and consider death as light.
Because of the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, and because of the signs of death present in their illness, some will offer you their wealth, hoping for health.  Their illness is deadly.  Therefore, you should absolutely not accept help from those who exhibit these characteristics.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness. Do not undertake anything like that unless you have certain knowledge of a favorable outcome. In all your treatment of patients, use the knowledge and forewarning of what will become of their health.
For in that, you will gain praise, glory, remembrance, and thanks. May God inspire you, my children, with wisdom, and not deprive you of correctness and success.  For it is in His power; there is no god but Him.
I have arranged the chapters of this section, following on from the previous section on cauterization, from the head to the foot, so that you may find what you seek, God willing.
Chapter One.
On treating the water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment frequently affects children at birth if the midwife presses the child's head roughly.  It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than children, and all those I have seen have died quickly. Therefore, I have decided to abandon treatment for it. I have seen a child whose head filled with water and the head grew larger each day. Until the child could not sit up due to the enlargement of their head, and the moisture increased until they perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone or under the bone on the peritoneum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, then a single horizontal incision should be made in the center of the head, about two finger-widths long, to allow the moisture to drain. This is the image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, make two intersecting incisions in this manner.
If the moisture is under the bone, and the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water sinks if you press it with your hand inward (this should be clear to you), then three incisions should be made in the center of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will come out. Then the incisions should be tightened with bandages and splints.  Afterwards, apply poultices of herbs and oil to the area, up to five days.  Then, the bandages are removed, and the wound is treated with ointments.  Do not allow the head to be too tight. The patient should eat a diet of dry foods, low in moisture.
Until the affected area is strong and healed. Another method of incision is to look at where the bone of the swelling and the accumulation of water is visible, as it may be more prominent in the back of the head, the front, or on the right or left side.
Aim the incision where the swelling and accumulation of water are evident.  Make the incision as you can, taking care not to cut an artery, causing bleeding, which would lead to the patient's death from that bleeding while expelling the moisture.
The Second Chapter.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the "hissers".
If someone experiences a severe affliction in the eyes or chest, and treatment with medicine is ineffective, the best treatment is to cut these two arteries.
If you wish to cut them, you must first shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery.  Then, hold the patient's neck by his/her garment.  Then look where the pulse beats, at the two sunken locations behind the ears. They are rarely hidden, except in some individuals.
Then mark them with ink.  Then cut them with a blunt dissecting knife, down to the bone, cutting across the breadth of the head. Or, if you wish, you insert the dissecting knife under the artery and draw it upwards in a cut, the length of the cut being about two clenched fingers.
When the artery is cut, the blood will flow in a pulsating manner, spurting forward in a continuous stream.  If the artery is not visible to the touch, you should measure from the ear by about three fingers, then mark with ink and cut to the bone.  What is necessary...
Approximately six ounces of blood will likely flow immediately.  Sometimes more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries. Then, observe the wound. If any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot swelling does not occur.  Then, bind the wound with a linen thread, and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
The arteries in the temples.
If a person experiences chronic headaches or acute ailments caused by sharp moisture and heat in the temple muscles, or severe, chronic headaches, and the usual medical treatments have been unsuccessful, then we have tried, in these cases, to cauterize or cut the arteries of the temples, as we have described.
The procedure for cutting them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the visible artery in the temple should be located.  Its pulse will make it evident; it is rarely hidden, except in certain individuals.
Or, in cases of severe cold, if it is not visible, the patient should tighten their neck with their clothing.  Then, you should rub the area with a cloth or apply hot water until the artery clearly and distinctly appears. Then take the lancet, which is depicted in this way. Then, gently slice the skin with the lancet until you reach the artery.  Then, insert a hook and pull it upwards, bringing it out from the skin and freeing it from the membranes beneath on all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the hook's end. Then, cut off a portion where its ends are separated and contracted, and no bleeding occurs. If it is not severed and not cut, the blood will not be quelled at all.  Then, drain from six to three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread, whether of silk or bowstrings, so that the wound will not fester before healing, causing bleeding.
Then cut off the excess between the ligatures; do this at that time or later, if you wish to cauterize to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery, so that its ends are severed, this will accomplish the same work or even better, as we have said.
However, if the patient has a fever or a heated temperament, since cauterization helps to consume moisture, it will be even more beneficial.  After dissecting the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton and firm bandages applied.  After healing, treat with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and with dressings until healed, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your procedure, immediately cauterize it, or fill the area with alum and hold your hand firmly until the bleeding stops. If nothing of that sort is available, place your finger over the site to cause clotting.
Apply intensely cold water to the area until the inflammation subsides and it is properly tightened.  A simpler and easier alternative to dissecting the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two places with ink.
Heat it intensely until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed at two points so that the ends are separated.  It will not heal with this cauterization.  This is a picture of the cautery.
The double-bladed cautery should be sharp, resembling small knives, but significantly less sharp than a knife.  If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach it quickly and the flesh would not separate quickly.  If there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire will adhere to it.
It quickly cuts through the flesh. This method is superior, easier, and simpler than any other method.  The distance between the blades should be the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
In the treatment of persistent hot weeping from the eyes.
If the weeping is constant and originates from the veins on the skull's exterior, and if no medicinal treatment provides any benefit, and you see the patient's face reddened... He feels a creeping sensation in his forehead, like an ant's crawl, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their edges eroded, and their eyelids stuck together due to the intensity of the tears.  Early practitioners of treatment resorted to this procedure, and they call it "treatment by the sword." It involves instructing the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, lengthwise, each incision approximately two fingers long, parallel to the nose's length in the center of the forehead, the second slightly offset from the temporal muscle action, and the third on the opposite side.
Care must be taken to avoid cutting the arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the jaw's contact.  After each incision, leave a space of three fingers' width.  Have with you sponges or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then, insert the sharp, double-ended scalpel, which has this form. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, use it to detach all the skin and periosteum between the incisions. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, and then remove the scalpel. And insert into the first incision another instrument, called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth/blunt on the other, which has this form.
Place the sharp side of the scalpel upward, toward the flesh attached to the skin, and the smooth side toward the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, and sever all the vessels that run from the head to the eyes, without cutting the surface skin.
Then do the same in the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas of clotted blood.  Then, in each incision, place a cotton wick, and apply a dressing soaked in a mixture of syrup, oil, or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, dissolve the ligaments, and apply a lot of lukewarm water. Then treat with basil ointment and rose oil, and other remedies used for wounds, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharges from the inside of the head to the eyes.
When someone has frequent, severe, persistent discharges, and the eyes appear sunken and small, and vision is weak, and the eyelids are ulcerated, and eyelashes are falling out. And there will be a severe, painful ache deep within the head, and repeated sneezing.  Know from these symptoms that these fluids and discharges come from many deep veins.  The best treatment for this is to have the patient shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead.
And there will be a severe, painful ache deep within the head, and repeated sneezing.  Know from these symptoms that these fluids and discharges come from many deep veins.  The best treatment for this is to have the patient shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead. Or raise it slightly in width, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple. The incision should be into the bone, keeping your hand away from the movable masseter muscles when chewing.  Once the bone is exposed and all the blood has been wiped away with a sponge...
Then separate the edges of the incision with used cotton or linen thread, and secure it from above with sutures.  Soak the sutures with medicine and oil so as not to cause a hot swelling.  When you have resolved it and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone so that flesh begins to grow there.
Then treat it with a dried preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts of a substance called *qalfuniya* and preparing a salve from them, which is used to stimulate flesh growth in wounds like these.
Chapter Six.
On treating what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic or semi-metallic stone, like iron or glass; or a plant seed, like chickpeas or nuts, and the like; or a liquid, such as water or vinegar, and similar things.
Or it is an animal.  If a stone or something similar that does not grow within the ear has fallen in, expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the stone, place some violet or ceruce oil in it, then try to remove it by moving the head or by inducing sneezing with a stick. And close the nostrils when sneezing occurs, after placing a bandage of linen or wool around the ear and extending the ear upward. Often, this treatment removes the object. If it does not, try removing it with gentle gauze, which has this form.
If it comes out with the gauze, otherwise, try to remove it with a gentle, slightly bent, blind hook.  If it doesn't come out with that either, make a brass tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil so that the wind has no passage except through the tube. Then pull it out with a strong pull.  This often removes it. If it does not come out with the methods described, take a small piece of Nabataean gum or a bird-catching gum and place it on the end of the tube.
After wrapping it tightly with a piece of cotton, gently insert it into the ear canal after the ear has dried from moisture.  If none of the described methods remove it, proceed to incision before a severe abscess or inflammation occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient from the temples, drawing blood according to the patient's strength. Then, place the patient before you and turn their ear upward. Make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the cartilage, in the lower portion of the ear.
Make the incision in a crescent shape until you reach the stone. Then remove it with whatever instruments are available. Then stitch the incision immediately and treat it until it heals.
If the object lodged in the ear is one of the types that swells and increases in size, try to remove it with the methods described. If this does not work, take a thin, delicate probe of this type.
Try to cut that type of fallen ear bean with it, but only do so if you are certain that the bean has become moistened by ear vapor, forming numerous small pieces. Then remove them with a blind probe, a gentle cloth, or suction.
As we mentioned, it is easier to remove [the object].  Regarding water entering the ear, the patient should first try sneezing with the kunds or, lying on the affected ear, filling it with old cotton. If this works.
Otherwise, take several small, smooth stones, warm them slightly by fire, and have the patient insert one into the ear canal. The patient should then place their foot on the affected side and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with stone after stone until all the water is expelled. Water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and burning the other end until it is mostly consumed. Then use another feather.
Repeat this process until all the water is removed, or extract it using the tube, as previously described for stones. As for removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, such as a flea or similar, treat it as described in the section [earlier].  As for...
If it is large enough to be felt, try removing it with swabs and hooks.  This is easier than all the other things that can occur in the ear.  As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you have treated them as described in the section on worms and your treatment is ineffective.
You should look into the ear in the sunlight. If you see any worms, remove them with swabs or delicate hooks. If you do not see any, take a tube—this is its form.
It is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top. Insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then apply strong suction. Repeat this several times until all the worms are expelled. If this does not work, seal around the tube with wax, as I described for stones.
If it is not expelled by the methods described, use the ear drops as previously mentioned, which the ancients used to kill worms.  You will find this in the section on ear drops.  Use this instrument to instill ointments and medications into the ear. This is its image.
As you see, it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top.  If you prefer, the projectile within the tube can be made of secure copper.  Alternatively, you can take a cord and tightly wrap cotton around one end.
Then place the ointment, juice, or any medication you desire into the tube, while it is in the ear.  Then, using the cord with cotton, press gently with your hand until the ointment enters the ear canal and the patient feels it.
Administer it to the patient internally.  Ensure that whatever you instill into the ear is warmed slightly in the fire. Be careful that the substance is neither excessively cold nor excessively hot, as the ear cannot tolerate such extremes.
Chapter Seven.
In treating ear obstructions.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unpierced.
Some people may also suffer from obstructions in the ear canal, caused by wounds or growths. This obstruction may be located deep within the ear canal, and therefore unseen.
Occasionally, it may be slightly exposed outside the ear canal, and thus visible.  Those obstructions deep within the ear, invisible to the eye, are most often difficult to cure, while those visible to the eye should be placed in the sun. And examine it.  If you see the obstruction externally, open it with a gentle scalpel, with this image in mind:
Its tip should have a small, limited projection, and the rest of the scalpel should be smooth on both sides to avoid harming the ear. If the obstruction is due to a growth within the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate forceps and cut it gently until all of the growth is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, heat it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient's experience of a hearing impairment, gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum.  Then, place a wick within the ear, sized appropriately to the width of the canal, and apply a coating of Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days until you are sure the wound has healed. Alternatively, take a wick, soak it in water, and dust it with powdered zea.
Use it.  If you see that the ear has become swollen with a hot tumor, you should remove the wick and replace it with another soaked in ointment made with rose oil, to reduce the hot swelling.  Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the section on cauterization.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  One should grasp them with a forceps or a hook and cut them off at their roots with a scalpel.  Then apply powdered antimony to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterization is better after the cutting, as it frequently returns if any part of the roots remains.  If cauterized with fire, the roots are burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of colds affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling a cold in its severity and hardness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called a cold, which is the gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is protruding on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, the treatment is simple; make a lengthwise incision across it.
Then gently remove it from all sides until it is separated.  Then suspend it with forceps and cut it, and if you cannot cut it without penetrating the eyelid, this will not harm the patient.  If the incision is large, stitch it up and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it, as the ointment will mend and knit it together.  If the cold is inclined towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, and suspend the cold with forceps without needing to make an incision, and remove it from all sides.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the incision, this will not harm it.  Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting the cold, and treat the area with something that will knit it together until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the sty that appears in the eyelid.
A sty is a swelling that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it most often appears in children. It weighs down their eyes, and they experience colds in the early morning hours, and they are unable to look at sunlight. Because tears rush to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces constantly and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, possibly swollen. The sty is clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the sty between them. The procedure is that the patient should place his head on your lap.
Then, take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick, encompassing the sty on all sides. Then place it on the sty and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the sty in the middle of the circle.
Then, with the lancet, slit the middle of that moisture, creating a slit across it.  The slit should not be larger than a phlebotomy incision.  As for depth, you should cut all the skin until you reach the sty.
In most cases, the sty will emerge from the slit as a white piece of fat.  Pull it outward with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, moving your hand back and forth until it heals. Be careful not to enlarge the slit, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the sty does not appear when you first slit it, gently increase the slit slightly until the sty emerges. Then pull it out as we described.  Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area and secure it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
Place it in the wound to dissolve any remaining moisture.  Then treat it until it heals.  If a hot inflammation develops at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eye dressings.
If superfluous eyelashes grow on the eyelid, exceeding the natural course and emerging under the normal lashes, they harm the eye and cause various ailments, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and opacity and thickness, to the point that this can cause blindness.
Eye dressings are of four types: either by cauterization with fire, or by a sharp medicine, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization; or the dressing may involve cutting and stitching, or using a splint, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it turns, then proceed, otherwise, insert a needle with a thread from beneath the eyelid and pierce the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself. Pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a rod, then cut the inner surface of the eyelid, away from the excess hair, with a lancet, from the larger to the smaller incision, then pull out the thread and place a small cotton or cloth pad beneath the eyelid, and then attach the eyelid with a bandage.
The shape should resemble a basil leaf, but the shape must be tailored to how much you want to lift the eyelid, because this may vary between individuals. Some require a certain amount of the eyelid to be cut, proportionate to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
Some require a smaller cut, all of this depending on the relaxation of the eyelid. Then, using a lancet, make incisions along the lines you marked, beginning from the larger to the smaller incision. Each incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a rod.
Then, insert a hook into one corner of the skin, then peel it all off.  Then, use stitches with a needle and fine wool thread to bring the two edges together. Wipe away the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows with a sticky substance.
You may wish to do this, or not bother.  The stitches and thread remain for about three or four days, then treat it.  If you wish, you can leave the wound un-stitched and treat it with something that dries and contracts it.  The eyelid will lift when the wound is closed and brought together; however, stitching is better.
This method of lifting the eyelid, described by the ancients, is laborious for the patient, yet a good method with no danger.  Another method of lifting the eyelid is to shape it like a basil leaf, as we described. Then, lift the eyelid with three hooks, which may be separate or grouped in this way:.
Then, cut away any excess eyelid with a small pair of scissors, in this way, cutting evenly.
If you cannot hold the hooks in place and the shape is not right, take a needle with thread, insert it into the center of the shape, insert another thread near the larger incision, and a third near the smaller incision.  Hold the threads evenly between your fingers, then lift your hand steadily with them.
Raise the entire marked skin as we described, then stitch the edges of the wound together and treat it until healed.  If a hot swelling appears after your cutting or incision, relieve the swelling with opobalsamum or similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also turn inside out.  In this case, use the cutting, stitching, and cauterization with fire or sharp medicine described earlier. Cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is less severe on the patient than cutting and stitching; cutting and stitching are more involved in these instances.
Cauterization with reeds is performed as follows: turn the eyelid inside out, and make the incision from the inside as described; then make two thin reeds or sticks, their length equal to the eyelid's length, and their width less than the scalpel's width.
Nibble at the ends of each of the two sides where the threads attach, then gently bring them together on the remaining eyelid and firmly pull the reeds from both sides. Leave it for several days; the stretched skin will die, turn black, and fester.
Until it falls off by itself.  If it is slow to fall off, nibble it away with pliers, then treat it until healed.  When it has healed, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes will not prick the eye.  This is the shape of the two reeds, made with the exact length and width shown here.
Chapter Twelve.
For removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If a hair or two are irritating, take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, thin silk thread, tie the ends in a very delicate knot, and make the thread approximately a span long.
Then, thread another thin thread of shorter length into a tweezers, and tie its ends if desired.  Have the patient place their head on your lap, near the light to see the work clearly.  This is precise work.  Then, use the needle and tweezers to grasp the base of the extra hair.
And carefully pull it out through the eyelid, above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards out of the tweezers and remove the hair(s) (one, two, or three, but no more) from the tweezers.
Then pull the tweezers outward, so that the hair is extracted from the eyelid along with the natural hair. If the tweezers are pulled but the hair does not come out with them, pull the tweezers downwards using the thread you have attached, to remove the tweezers from the upper opening.
Then, reinsert the hair into the tweezers and pull them out. Repeat this until the hair is pulled up through the opening, and you can see it clearly. At that point, release the tweezers and thread, remove them, and bandage the eye. Leave the bandage on for two or three days, until the hair is securely in place and the flesh grows around it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer piece of natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a while, and then lift it out.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid.
This stye, which is called "arniyyah," may be either natural or accidental.  An accidental stye results from an abscess, wound, cut, or burn.
The procedure is to cut open the abscess, separate its edges, and place a linen thread between them and tie it, until it heals.  One should not use things that dry and constrict in treating it.
If you do that, the stye will return worse. Instead, use things that soften it, such as fenugreek, a decoction of thyme root, linseed, and diachylon ointment mixed with some oils.  Apply this mixture to the thread and treat with it.
The aim of treatment is to prevent it from adhering to its original state in every way possible.  This method we have described is only part of the treatment for the stye, and it does not restore the area to its original form.
Fourteenth Chapter.
On treating the stye that occurs in the lower eyelid.
This stye, which occurs from below, is the true stye and is natural.  The accidental styes result from wounds, cuts, burns, and the like.
The method is to take a needle with a doubled thread, pierce the flesh, passing it from the inner to the outer canthus until the thread is on both sides of the flesh.  Then, pull the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, well and good.  Otherwise, take a thread and place it on the site of the cut, then turn the eyelid, cutting two incisions on the inside of the eyelid, making the ends of the incisions meet at the angle of the previous cut. So that when they meet, the shape will resemble this form.
Then, remove the flesh so that the sharp side is below the eyelid, and gather the separated pieces with two stitches using a wool thread. Then treat with the relaxing and drawing remedies mentioned previously until healed.
If the scar resulted from a cut, stitch, or cauterization, a small incision should be made, also without affecting the eyelashes, as previously described.  Then separate the lips with a thread, as mentioned before.
In short, for treating a scar, whether above or below, perform the procedure according to the scar's appearance.  For scars vary greatly in appearance, and the skilled surgeon devises the best possible method.
Until the shape returns to or nearly resembles its natural form; the nature of the illness itself indicates the appropriate procedure and instruments in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion can affect many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, preventing its free movement and hindering its natural functions.  Therefore, a slender instrument should be inserted beneath the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not as sharp as other scalpels, but rather somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not distressed during the procedure and the scalpel does not slip, hurting the eye with its sharpness.  Your incision should be like peeling away the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion heals.  Then, instill a saline solution into the eye to wash it, or drop a solution of zinjari (possibly a type of mineral) in water from a dropper onto the eye.  Then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen thread.
And place moistened egg white on top of the eye. After three days, use softened dressings until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In the cutting of the nail and deep flesh growths.
The nail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a stiff, white moisture; if touched with iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook, it will break.
No hook will hold it. Both types begin from the larger root and slowly spread to cover the eye and block light and eye movement. The procedure for cutting it involves the patient placing their head on your knee.
Then, open their eyes and raise the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the nail with a slightly bent hook and pull it upwards.  Then take a needle, thread it with a horse or ox hair and strong thread, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce the center of the nail.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the nail with the thread, pull it upwards, and remove the side of the nail next to the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it with the hair to its end.  Then cut the rest at the root of the nail with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural nail bed flesh.
So that a constant flow of tears will not result from its cutting.  The nail may be pulled out with the hook alone, or with the thread, as we have said, then the smooth side, which is depicted in this image, is removed with the scalpel.
And be careful not to touch the cornea with the scalpel, causing a rupture and damaging the pupil.  Then, after the cut, apply some powdered salt or a zinc-based preparation to the eye. Leave it for another day, then treat it appropriately until it heals.
If the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure a hook, this type of nail is often treated with medication and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this image. Carefully scrape the nail from above with it, gently.  If you see that it is crumbling and only the scraping is affecting it, apply some zinc-based preparation, or red preparation, or powdered salt to the eye from your container.  Apply pressure to the eye until the next day.
Then, continue the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot abscess appears in the eye. Leave it alone, treat the abscess until it heals, then resume scraping until it heals.  And for the protruding flesh of the eye...
If the protrusion is causing the eye significant pain, attach the protrusion with a hook and cut away some of it, but do not cut excessively to avoid tearing the eye. Then apply red or zinc-based preparation to the depth until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the protruding growths and excess flesh in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, layered flesh may grow, completely covering the pupil, almost completely covering or overflowing onto the eyelids, and possibly causing the eyelids to turn outward, resembling a rose.
The procedure is for the patient to place their head on your lap, then open their eyes, and to pick out all the red flesh with the appropriate hooks.
Or to grasp it with a scalpel or a forceps, and then to cut the flesh piece by piece until all of it is removed, safeguarding the eye so as not to injure it during the procedure.  Your cutting can be done using the scalpel that we described in the cutting of the toenail, or with small scissors. Like the ones used to pick out thorns, as their image will appear in the following chapter.
Once the cutting is complete and all the flesh is gone, fill the eye with ground salt or drop in red, or similar, corrosive medicines.  Apply a white of egg poultice externally to the eye to relieve any burning inflammation.
If any flesh remains and bleeding occurs, and you fear a burning inflammation, leave the eye alone and treat it with something that relieves the swelling, then return to the procedure until it heals.  Likewise, treat any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye, either by nature or by appearance, through surgical procedure with iron, and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that the organ is able to tolerate such an operation.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the extraction of objects from the eye.
The threads are red veins that intertwine on the eye, preventing sight and weakening the eye over time.  Therefore, you should first look to see if the eye containing the threads is strong and has no other ailment besides the threads.
Then, the extraction is done by instructing the patient to place their head on your lap.  Then, grasp those veins with one or two delicate hooks, shaped like this:
Or, two hooks may be joined together as one, like this:
Then, gently extract those veins with a fine pair of scissors.  Wipe away the blood periodically until you see the eye is free of those veins and the blood has ceased.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the scissors' edges.  Work during the middle of the day in the sun's light.
Be very diligent in your work, so as not to cut anything besides the veins.  When you are finished, apply green or red henna to the eye, so that its sharpness will consume what remains of the threads.  If you cannot extract all of it at that time, bandage the eye to relieve any pain.
Leave it for a few days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is gone. Then repeat the procedure exactly as described until it is healed. This is the method for extracting the veins without harming the eye.
Leave it [with the patient's head resting on the student's lap, and another student wiping away tears and examining the front and back of the eye].  The essential part of the procedure is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openers held by a student.
Observe carefully, and the patient's head should rest on the student's lap.  Another student should wipe away the tears and examine the front and back of the eye.  You have the choice of starting the extraction from the top or bottom of the eye.  The preferable method is to first wipe away two spots on the upper side; one at the end of the eyebrow and the other at the beginning.
Hold it between the middle and index fingers.  Then, place hooks beneath them.  Then, hold it between the ring and pinky fingers.  Gently remove your hand so that you see all the threads have risen.  Cut them, if you wish, with small scissors, or with a gentle scalpel.
Thread a needle with the instrument, and carefully separate all the threads until they rise above the conjunctiva.  Do not cut the threads too harshly, for tears and blood will mix with the substance (become liquid) on you.
Then, cut with scissors any excess thread above and below the eyebrow, so that only the thread on the cornea remains exposed. If possible, lift the threads from the cornea with the instrument; this is better, and it may be possible for some individuals.
If you cut it, the wound will be sore for several days.  It will reduce the pain and swelling.  Then repeat the procedure.
Chapter Nineteen.
In returning the quill to the nose.
Doctors call the quill a polypus if, after treating it with cauterization or a sharp burning medicine, as previously described, it does not heal.  Then the only remedy is to incise the tumor when it is ripe and remove all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, remove it with a tool of this shape:
The scraper-head, made of Indian iron, is round like a button.  It is engraved with fine grooves like a file or a scraper.  Place it on the corrupted area of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly clenching your hand until you are certain that the corruption has been removed.
And do this several times. Then bind the area with desiccated, astringent medicines.  If the area is sealed, flesh grows there, the flow of the illness stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, discharge, or any other incident.
Know then that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no other remedy except to return the polypus to the nasal cavity in this manner: expose the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine.  Once the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the iron should be triangular and its shaft tapered to a point, as you see.  Then place it on the bone itself, near the nasal opening, keeping your hand a little away from the eye.  Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
If he feels air escaping from it when he holds his hand over his nose, then bind the area with desiccated, astringent medicines, as I have taught you.  When flesh grows and the area hardens, the material that was protruding outward returns to the nose, making it easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On the repositioning of the eye protrusion.
If the entire eye has protruded and there is no impairment or loss of vision, the patient should be made comfortable, then be restrained in a cradle, then a compress should be placed on the back of the neck, without any specific condition, and gently suctioned.
Then apply a compress made of acacia, licorice root, frankincense, and henna to the eye.  The eye should then be tightly bound over the dressing with many bandages.  The bandages should be made by taking many soft strips of cloth and folding each into four layers, and making many of these.
Then place them one over another as needed for the eye, then tightly bind them with a strong cloth, and reapply the compress by suctioning, without any specific condition. Then remove the compress and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the ligament relaxes during this time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after one day and one night, as we said. If you see the eye has returned, then that's good. Otherwise, repeat the dressing, bandages, tightening, and compress until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the cutting of the conjunctiva.
If a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva, then it protrudes and appears outside the eyelids like a conjunctiva, disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as described.
Insert a needle into the base of the conjunctiva from below to above. Then, insert another needle carrying a doubled thread from the [medial] side and pass it through [the conjunctiva]. Leave the first needle in place.
Then, cut the point where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the conjunctiva around the needle and tighten it firmly.  Then, remove the needle and apply wool soaked in egg white to the eye.  Leave it until the threads fall away with the conjunctiva.  Then treat the eye with a strengthening remedy.
Until the wound heals.  The conjunctiva may be tightened from all sides with a circular bandage, so that the eye protrudes more firmly.  Then, apply a thin scalpel to the recessed part of the eye, so that the white moisture drains and the eye descends, wilting in its position.  Then, tighten it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
Concerning the treatment of the abscess.
This affliction, called the abscess, is a collection in the eye resembling descending water but without it.  The method of treatment is that the patient sits upright on a chair, and then you take their head in your hands from both sides.
Move it, so that you see the collection descend before your eyes, then stop and don't move it.  Light will be released, and the patient will see objects as they used to. If it doesn't descend, we know it is water. If the collection does not descend as described.
Otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision on the cornea, near where the abscess meets the cornea in the limbus, until the material is expelled.
If it is expelled, instill warm water into the eye, with honey added, or boiled water with licorice and honey, and then treat with other remedies until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have described the types of water in the classification and which are suitable for instillation, with detailed explanations.  Follow that for accuracy.  Then, the patient should sit before you, in a squat position, facing the light near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold the healthy eye, then lift the eyelid with your left hand if the affected eye is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the cup near the limbus, with the thickness of a date seed, in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller conjunctiva, and push the cup firmly, turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the cup has reached an empty space.
The cup should penetrate to a depth equal to the distance from the edge of the pupil to the edge of the iris (limbus).  The brass cup will be seen by the observer within the eye, due to the clarity of the cornea. Then move the cup upwards to the location of the water.
Then press it downwards repeatedly.  If the water descends at once, the patient will see what has been revealed to his sight at once, the cup in his eye, and then rest a while.  If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cup.
If it settles and does not rise, gently remove the cup, twisting your hand a little at a time.  Then dissolve some clear, fine salt in water, wash the inside of the eye with it, and apply a moistened cloth or wool to the entire outside of the eye.
Apply rose ointment and egg white and bandage the good eye.  As for us and people of our time, we apply ground cumin with egg white. If the cup does not enter the eye due to its hardness (for some people have very hard eyes), One should take the lancet called the *bayrid*, which is depicted thus:
Pierce only the adhered tissue with it; do not go deep into the puncture.  Rather, make a gentle incision for the cup, then insert the cup as previously described.  When your work is complete, prepare a secure, lying position for the patient, with him sleeping on his back in a dark room.
He should refrain from all movement and coughing.  His food should be easily digestible, and he should not move his head to either side at all.  The bandage remains in place for three days.  Then, in that dark room, remove the bandage and test his eyesight.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage for seven days.  You should not do this during treatment or after the cup has been applied, but you should avoid doing it before the water quickly rises due to excessive staring.
If a hot swelling appears, then you should remove the eye patch before the seventh day and treat it with whatever relieves the swelling. Once the swelling subsides, then release his sight and place a veil over his face, training his vision from underneath it for several days while he remains in the dark room.
He gradually leaves the room and attends to his affairs. Know that the learner cannot dispense with observing the process multiple times before performing the procedure. I have heard from some Iraqis that they make a cupping device in Iraq that sucks out water.
I have not seen anyone in our country do this, nor have I read of it in any book by the ancients.  It may be a recent innovation.  Here are pictures of various types of cupping devices, so he can familiarize himself with them.

Chapter Twenty-Four.
In the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous fleshy growths may appear in the nose, some resembling scorpions with many legs, others cancerous, hard, and dark-colored, and still others soft and not dark-colored.
For those fleshy growths that are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, their nostril opened, and a forceps applied to the growths.  Then, draw them out, and cut off any remaining part with a gentle, sharp scalpel on a single stroke.
Ensure all the flesh is removed. If any part remains uncut, gently scrape it away with a delicate instrument until nothing is left, lest bleeding or a hot swelling ensue.
If it is a malignant tumor, cauterize it immediately until the bleeding stops and all the flesh disappears.  Then, after the excision, apply vinegar and water or a drink to the nose.  If the nose opens and moisture runs down to the throat, it is well.
Know that it is healed if the moisture flows properly. If not, know that there is a fleshy growth within the spongy bone, unreachable by the cutting instrument.  In that case, a thick linen thread should be taken.
Tie many knots in it, spacing each knot a finger's breadth or less.  The patient should maneuver one end of the thread into the nostril with a probe or any suitable tool, fashioned like a button, and pull gently until it reaches the turbinates.
It will emerge at the throat.  Children often do this in school, and it is easy for anyone willing.  Then, gather the two ends of the thread—the one that came out of the mouth and the one that remained in the nose.
Then, use the knots in the thread to dissect the flesh, continuing until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots of the thread.  Then remove the thread.  The nose, after cleansing the blood, should be treated with a bandage soaked in Egyptian ointment for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all remaining flesh.  Finally, a lead tube is placed in the nostril for several days until healed. If further drying is needed, use this treatment.
This is an image of the instrument used to instill ointments and medicines into the nose.
A small, open-mouthed, ointment-filled lamp-like vessel of silver or copper is placed.
If you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the instrument's applicator flat.
It has a handle at the end, as you see, to hold it when heated with ointment or whatever liquid substances you desire.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing and enlarging over time until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance, removing it completely, and then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that acts as a cauterant.  If it has been missed until it has grown large, examine it. If it is hard, stony, dark-colored, and insensitive, ...
Do not cut it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen that cutting this tumor has brought about great misfortune for the sufferer. If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and I see that it can be completely removed by cutting, ... Then remove the affected tissue and cut it without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or the like, know that if the connection of any of these cartilages is separated, rarely will treatment be effective, except in some individuals.
Therefore, if any of this occurs to someone, examine if the wound is fresh and bloody.  If so, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and then treat it until it heals.  If the separation has occurred and each side of the separation is intact, then carefully peel back each side of the separation to the extent of its visible skin until bleeding results.
Then bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle powdered  cinnamon and frankincense on them.  Apply a plaster of palm tree ointment or another adhesive ointment on top of that. Leave it tight for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medication and leave it until the stitches come out on their own.  Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection either with needles, as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread, as I explained there.
The Twenty-seventh Chapter.
On removing knots that appear in the lips.
Small, hard tumors, resembling some carbuncles and others smaller or larger, may appear inside the lips of many people.  It is necessary to turn back the lip and cut open each knot, and remove it with forceps.
Cut it from every side, then fill the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt.  Treat the areas with what contracts until the wounds heal.
The Twenty-eighth Chapter.
On cutting excess flesh in the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *abolus*.  It should be lifted with forceps or held with a scalpel and cut at its base, allowing the blood to drain, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent, dried powders.  If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does; cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
The Twenty-ninth Chapter.
On scraping the teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  They may turn black, yellow, or green, causing decay to spread to the gums and disfigure the teeth.
The patient should be seated before you, head resting on your lap.  Clean the tooth or teeth showing scales and sand-like material until nothing remains.  Do the same for black, green, or yellow discoloration, and so on, until clean.
If the first scraping removes all the material, then it is done. Otherwise, repeat the scraping another day, and a second, and a third, until you achieve your goal.  Know that teeth require a variety of scraping tools, many shapes, according to what is needed for your task.
Because the scraper used for cleaning the inside of a tooth is different from the scraper for the outside, and the scraper for between the teeth has a different shape.  These are various types of scrapers that should all be prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should treat the tooth's pain with every possible method, and avoid extracting it if possible.  For there is no remedy after extraction, as it is a precious substance, even if extraction becomes necessary.
If the patient is determined to have the tooth extracted, one should be certain that the tooth is properly located and the pain is well-defined. Often, the patient is deceived by the pain, believing it is in the healthy tooth and extracting it, then the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this happen repeatedly with the actions of the barbers.
If you are certain of the precise location of the painful tooth, then you should use a forceful scalpel to cut around the tooth, separating the gum from all sides, then move it gently with your fingers or fine forceps, little by little, until it loosens.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps, while the patient's head is securely held between your knees.  Pull the tooth straight to avoid fracturing it.  If it does not come out, take one of those instruments and insert it beneath it from all sides gently, moving it as you did before.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, you should fill the hole with a cloth and seal it firmly with a thin, smooth instrument so that it does not break apart when you pull on it with forceps.  You should carefully cut around the gum from all sides.
Use caution to avoid breaking the tooth, for any remaining portion will become a greater affliction for the patient than the initial pain.  Do not do as the ignorant barbers do in their haste and attempts to extract it without using the methods we have described.
Often they inflict great calamities on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots, either all or some of them. Or, they may pull out parts of the jawbone, as we have seen repeatedly.  After extraction, the patient should rinse with a drink or vinegar and salt.  If bleeding occurs at the site, which often happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if alum is ineffective, use forceps.  The delicate forceps used to first move the tooth should have long tips and short handles, thick enough not to bend when gripping the tooth.  Here is a picture of the larger forceps.
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles, so that when grasped, they do not yield or bend; short in the tips.  They should be made of Indian iron or tempered steel, with firmly joined tips, and teeth on the tip that fit together, to grip tightly.
The tips can also be shaped like a file, making them very strong and precise.

Short, slightly thick at the tip, and not tapered so as not to break. If the root comes out with this, then use these other instruments, whose illustration is this. The first is triangular, with some thickness.
