Chapter Thirty-two.
In the spreading of the teeth that grow over others.
Teeth that grow out of their natural course disfigure the appearance, particularly in women and slaves.  If a tooth has grown behind another tooth, one should observe.
If it cannot be spread or straightened, extract it.  If it is stuck to another tooth, cut it off with this instrument, whose image is this.
It resembles a small beak, made of very sharp Indian steel. Cut it in several days due to the tooth's hardness and so as not to loosen other teeth. If it is protruding and firmly in place, cool it with an Indian file, whose image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with very delicate engravings, like the file used to make needles.  Cool the tooth gradually over many days, gently, to avoid dislodging it and causing it to fall out. Then smooth it afterward and scrape it with some scrapers.
If a tooth has broken off, causing the tongue pain when speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the break disappears, it becomes smooth, and it no longer hurts the tongue or disrupts speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If old teeth become loose and shift due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat anything for fear of losing them, and binding medicines have not helped.
The remedy is to tie them with gold or silver thread. Gold is preferable because silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, appropriate for the space between the teeth.
The method of tying is to take the thread, thread its bend between the sound teeth, and then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound tooth on the other side.
Then repeat the weaving from the side you began on, gently and skillfully tightening your grip so that nothing moves.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth so it does not come loose.  Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, bring them together, twist them with a knife, and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
To prevent injury to the tongue.  Then leave it tied like this for as long as it remains. If it comes undone or breaks, tie it again with another thread.  He will enjoy it like this for his whole life. This is the image of the teeth and the method of tying in two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single or two teeth may be returned to their place after falling out, and fastened as we have described, and remain.  A skilled artisan does this.  He may carve bone from an ox, shaping it like a tooth, and place it in the position from which the tooth has gone. And ties it as we said, so it remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may be either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a healed wound.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, holding their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, cut the nerve ligament transversely so the tongue is released from its restraint. If it shows some stiffness and complexity, if it's from a healed wound, then insert a small hook and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Beware that the cut does not penetrate deeply into the flesh and sever an artery, causing bleeding.  Then, rinse the area after the cut with rose water or vinegar and cold water.  Place a linen wick under the tongue for the patient to hold each night.
To prevent re-adhesion. If bleeding occurs, apply powdered zea to the area. If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area with a suitable, lens-shaped cautery. Then treat it with further remedies until healed.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On removing a toad that has developed under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small toad may develop under the tongue, hindering the tongue's normal function, and possibly enlarging to fill the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the swelling.
If it appears dark-colored or black and hard, causing no sensation to the patient, do not attempt to treat it, for it is cancer. If it is whitish with moisture, then use a forceps to open it, and dissect it gently with a sharp scalpel, removing it from all sides.
If bleeding occurs during the procedure, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then resume the procedure until the swelling is completely removed.  Then, the patient should rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with whatever further treatment is appropriate until healed.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any tumors that grow in the throat may present glands inside the throat resembling those that appear outside, called tonsils. If these are treated as described in the division but do not heal.
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use the iron on it.  If it is red and thick, do not use iron either, for fear of bloodletting. Instead, let it mature, either by applying a poultice or until it bursts on its own.
If it is white, round, and its origin thin, this is what should be cut. Before doing so, observe if the hot swelling has subsided completely or if some of the swelling has decreased.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head in your lap, mouth open.  Take an assistant, and have them press the tongue downwards with an instrument, which looks like this:
Made of silver or copper, it is as thin as a knife. When the tongue is pressed down and you can clearly see the tumor, take a hook, and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes along with it.
Then, cut it with an instrument, which resembles scissors, but its ends are curved, with each of their ends sharply pointed.  It is made from Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is unavailable, then cut it with a scalpel, which looks like this:
Sharp on one side, blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, have the patient gargle with water in which pomegranate rind or mugwort leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals.  Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils, and these are cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a small, somewhat insensitive swelling.  It had nearly blocked her throat, and she was breathing through a narrow passageway. It had prevented her from eating and drinking.
She had been close to death if she had lived another day or two. Two branches had grown from the tumor and protruded from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserting a hook into one and pulling it out, and a piece suitable for removal was extracted.
Then I cut it where it emerged from the nostril.  I did the same with the other nostril.  Then, I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut off a piece of it.  Only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened up, and she immediately drank water and then ate. We continued to cut away from that tumor repeatedly over a long period, and it kept regrowing after each removal.  This went on for a long time, affecting both of us.
I devised a method of cauterizing the tumor within her throat, which stopped its growth. Then I left the area, and I do not know what happened to her after I departed.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
In the excision of a tumor of the larynx.
If a tumor, known as a "bunch" [or "cluster" or "grape"], descends to the larynx and swells, it is called a "column" if it is white and oblong, and a "bunch" if it is thick at the bottom and rounded.  If treated as described in the division, and the treatment does not succeed, ...
If the inflammatory tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be excised.  However, if the tumor is a compact, rounded mass without length and is reddish, or dark-colored, or black, or without sensation, it should not be excised.
For in this condition there is risk to the patient. Therefore, if you see it with the characteristics I mentioned – its whiteness and length – the patient should sit facing the sun, and the tongue should be pressed down with the instrument described earlier, and then the snare should be inserted into the bunch.
Pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments that I described for the removal of tonsils. Only the portion exceeding the natural size should be excised; for if more is removed, it will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after excision, use the treatment described for the removal of the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is reluctant to have it excised, you should use cauterization without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicament, placing the patient's head on your lap.
Then, press down the tongue with the instrument we mentioned, take the sharp water described in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with quicklime that is not extinguished, form a paste that is neither thick nor thin, fill the concavity of this instrument, which has this form:
The end of the instrument where you place the medicament should have a concavity like the bowl of a spoon. Place the instrument with the medicament on the uvula itself, with the patient lying on their side, so that saliva flows from their mouth from inside their throat, so nothing goes down to their throat and causes harm.
Then, hold the instrument with the medicament and press it against the uvula for about half an hour, until you see it blackened and the burning from the medicament has subsided.  If you wish, you can take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicament.
Insert the spoon with the cotton into the tube from above so that the cotton adheres to the uvula.  Do this repeatedly until you have achieved the desired cauterization of the uvula.  Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to re-apply the medicament, do so.
After the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in clarified butter, removing any remaining medicament. Then, have them rinse their mouth with cold water, and treat externally with lotions and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and avoids fear.
The uvula can also be treated with something milder than cauterization or cutting.  Treatment with incense is done in this way:  Take frankincense, saffron, sage, fumitory, wormwood, chamomile, and other herbs, gather them all or some of them in a pot, and soak them in vinegar.
Bring it to a boil while the pot is covered with clay of wisdom.  A hole is made in the center of the cover through which the hollow instrument is fitted, made of silver or copper.
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube.  Cauterize the uvula repeatedly until it withers.  Avoid using this treatment when the swelling first occurs.
For it often increases the swelling.  Instead, it should be done when the hot swelling subsides. If you don't have this instrument, take a reed and fit an egg shell to one end, so that the patient's mouth is not burned.
Because the egg shell prevents the heat of the vapor from burning the mouth.  This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other foreign objects from the throat.
Thorns, fish bones, or other foreign objects often lodge in the throat.  It is necessary to remove any that are visible, after depressing the tongue with the instrument in the light.
To reveal what is in the throat. If something is hidden and not visible in the throat, the patient must vomit before his food is digested in his stomach.  Perhaps the object lodged will come out with the vomit, or the patient will swallow a piece of cloth or a stalk of lettuce.
Or he swallows a piece of dry bread. Or, a piece of soft sea sponge is taken, tied to a string, and swallowed. When it reaches the position of the thorn, the string is pulled quickly, repeating this several times.
Often, the thorn or bone adheres to it and comes out. If it does not come out by these methods, use a lead instrument in this manner:
It should be slightly thicker than a lead rod.  At its tip is a bend. The patient, raising their head, gently inserts it into their throat, being careful not to touch their larynx to avoid coughing, and using it to push out the bone or thorn, or the physician inserts it with his hand.
Having the patient insert it is better, as they know the location of the lodged object. They push downwards or pull upwards with the instrument, doing so as best they can until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
Removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If, having treated the leech as described in the section on medicinal treatment, it proves ineffective, then examine the patient's throat in the light of the sun after pressing their tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If you cannot do this, take a hollow tube, insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, and then insert a heated, protected iron into the tube.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from water all day.  Then, they should take a jar filled with cold water, open their mouth over it, rinse their mouth with it, but not swallow a drop.  The patient should move the water back and forth with their hand from time to time.
If the leech feels the water, it will fall off. If it does not come out with the methods described, fumigate the throat with urine and hellebore using the instrument described for inhaling fumigation.  Do this several times, and it will fall off.
The procedure for fumigation is to take a vessel containing embers heated by fire. The vessel is covered with a lid that has a hole in the middle. The instrument's tip is placed in this hole.  Then, the fumigant is placed inside, and the patient places their mouth over the end of the tube, closing their mouth so that the fumes enter their throat.
If the leech falls off at this point, good.  If it does not, the fumigation is repeated multiple times.  The patient endures thirst and eats salty foods and garlic, but does not drink water.  The leech must come out with this treatment.  This is an image of the instrument used to pull the leech from the throat once it is spotted.
It resembles tongs, as you see, except that it has this curve that enters the throat.  Its tips resemble a bird's beak, rough like a file; if it grasps something, it does not let go.
Another instrument is used to press the tongue.  It is a wide ring of iron, sized to fit the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth upright on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will.  The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument that opens the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
It deals with the lancing and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many diverse types, as will be described one by one in this book.  They differ in lancing and incision in two ways: one is the nature of the tumor itself and the humors it contains.
And the second type is by the locations in the body where they occur, for the tumor arising in the head is different from the tumor arising in the buttocks, and a tumor arising in fleshy tissue is different from one in a joint. Each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be lanced until the pus is fully matured.  Others should be lanced while still fresh and not fully matured, like tumors near the joints.
Because if a tumor near a joint grows to the point of causing gangrene around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing dysfunction of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay lancing it until it ripens, you harm that vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, lance it while fresh.  It is necessary to lance a fresh, incompletely ripened tumor near the buttocks to prevent gangrene from spreading to the inside of the buttocks and forming a fistula.
And it becomes incurable to some extent.  You should know when to lance fully ripened tumors, which is when the tumor pain subsides, the fever is gone, the redness and pulsation decrease, the head of the tumor is softened, and other symptoms improve.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, to allow the discharge to flow downward more easily, or at the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  If the tumors are on the arms or legs, the incision should follow the length of the limb.
The incision should be made over the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in general, over all flat, non-flexing areas.  For flexing areas, the incision should follow the contour of the area.  If the tumor is in a fleshy area, it is better to leave it unlanced.
Until its ripening is complete, as we said. If you lance it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, causing much harm and dirtiness, and perhaps its edges and depths will harden.  Some tumors may be lanced across the body, if necessary, or according to the needs of the limb.
Small tumors should be lanced with a single incision, while large tumors should be lanced with a wide incision or many incisions, depending on the size of the tumor.  Some tumors may require the skin to be incised and cut if it has become like a torn fabric.
The tumor has, to some extent, died, similar to what occurs in many abscesses and hidden tumors.  Some are incised with a three-sided incision, while others are incised in the shape of a rue leaf, like the tumor of the groin.
Some tumors require a circular or crescent-shaped incision, or similar incisions.  Tumors without a head, such as smooth, flat tumors, should only be lanced with a simple incision.  If the tumor is large, has accumulated for a long time, and has been lanced, one should not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Rather, drain some of it, then compress the tumor for another day, then drain some more pus.  Do this repeatedly, gradually, until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, a young child, or an elderly person, the animal spirit often weakens with the sudden draining of pus.  The patient might die, and you would not be aware. Therefore, be extremely careful in this matter.
After lancing these tumors, you should clean the wound and examine it.  If the tumor wound is small or the incision is simple, use a bandage of flax or old cotton.  If the tumor is large and the incisions are numerous,
You should insert a thread into each incision until some of them connect. If the tumor has damaged or broken the skin, you should fill it with old cotton or flax lint, without moisture, and compress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.  If bleeding occurs while you are working, use cold water and vinegar, after having soaked a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the methods and treatments and cauterization techniques described in many places in this book and in the classification.  If it is winter and the tumor site has many nerves, you should soak the dressings with warm drink and warm oil.
And place it on the affected area.  If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply the dressings soaked in water and oil, or in cool drink and oil, until the third day, as we have said.  Then you should loosen the tumor and clean it.
And use similar treatments until it heals.  This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  As for detailed methods, each tumor is described with its specific treatment, summarized and categorized.
Chapter Forty-one.
Concerning the treatment of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, which are a type of swelling enclosed in membranes.  They have characteristics similar to a hen's egg sac, and their types are numerous.  Some are fatty, and others contain moisture resembling mud.
Some contain moisture like broth and soup, and the like.  Some are hard and stony.  All of them are without danger to incise and remove, unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image will follow, called the "midas," so that you know what they contain. If it contains moisture, incise it longitudinally with a simple incision, like this:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~. If the moisture has drained, dissect the sac that contained the moisture and cut it entirely; do not leave any part of it, for it often recurs if any remains.
Then, dip a cotton swab in the astringent ointment, if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, for it will consume any remaining sac. Then, reapply the cotton swab with the astringent a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains.  Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty tissue, incise it like this:
Make a crosswise incision, and insert forceps into the edges of the wound. Dissect it from all sides and exert yourself to remove the peritoneum that contains it.  If an artery obstructs you, do as described previously. Similarly, if the swelling is hardened, do this from the incision.
The treatment is as described, and the incision on the hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture.  Some of these swellings in the head, in some people, have absolutely no moisture.  I incised a swelling in...
the head of an elderly woman, and found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone. It could not be broken, even if someone threw it, it would only chip.  The other swellings that occur on the head, such as those that appear on the heads of children, were different.
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision all around.  Make a flap from the bottom so that the matter flows downwards.  Then treat it with appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning the incision on swine that frequently appear on the neck.  These swellings often appear on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groin. They may appear on other parts of the body.  One may appear on the neck, or many. Some develop from others.
Each cyst has its own membrane, like the swellings and tumors of the head we have described. There are many types of these cysts, some are hard, some contain moisture, and some are malignant and unresponsive to treatment.
Of those that I saw, the ones that felt good and appeared close to the skin color, moving in all directions, were not adhered to any neck nerve, vein or artery, and were not embedded deep. It is necessary to make a simple incision from top to bottom of the body, along this line, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
Peel it from all sides and spread the edges of the skin with a hook or two, or many if needed, as we described for tumors of the head.  Remove it little by little, taking care not to cut a nerve or artery. The scalpel should not be excessively sharp.
So that your hand does not cut too much or the patient is disturbed, cutting what is not needed. If you cut an artery or vein and this hinders your work, place powdered alum or some of the styptic agents that stop bleeding in the wound.
Tighten the wound and leave it until the inflammation subsides, the wound relaxes, and it is about to fester. Then the bleeding will stop, so return to your work until it is finished. Then, with your index finger, check if there are any remaining small ones. If there are, cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the cyst, then it is not necessary to cut the cyst from its base, but rather to tie it with a strong double thread and leave it until it falls out on its own without harm. Then fill the wound with used cotton, soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it.
If you have cut the entire cyst, you should bring the edges of the wound together and stitch it immediately, after ensuring that there is absolutely no remaining piece. If you see that a piece of the cyst remains due to its size,
Then you should aim to cut at the base and root, and use the stitching and what we have mentioned. For those cysts containing moisture, apply a simple dressing where the ripening spot appears.  Place the dressing on the lower part, as we said.
Then, after the dressing, use application of Egyptian ointment or similar to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that promote healing until it is healed.
Chapter Forty-three.
On the incision of the throat for a tumor occurring inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision of the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is in agreement with their words.  However, those with quinsy should avoid incisions of the throat, as they will not benefit from it.
For all tumors and diseases of the lung to be considered sick, and those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no problem in the trachea, then the incision of the throat must be used to escape the harm of suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we should incise the throat below three circles of the trachea, or four small transverse incisions between two circles, to the same extent as an incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location is appropriate for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is cowardly, then he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook, then cut the skin until, when he reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the peritoneum that we described, and ascertain the incision of the trachea from the phlegm that comes out of it along with what is punctured, and from the cessation of sound. He should leave the wound open for a time. When the period of fear of suffocation is over, he should bring together the edges of the wound from the skin.
And its incision is alone, without the cartilage. Then the medicines that promote flesh growth are used until it heals. Said the author of this book: The interpretation of this statement that we have related is that if they see the patient's throat blocked by one of these tumors that we mentioned.
And when the patient was close to death and despaired of life, they went to cut the throat so that the patient might breathe through the wound site and be saved from death.  But they were instructed to leave the wound open until the course of the disease was completed, and the course is typically three days or similar.
At that point, they instructed them to stitch the wound and treat it until it healed.  What I witnessed myself was that a servant took a knife and stabbed her throat, cutting part of her lung's trachea.  I was called to treat her. I found her weakening like a slaughtered animal, so I examined the wound.
I found little blood coming from the wound, and thus I was certain that she had not severed a vein or artery.  Air was escaping from the wound.  I quickly stitched and treated the wound until it healed. The servant suffered nothing, except for a hoarse voice.
And after a few days, she returned to her best condition.  Therefore, we say that cutting the throat poses no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision of the tumor appearing on the larynx from the outside, called the laryngeal tumor.
This tumor, called a laryngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body, common in women, and of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental; the natural one has no remedy, while the accidental one is of two kinds: one resembling fatty tumors, and the other resembling a tumor arising from arterial occlusion; cutting it is dangerous. Therefore, one should not use the knife at all, unless it is small; if you probe and examine it with a probe and find it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any blood vessels, cut it as you would cut a tumor. And remove it with the sac it is contained in, if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove it completely, and then treat the area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On cutting various tumors.
Tumors are of many types, and all their types were mentioned in the classification. Here, it is necessary to explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as this is problematic. I say that an abscess is accompanied by fever, heat, and terrifying pains until the boiling of the abscess subsides. And when the pus is complete, the fever and severity subside.  A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain.  It is contained within a sac-like covering, specific to it, and is the same color as the surrounding body.  Its initial appearance is like a boil, and it grows to be like a fig and larger or smaller.
Tumors are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture.  The colors of moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, when treating a tumor, one should first probe and examine it using the instrument called the 'medas', as its image will appear in the following chapter.
Assess the size of the tumor, then withdraw the probe and observe what comes out afterward. If a flowing moisture of any color comes out, make a small incision, as described in other tumors. If no moisture comes out after probing, know that it is fatty.
Incise it with a sharp incision in this manner, as I have instructed you, and suspend it with hooks. Carefully dissect the skin from every side. If possible, remove the sac containing the tumor intact.
If the sac ruptures during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, which often happens, remove it in pieces, until nothing remains.  If any part remains, however small, the tumor will likely return.
If you are unsuccessful and a small portion remains, cauterize the wound thoroughly with sharp cautery irons.  Afterward, compress the wound and apply something to relieve the hot tumor. Treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals. If the tumor is large, suture the edges and treat it with a healing agent.
If a bleeding vein, whether pulsating or not, obstructs you, immediately apply powdered alum to the affected area. Leave it compressed for two or three days until the wound suppurates and the blood flow subsides. Then proceed to remove any remaining part of the tumor.

Chapter Forty-seven.
In the treatment of the male breast.
The breast of some people, resembling that of women, may swell at the age of puberty, becoming swollen and unsightly. Whoever dislikes this should have the breast incised with a crescent-shaped incision, like this.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated ointment, then bring the edges of the wound together with stitches, and treat it until it heals.  If the breast sags and loosens from its bone, as happens with women.
Then an incision should be made on its upper sides, with two incisions resembling a crescent shape, each connecting to the other at their ends, so that the larger line surrounds the smaller one in this way. From line ~b~ to line ~c~, then the skin between the incisions is peeled away, the fat removed, and the aforementioned sutures and cauterization, and any necessary treatment, are used until it heals.
If the incision does not encompass all that should be cut because of the patient's anxiety or bleeding, then the wound should be packed with cotton dipped in the corrosive ointment and left until it consumes the remaining fat, and then treated until healed.
Chapter Forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors appearing under the armpit.
These tumors appearing under the armpit are of the pig-like, hard, sac-containing type; some contain moisture.  Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, like this.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~.  If all the moisture in the tumor has been drained, pack it with used cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat with the appropriate ointment until healed. If the tumor is of the pig-like lump type, incise it as we described in the incision for pig-like tumors. If the wound becomes suppurative, apply cauterization as previously mentioned.
Chapter Forty-nine.
Incising the tumor that appears near the artery or vein, called an aneurism.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it is adhered, a tumor often appears.  Similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one can know if the swelling and puffiness are from an artery or a vein are:  If it's from an artery, the swelling is oblong, situated deep within the body, and when you press it with your finger, you feel a crackling sensation.
And that which is from the vein is a round swelling on the surface of the body.  Incising these tumors is dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body, and if they are very large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
Also, those in the extremities or head should be avoided.  For those that are swellings of the artery's mouth, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with forceps, then dissect and free the artery from its sheaths.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I instructed you in tying the arteries of the temples, then pierce with a lancet the area between the ligaments until all the blood therein is expelled.
The swelling will resolve, and then use the treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall away, then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is from a vein rupture, you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as possible, then insert a needle below the area you grasped, and pass it through, with a double thread, until it comes out from the other side. Then tie the swelling firmly, as I described to you in tying the veins on both sides with the ends of the thread.  If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire swelling at the point where the first needle crosses.
Tie your threads in four places, then slit the swelling in the middle until whatever is inside is expelled. Cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then place a dressing on it that has been soaked in a potion and oil. Then use the treatment that involves twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling caused by nerve twisting.
Just as swelling occurs in the artery and vein, swelling also occurs in the nerve if a blow is inflicted or if excessive fatigue or the like happens. This is more common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every place where joints move, it is a hard swelling similar in color to the rest of the body.  Most often it is painless, but if pressed forcefully, the patient feels a sensation like numbness.  The swelling is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it is beneath the skin and moves in every direction, not forward or backward.  If it is in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, as it may cause a wound.  What is in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, grasp it with a dissector and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook, and peel it away, then remove it. Close the wound with stitches and treat it until healed.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On the excision of warts appearing on the abdomen.
Warts, called fungous because of their resemblance to mushrooms, frequently appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people.  Their base is thin, and their head is thick. Sometimes their lips have fused, and they vary in size, some being very large.
I observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling mushrooms in appearance.  Their thin, whitish bases had fused, and their lips were cracked.  Moisture constantly dripped from them.  I cut them away.  One weighed approximately eighteen ounces, and the other about six ounces.
When cutting them, observe.  If the growth is moist, the wart is white, moist, and thin-based, then cut it with a broad scalpel.  Have a cautery iron heated nearby, as significant bleeding frequently occurs during excision.
If profuse bleeding occurs, cauterize it immediately.  If the growth is tender and recoils from being cut with a tool, take a tightly wound lead thread and bind it to the wart of this description.  Leave it for two days, then gradually tighten the lead.
As the lead tightens on the wart, it will eventually detach and fall off on its own, without further intervention.  If the wart has a thick base, cutting it presents some danger and uncertainty, especially when located on the abdomen.  However, it may be possible to remove half or part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals.  Beware of cutting a wart that is dark-colored, insensitive, and discolored, for it is a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
In treating umbilical protrusions.
Umbilical protrusions arise from various causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen, causing the bowel or omentum to protrude, as seen in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from accumulated gas.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the omentum, the tumor will have a color similar to the body, be soft without pain, and appear with various shapes. If it is due to the bowel protruding, its appearance will differ more significantly from the description given.  If you press it with your fingers, it disappears then returns, and there may be rumbling sounds; it will enlarge considerably during a bath or heavy exertion. If caused by moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed by hand and does not increase or decrease in size, it is likely due to blood.  In this case, the tumor will show a dark discoloration. If it is due to a growing piece of flesh, the tumor will be firm, solid, and of consistent size.
If it is due to gas, its touch will be soft. The procedure in such cases is to observe carefully. If the umbilical protrusion is caused by arterial or venous blood, or gas, one should refrain from treatment, as there is danger and deception involved, as I have informed you in the section where I discussed tumors arising from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is due to the intestines or omentum, instruct the patient to hold themselves still and stand upright, fully extended.  Then, make markings around the entire navel. Then, instruct them to lie on their back before you.  Cut around the navel using a wide scalpel, along the marked area.  Then, pull the center of the tumor upward with a large forceps. Finally, tie the incision with strong thread or silk thread securely.
The knot of the bandage should be a figure-of-eight.  Then, open the center of the tightened tumor above the bandage, and insert your index finger.  If you find the intestines have been grasped by the bandage, loosen the figure-of-eight knot and push the intestines back into the abdomen. If you find omentum, stretch it using forceps.
And cut off the excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, tie it off firmly.  Return to your task, and take two needles.  Thread strong threads through them.  Insert the two needles into the incision you made around the tumor, piercing it firmly.  Then, tighten the tumor at four points on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the tightened flesh decays and falls off on its own, or cut it if it becomes thin and decayed.  Then, treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh that has grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, you should cauterize the tumor as we have said, and remove the blood or moisture you find there.  Then treat it with whatever will heal the area.
The fifty-third chapter.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medicines, and cautioned against treating it with iron to avoid ulceration.  We have also mentioned cancer originating in the uterus and cautioned against its treatment.
The ancients stated that when the cancer is in a location where it can be completely excised, like cancer in the breast or the thigh, and similar excisable organs, especially if it is small and early, one should remove it completely.  However, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If the cancer is, as we said, widespread, one should proceed to draw out the black fluid repeatedly, and then cut it out if it is in the veins with clear fullness.
Then, apply a bandage to hold the cancer firmly, and then insert the appropriate hooks into the cancer.  Remove it from every direction along with the skin, thoroughly, until no part of its roots remains.  Allow the blood to flow, do not stop it quickly.
Instead, squeeze the affected areas and extract all the thick blood with your hands or with any suitable tools.  If during the procedure a significant bleed occurs from a severed artery or vein, cauterize the vessel until the bleeding stops, then treat it with further methods until healed.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the treatment of ulcers.
We have informed you about the different types of dropsy, how water accumulates, the signs of each type, and its treatment with medication.  Only the 'thick' type of dropsy is treated with iron.  The 'thin' or 'fleshy' types should not be treated with iron at all.

If you see that the patient is strong, has no illness other than the ulcer, and is neither a child nor an old person, then the procedure is to have the patient stand before you. and a servant behind him to press his abdomen with his hands, pushing the water downwards towards the pubic area.  Then take a pointed scalpel, like this:
It should be narrow at both ends, long, and pointed, like a scalpel, but with a slight curve to prevent it from going too far into the intestines and harming them. Then, examine if the ulcer originated from the intestines.

A small needle-like tip may be placed on the end, like the nib of a pen, in this form.
At its upper end is a ring.  When the instrument reaches the fluid, it will drain onto the instrument, draining a moderate amount of fluid at a time.  If you drain more than is necessary at once, the patient might die from the dissolution of their animal spirit.
Or he may experience a near-fatal faint. But drain it according to his strength, guided by the patient's pulse and complexion.  Then remove the instrument and retain the fluid, as it will collect at once.
Because of the skin that holds the perforation in the peritoneum, which you were instructed to apply to in that manner, and then repeat the procedure another day, if you find the patient able to tolerate it.
Also remove a small amount of fluid, doing this for several days while staying vigilant and avoiding mistakes, so that only a little fluid remains.  If you fear for the patient and leave too much fluid, treat him by burying him in hot sand.
Excessive sweating in the bath and sun, enduring thirst, treating with dried medications until he recovers, and cauterizing the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the fluid as described previously.

Chapter Fifty-seven.
In the purification of boys and the treatment of their errors.
Circumcision is nothing more than separating a connection, like other surgical procedures, but since it is something we do intentionally, and is used particularly in boys, we must establish the best course of action.
The easiest path to safety, I say, is that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have adopted.
By experience, for I have found the majority of craftsmen and barbers using purification with a knife and scissors, and using a bandage with thread, and cutting with a nail.  I have tried all these methods.
So I have found nothing better than purification with scissors and bandaging with thread, because purification with a knife often causes the skin to tear, since the foreskin has two layers; it might cut the upper layer and leave the lower layer.
He may be forced to make another cut, and further pain; purification with a cupping tool does not provide protection against cutting the end of the urethra because it might enter its opening, and purification with a nail might cause the skin to tear and spoil your work, or the boy's skin might naturally be too short.
Often, they are born in such a way that they do not need purification; and I have seen this.  As for purification with scissors and bandaging with thread, experience has shown me its superiority because scissors have a proportionate cut, as the blade above is the same as the blade below.
When you squeeze your hand with the blades in proportion, the cut is uniform and in one time.  This makes the thread's grip similar to a wall against the urethra's skin on all sides; there will be no mistake.  First, you must calm the boy.

Do not have him sit, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot.  Do not let the boy's eyes see them, nor any of the instruments. Then, insert your hand into his urethra, inflate the skin, and lift it upwards until the head of the urethra emerges.
Then, clean it of any accumulated dirt.  Then tie the marked area with a doubled thread. Then, tie a second bandage slightly below that. Then allow the blood to flow a little; this is better and lessens swelling of the urethra. Chapter Fifty-eight.
On treating urine retention in the bladder.
Obstructed urine in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or a growing mass, and the like. If you treat it with the various treatments described in the classification, this will help.
If the urine does not flow and you suspect the obstruction is due to a stone damaging the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then a person should sit on their back, stretching their abdomen toward the back, and the patient should hold on as much as possible.
This will force the neck of the bladder to push the stone backward, allowing the urine to flow. If the urine does not flow with this method and the patient's condition worsens, then the removal of the obstruction should be attempted with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is shown here.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollow like a bird's feather tube, about one and a half spans long, with a gentle funnel at its head.  The method for drawing the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie a piece of wool or cotton securely to one end.
The thread end is inserted into the lower part of the catheter, and any excess wool is trimmed with pliers so that it fits snugly inside the tube, like a button.  Then, the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
The bladder and urethra are softened with moist ointments or warm oil and water.  The catheter is then gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra.  Then, the urethra is pulled upward towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed inside.
Once the urethra is pulled, the catheter is pushed inward until it nears the anus.  The penis is then tilted downward, while the catheter is inside, and pushed further until it reaches the bladder, where the patient will feel it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this way because the urinary passage has curves.  Then, the thread with the wool is pulled gently, so the urine follows the wool.  It is then removed, and the urine is drained, and the catheter is withdrawn.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

The hollow space where the nozzle is will be just enough to hold it without any extra space.  Thus, when you pull it, any moisture will be pulled, and if you push it, it will be pushed far away, as the oil-thrower used in naval warfare does.

Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.
We have previously discussed the types of stones, their treatment with medications, and the differences between kidney stones and bladder stones, along with the signs of each.  We have also mentioned the stones that require manual removal and incision.
These are the stones originating in the bladder, specifically those that form in the urethra. I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that bladder stones are most often found in young boys.  A sign of this is that the urine passes from the bladder, thin like water.
Sand appears in the urine, and he rubs and fiddles with his penis.  Often the penis hangs down, and the anus prolapses in many of them.  Young boys recover easily from this up to the age of fourteen, while it is harder in the elderly; young men lie in between these two extremes.
Those with larger stones have easier treatment, and the opposite is true for smaller ones.  Thus, regarding treatment, first the patient should be given an enema to remove all the waste from his bowels, as this can hinder the examination for stones.
Then the patient is taken by the legs and shaken downward to bring the stone to the neck of the bladder, or he is made to jump repeatedly from a high place.  Then, he is seated upright before you, with his hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder tilts downwards, and then he is examined.
Feel for the stone from the outside.  If you feel a stone in the space, immediately begin to cut into it.  If it does not come into contact with your touch at all, then you should smear the index finger (if the patient is a boy) or the middle finger with grease from your left hand.
If it is a fully grown boy, insert it into his anus and search for the stone. Once it is under your finger, move it little by little toward the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it out toward the location you intend to cut.
And order a servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand, and order another servant to stretch his right hand towards the upper part of the vulva and, with his other hand, stretch the skin below the vulva toward the location of the incision. Then take the lancet, which has this shape:
Incise between the anus and the vulva, not in the middle, but to the side of the left groin. The incision should be on the same spot as the stone, and your finger should be in the anus, pushing it outward obliquely, so that the incision is wide from the outside and narrow from the inside, to the extent that the stone can be removed from it.
Do not make it too large; perhaps the finger in the anus will push the stone out during the incision without difficulty.  Know that some stones may have angles and edges, making their removal difficult.
Some are smooth, like acorns, and rounded, making removal easier; those with angles and edges should have the incision enlarged a little.  If it still does not come out, then a clever method should be employed, such as grasping it with a firm forceps, its tip like a file.
To grip the stone so it does not slip; or, introduce a delicate, curved instrument underneath it. If that fails, widen the incision a little. If you encounter significant bleeding, stop it with a cautery.
If there is more than one stone, first push the largest into the bladder opening, then incise on it.  Then push out the smaller one(s).  Do likewise if there are more than two. If the stones are very large, it is foolish to make a large incision on them, because the patient will suffer one of two things.
Either he will die, or he will suffer constant dribbling of urine, as the area will never heal properly.  However, try pushing it out; or, devise a method to break it with forceps until it is removed completely.
Once your work is finished, treat the wound with kandar, sabr, sheyan, and a tight bandage.  Place a soaked cloth in oil and drink or rose oil and cold water over it to relieve the inflammation.  The patient should then lie on their back.  Do not remove the bandage until the third day.
Once the swelling subsides, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with the palm ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If a further inflammatory swelling, ulceration, or similar problem develops in the wound, such as clotting of blood within the bladder, which prevents urination, and this is evident by blood in the urine.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove the blood, for if it remains, it will lead to bladder corruption and festering. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of issue with a corresponding remedy until it heals.
During all treatments, the thighs should be bound together to secure the medications applied to the area.  If the stone is small and located in the urethra, causing inflammation and preventing urination, treat it with the methods I've described before it reaches a rupture.
I have often avoided incision with this treatment.  I have tested using a lancet made of hardened steel, with a shape like this:
The tip of the lancet is sharp, pointed, and fixed to a stick.  Then, take a thread and tie it around the penis beneath the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder.  Then, carefully insert the lancet into the urethra until the lancet reaches the stone itself.
Rotate the lancet slightly within the stone, aiming to pierce it until it exits from the other side.  Urine will then flow immediately.  Then, press on the remaining portion of the stone outside the penis; it will break up and be expelled with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not possible due to an obstacle, tie a thread below the stone and another above the stone.  Then, make an incision on the stone within the penis between the two threads, and remove it.
Then, loosen the threads and clean the congealed blood in the wound.  The thread was tied below the stone so that it would not return to the bladder, and the other thread was tied above it so that when the thread is loosened after the stone is removed, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when tying the upper thread, you should lift the skin upward so that it returns and covers the wound as we have said.

In removing stones in women.
