Chapter Sixty-two.
In the opening of the hydrocele.
Hydrocele is the accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum that is beneath the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It may also be in a special membrane that nature has prepared for it on one side of the testicle.
So that one might think it another testicle, and it is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we mentioned. This only happens rarely. This hydrocele arises from weakness, which is common to females, and this material is deposited there; a blow to the scrotum may also cause it.
This moisture may have various colors; it may be yellow, or blood-red, or dark-grey, or whitish like water, and the latter is the most frequent.  The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum that we mentioned, the swelling is somewhat oblong, like an egg, and the testicle is not visible, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a membrane specific to it, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, one might imagine it to be another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotum and the white peritoneum, it is palpable.  To determine the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe, as its image was previously shown.  Judge the color by what emerges from beneath the probe.
If we proceed to treatment with iron, the patient should be bled if possible, and if you see that their body is full, then the patient should lie on their back on something slightly elevated, with many cloths placed underneath.  Then you sit on their left side.
And instruct a servant to sit on his right side, extending the patient's penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the groin.  Then, take a wide scalpel and incise the scrotum lengthwise from the middle to near the pubic area.
The incision should be straight, parallel to the line dividing the scrotum in half, until you reach the white peritoneum.  Carefully dissect it, taking care not to cut it.  Your dissection should be primarily along the side that adheres to the testicle, and continue as far as possible.
Then, make a wide incision into the water-filled peritoneum and remove all the water.  Then, separate the edges of the incision with forceps, and lift the peritoneum upward, avoiding touching the scrotal skin.  Cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either in one piece or in sections.
Especially the thin part, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure the water will not return.  If the testicle protrudes from the skin during your work, replace it and then stitch together the edges of the scrotal skin.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until it heals.  If the testicle has been damaged by another illness, you should tie off the vessels in the sac to prevent bleeding, and then remove the testicle from the sac and take out the testicle.  Then treat it as previously described.
If the accumulated water is in both sides, know that they are two separate sacs.  Incise the other side in the same manner as the first, or if you prefer to work in a single procedure, do so.  Then wipe away the blood.
Insert wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  Place another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of syrup and oil over the testicles and the abdominal area.  Put folded bandages (the *rafayd*) over that. Tie it down from above with a bandage having six ends, in this manner.
Then take two bandages, sew them together, and fill them with fluffy wool, in this manner.  Make the ends for tying from cotton or damp wool.  Secure the bandages with a belt tightened around the waist, one end extending up toward the back on the buttocks.
The second end extends toward the pubic area, and the other two ends go down the thighs.  The remaining two ends go to the upper thighs.  Tighten the whole thing with a belt around the waist.  This dressing can also be made using cautery instead of incision with an iron.
This involves using a gentle, heated scalpel to incise the scrotum, until the white membrane containing water is exposed. Take another cautery iron, in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye.  Then use it to cauterize the membrane, while it is hot, until all the moisture is removed.  Then pull the membrane with hooks, and cauterize and cut the surrounding layer as much as possible to remove all of it, avoiding the egg, so that the fire does not touch it.
Know that this method is safer and prevents bleeding, and is better than using incision. If during the incision or cauterization, a hot swelling, or bleeding, or something else arises, it is necessary to treat whatever arises with the appropriate treatment as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot tolerate this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then let him use the cauterization as I have described, if it is suitable to him according to what has been presented before as evidence.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should be seated on a raised chair. Then, cauterize the swelling at its base with a wide spatula. Make the cauterization along the body, and make the opening wide enough for all the water to drain. Then, place cotton on the area and bandage it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during the cauterization, it is because the white membrane is obstructing the opening of the wound, preventing the water from draining.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the instrument used to extract water from abscesses, whose image was previously presented, or enlarge the wound slightly.
Know that the water may return and collect after six months or so. If it collects, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should endure the days.
Chapter Sixty-three.
On the incision of fleshy sores and their treatment.
Know that incisions on these fleshy sores are fraught with danger, leading to death in most cases. Therefore, we recommend leaving them alone and avoiding them.  I will now describe the treatments and various types of tumors.
I say that many tumors can arise in the bodies where the female organs are located.  This may be due to various causes, either from a sharp fluid discharge into the female organs or a blow.  The color of the tumor is the same as the body's color, and there is no water [in it].
The tumor is hard, and it may be a hardened mass, dark in color and without sensation. It may also arise from the twisting and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins.  If from the swelling of the arteries, this is recognized by the tumor's dispersion.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be incised at all.  However, if from the swelling of the veins, nothing disperses when pressed with the fingers.  This type can be incised, as well as other fleshy tissues.
This involves incising the scrotum, then lifting the testicle upward and removing it from the white peritoneum.  The adhesion is separated from the vessels, the vessels are tied off, and the adhesion is cut after being freed from every part of the testicle.  This is if the testicle is adhered to those growing tissues.
Therefore, the testicle should be removed and cut if there is an adhesion between parts of the peritoneum or between the vessels.  In that case, all such adhesions must be freed and cut in a circular fashion.
If the fleshy growth is in the area of adhesion behind, all of it must be cut and the testicle removed, as we said.  Once your work is complete, treat the wound with wet wool soaked in rose-scented oil and drink, then continue treatment until healed.
Chapter Sixty-four.
On treating dropsy associated with dropsy.
Dropsy is a swollen, somewhat twisted lump, resembling a cluster, with relaxation of the thighs, making movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the afflicted person.  In treating this affliction, the method is similar to that previously described.
However, it is necessary to mention the method used by our predecessors. I say that the afflicted person should sit on a raised chair, then pull the thighs downward, and then hold the scrotum with your fingers, along with the vessels close to the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it and stretch it forcefully.  Then, using a broad, sharp scalpel, make a slanted incision next to the vessels until they are exposed.  Then, dissect from every side, as I described for the dissection of the arteries in the temples, and insert a needle with a doubled thread into them.
Tie the threads at the initial points where the dropsy appeared and also at the end of it.  Then, cut it open in the middle, vertically along the body, and remove the accumulated foul, corrupted moisture.
Then treat the wound like any other surgical wound that needs time to heal.  Removing the vessels that nourish one thigh is acceptable. If the dropsy affects all the vessels, then one thigh should be removed along with the vessels so that the egg does not lack nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wilt and be of no use.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal dropsy.
This dropsy occurs when a rupture appears in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen, near the groin area of the abdominal walls, causing the intestines to protrude into one of the thighs.
This hernia may be either from a rupture in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types occur from many causes, such as a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, and the like.  If it is from an extension of the peritoneum, the hernia will develop gradually over a long time.
It does not appear suddenly. The swelling is flat and roughly deep, as the peritoneum compresses the intestines.  If the hernia is from a rupture in the peritoneum, it will initially cause great pain immediately and the swelling will be noticeably different, appearing under the skin nearby.
This is due to the intestines protruding outward from the peritoneum.  Pus may also exit with the intestines, in which case the dropsy is described as intestinal and purulent.  It may also be accompanied by gas, or the intestines may contain fecal matter that becomes trapped, leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when compressed.  Treating these types of illnesses with iron is dangerous, so one should avoid it.  The method is to instruct the patient to push the intestines back into the abdominal cavity with their hand if they can be returned.
Then, the patient lies supine with you at their side, raising their legs.  Then, you pull up the skin near the groin and make a lengthwise incision through the entire scrotal skin.  Then, you insert hooks into the edges of the incision, as much as necessary to open the cut further.
The incision should be as wide as possible to allow the testicle to emerge.  Then, peel back the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  Then, insert your index finger behind the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, separate the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, with your right hand, pull inward on the scrotal skin.  Simultaneously, with your left hand, pull the white peritoneum upward, lifting the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the attendant to pull the testicle upward.  You completely detach the adhesions behind it.  Use your finger to check for any twisted intestines within the tough white peritoneum.  If you find any, push them back into the abdominal cavity below.
Then, take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten strands, and insert it at the edge of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin near the incision.  Then, cut the ends of the thread so that four strands remain. Then, join some of the strands together in a knotted fashion and tie the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin firmly on both sides.  Then also wrap the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that no blood vessels feeding it can reach it. So that a hot swelling does not result.  Also make a second ligature outside the first, at least two finger-widths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a fingertip.
So that a hot swelling does not result.  Also make a second ligature outside the first, at least two finger-widths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a fingertip. Cut the rest completely around and remove the testicle. Then, make an incision below the scrotal skin that bleeds and drains as described previously.  Then, use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and use the ligature we described.
The white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting it, may require cauterization to prevent bleeding, as this often occurs.  Leave the ligature until it falls off on its own. If it falls off slowly, apply hot water and something that will make it sweat to cause it to fall off.
Then treat the wound with the standard treatment for surgical wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-six.
In this type of wind colic, I have not seen anyone dare to treat it with iron.  The ancients mentioned making it as described in the colic with a dhalia, meaning to tie the vessels gently below the incision.
Then cut through the middle and treat the swelling to open it, until the vessels fall out. Then treat the wound as previously described until healed.
Chapter Seventy-six.
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, but nothing from the intestines descends into the groin.  If it does descend, it is slight and returns at all times.
However, if it lasts a long time, the tear in the peritoneum increases so that the intestines and the testicles descend into the scrotum.  This appears from the extension of the peritoneum in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and protrudes the area, and its treatment is with cauterization, as I previously described.
And it may be treated with an iron in this manner: The patient lies on their back before you, then you make a transverse incision in the swollen, protruding hernia site, about the width of three fingers, and then cauterize the peritoneum beneath the skin.
Until the underlying white peritoneum is exposed. Then take a thread and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum, pressing it towards the depth of the abdomen, and then stitch the two protruding areas on the thread end of the peritoneum, fastening one to the other with the stitches.
Then pull the thread, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I informed you in the treatment of intestinal protrusions. Then treat it with surgical treatment.
When the threads are cut, clean and close the wound until it heals, then the peritoneum contracts, and the hernia protrusion does not increase.
And this is the best method in this case, because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the scrotum.
The scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, which makes it unsightly.  Therefore, whoever desires to treat this should have the patient lie on their back.
Cut all the relaxed skin from the thick skin, then bring the edges together.  If you wish, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, secure the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other surgical wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
The sixty-ninth chapter.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not mention it in this book of mine. However, I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be part of the physician's knowledge when questioned about it, and so that the afflicted may be treated, and the other that we frequently need to castrate certain animals for our own benefit.
Like sheep, oxen, and cats, and animals of that sort, I say that castration comes in two forms: either by crushing or by cutting and severing.  For that which is done by crushing, the method is that we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer discernible to the touch.  As for castration by cutting and severing, it is necessary to hold the animal and compress the skin of its testicles with the left hand, then tie the cords and make a single cut on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them after peeling them, leaving nothing of the membranes except the thin membrane that covers the vessels. This method of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing might leave some of the testicles, and the animal might desire mating, then treat the wound until it heals.
Seventieth Chapter.
On the treatment of hermaphrodites.
Hermaphrodites in men occur in two types. One type appears in the area near the testicles, between the testicles, resembling a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from the area within the scrotal skin.
In women, there is one type, located above the vulva on the pubis, like small, protruding male testicles.  One of them resembles a male penis, and both resemble female genitalia.  And the treatment for these three types.
The two types of men and the single type of women, the excess flesh must be cut away until the effects are gone, then treated with the usual surgical procedures until healed. As for the second type of men from whom urine emerges from the scrotal skin, no treatment is necessary and no improvement will result.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the cutting of the clitoris and the protruding flesh of women's vulvas.
The clitoris may increase beyond its natural size, becoming unsightly and inflamed. In some women, it may enlarge to the extent of resembling a penis, interfering with sexual intercourse.
One should hold the excess clitoris with one's hand or forceps and cut it off, but not excessively or deeply into the root, so as to avoid excessive bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals.  As for the proliferating flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the uterus, filling it.
And sometimes it protrudes outward, like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients called it the "tail-like disease," and it should be cut off, just like the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of ruptures.
Rupture is when a woman's vagina is not perforated, or the perforation is small, and it may be either natural, present at birth, or accidental.  The accidental rupture is due to a prior cause, and it may arise from excess, growing flesh.
Or from thin or thick peritoneum, and may be located deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or at the top or bottom, and it prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and possibly menstruation.  This is determined by the midwife's examination.
If the obstruction is visible and close, then if it is not visible, she should investigate it with her fingers or with a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, then hasten to pierce it; this is done by placing something like a flap over the labia, and then place the thumbs of both hands together.
And the woman should be lying on her back with her legs spread apart. Then, firmly stretch the labia until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction is opened.  Then, take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. The woman should be treated daily so that the area does not close again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a broad scalpel, resembling a thin leaf. If the obstruction is from a growth of flesh, then grasp it with forceps and cut it off. Have with you remedies to stop bleeding without cauterizing, such as acacia, myrrh, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then, use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not heal quickly. Hold it for several days, and use a thread of dry linen, then treat it with the usual treatment for abscesses until it heals.  Another growth of flesh might appear in the uterus, which should also be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, the cancerous tumor that occurs in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On treating hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the veins' mouths, causing frequent copious bleeding.  If hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  Their types and signs were mentioned in the classification, and we will discuss them here.
As for what is curable and what is not, I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and not perceptible by touch, have no cure with iron.  Those in the mouth of the uterus, where they are perceptible, are the ones that are treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, and then the warts should be scraped with a lancet or a rough cloth, and cut from their roots.  Then, after bleeding from one of the blood-cutting lancets, but without pricking, powder should be applied to them.
Like qaqia, sheyan, and frankincense, and similar things.  Then, raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them in cold water.  If the bleeding persists, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and oak bark, and similar things.  Then, take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back. If the bleeding stops, good.  Otherwise, keep applying cupping to the back, breasts and chest without restrictions.  Then, take wool, dip it in the juice of plantain, or the juice of comfrey, or the juice of willow branches, with oak bark drink, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until healed.  As for the red sore, it resembles the heads of warts, rough in appearance.  It is necessary to cut off what is visible of it, in the same manner as with the warts, and treat it until healed.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the treatment of abscesses in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may appear in the uterus, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, gangrene, consuming ulcers, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and inflamed tumors.  All of these diseases and their types and signs have been described in the classification.
Therefore, in this section, we must discuss the inflamed tumor appearing in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors that accumulate over time. How should it be treated with iron?  One must observe if the pain of the tumor at its beginning is severe, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red, do not rush to treat it with iron. Treat it with what aids in maturation until these symptoms subside. Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats, leaning back.
And she should bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and place her arms below her knees, binding them with a suitable bandage.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument to open the cervix, the image of which will come later.  When using this instrument, one must estimate the depth of the woman's uterus, so that what enters the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
For if the instrument is deeper than the depth, then pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterus's depth. The coil that runs through the instrument should be on the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the attendant turns the coil to open the cervix.  If a boil appears and can be felt by hand, soft and thin, it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel.  If the entire abscess has discharged.
A soft wick dipped in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound.  The wick should come out of the incision in the cervix, and pure wool soaked in boiled baker's water should be placed externally on the uterus and pubis.
Then, after two days, it should be treated with the appropriate ointment until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water in which the root of sow thistle or long wormwood has been boiled.  Then return to the ointment. This is if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Iron should not be used to treat it; rather, it should be treated as described in the classification.
Chapter Seventy-five.
On instructing midwives on how to treat living fetuses that emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the natural form of childbirth.  Signs include seeing the woman lean forward, desire to smell the air, and lessen her discomfort from the lochia.
And if the fetus emerges quickly, know that this birth is natural.  It will emerge head-first, with the placenta either attached or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, you should gently press her abdomen to hasten the delivery of the fetus.
If it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it, and is cleansed thoroughly from impurities.  Any birth other than this is unnatural and reprehensible, as the fetus may emerge feet-first, with hands or feet emerging before the head.
And the fetus may emerge with one hand or one foot, or with its head and a hand, or emerge curled, or perhaps turn upside down, and so on, are all reprehensible forms. Therefore, the midwife must be skillful and gentle with all these things and beware of errors and mistakes.
And I explain each form and how to handle it, so you may recognize it and understand it. If the fetus emerges head-first—the natural way—and the woman experiences significant difficulty and her strength weakens, have her sit on a chair and direct women to assist her.
Wrap her womb with boiled dates and moist ointments. Then, the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers and cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, so that the moisture within it flows out, and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, the woman should be injected with the juice of dates and sherj ointment.  Then, after the injection, she should be induced to cough and be given a sneeze with a spoon, and her mouth and nose should be held. The fetus will emerge at once. If the fetus's hands emerge first, it is necessary to gently push them back, little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a stool, raising her legs upward. Then shake the stool on the ground, while the woman holds on to prevent falling during the shaking.  If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut off the hands and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them gently, for it will emerge.  If the fetus emerges on its feet, the feet should be pushed upward, then the fetus should be turned gradually until it is straightened, then one of the feet should be gently pulled.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to cough and sneeze with a spoon, for it will emerge. If it does not emerge as described, gently reposition the fetus until it is in its natural position, then it will emerge smoothly. If it still does not emerge by all of the described methods...
Take some juice of *al-khatmi*, juice of dates, sherj ointment, and dissolved gum. Grind them well in a mortar. Then apply this mixture to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen.  Then seat her in lukewarm water until the sharaaseef are reached.
If you see that her lower part has softened, make her a compress of myrrh and let her endure it. If she holds the compress for an hour, seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze, and close her mouth and nose, and gently press on her lower abdomen. Then the fetus will emerge immediately. The fetus will emerge on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands, then the woman should lie on her back, her feet dangling down while lying on a bed, and hold her hands. Then gently press on her upper abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her feet are elevated. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then press gently on above the sharaaseef until the fetus rises.  Then the midwife should insert her hand and gently reshape the fetus.
Tell the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The fetus emerges sideways, with one arm hanging down.  Try to straighten the arm. If you cannot straighten it, make the woman stand, and have her walk. If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously, then use the lubricant described using saliva. If the delivery is complete, seat her on a chair, then work to straighten the arm and put the fetus in its normal position. Make the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it emerges.
If the fetus emerges on its back, with outstretched arms, and its face towards the mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's hand and gently turn it and straighten it.  Once straightened, apply the grease and saliva we have previously described to the area.
Then tell her to move slowly. When she does, seat her on a chair and instruct her to lean to her right side.  Then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with a stick. This will cause it to emerge.  The fetus emerges upright on its side.
If it descends into the cervix in this condition, along with the placenta, split the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture. If it is clinging to the fetus's face and neck, cut it from the umbilical cord to prevent suffocation and death. Then push the fetus inwards until it is in the correct position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly; this will cause an easy delivery.  For the delivery of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins often are born, and three or four may be born and live, but this is rare.  However, five is something unnatural, and they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded by a single placenta, even if numerous.  They are separated by a membrane, a partition, connected to the umbilical cord of each one.  They may emerge in the same way as a single fetus, normally and easily, or they may emerge in a way that is considered undesirable, as described.
If any of them are difficult to deliver and present the unnatural forms we have mentioned, treat them as described.  Let the midwife be gentle, and do everything with care, patience, and ingenuity, finding a method that leads to safety.
Descriptions of fetuses conceived in the womb that may be miscarried.  One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived. I have heard that one woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing, All-Wise, all of this should be part of a midwife's knowledge.  Perhaps such a thing might happen to her someday, and she should respond appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
In the extraction of a dead fetus.
If you treat a dead fetus using the methods described in the classification and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use manual extraction, then you should observe. If the woman is strong and healthy, and there are no symptoms that suggest potential harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downwards, her legs raised, and an assistant should hold her from both sides or tie her to the bed to prevent her body from shifting when the fetus is being extracted.
Then, the mouth of her uterus is moistened with the moist ointments, along with the saliva of the *khatmi*, and the powders of fenugreek and linseed. Then, the midwife anoints her hands with these ointments and saliva, then gently inserts them into the uterus. Then, she seeks out a suitable location to insert the hooks into the fetus, and the corresponding locations for that.
Observe if the fetus is easily descended, head first. If so, insert the hooks into its eyes, or nape, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or collarbone, or into the areas near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
And if it descends feet first, then in the pubic area and the middle ribs.  One should grasp the hook with the right hand, and curve it between the fingers of the left hand, gently insert the hand and impale the hook into one of those locations that we mentioned.
Until it reaches an empty space, then insert another or a third hook opposite it, if needed, to ensure an equal pull and prevent tilting.  Then, the pulling should be even and not just straight, but also moving in all directions.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth during extraction.  And in between these actions, the pull should be relaxed if it becomes obstructed on one side. Then the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment, and insert them to rotate whatever is obstructing it. If some part of the fetus comes out, then reposition the hooks to other locations.
She should slightly raise it to do this until the entire fetus comes out. If a hand comes out before the rest and cannot be pushed back due to constriction, it should be wrapped in a cloth and pulled until the whole body is out, and then cut it off.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Likewise, this should be done for the other hand and feet. If the fetus's head is large and experiences pressure during its exit, or if there is accumulated fluid in the head, one should insert a pointed scalpel between the fingers.
With it, one should split the head to remove the fluid, or crush it with the instrument called *al-mashdakh*, the image of which will be shown with the images of other instruments in the next chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus has a large head by nature, one should split the skull or crush it with the *mashdakh*, as mentioned before. Then, the bones are removed with forceps. If the head emerges and becomes constricted at the collarbone, one should split it open so that the moisture in the chest drains out.
Then the chest will adhere. If the chest does not adhere and cannot be extracted, cut as much as possible.  If the lower abdomen is swollen or contains a lump, one should lance it to drain the moisture.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and guiding it to the mouth of the uterus is easy.  If it is constricted at the abdomen or chest, one should pull it with a cloth wrapped around the hand and cut the abdomen or chest to drain what is within.
If all the other organs have been removed and the head remains trapped, let the midwife insert her left hand.  If the mouth of the uterus is open, insert the hand into the cervix, locate the head, and pull it with the fingers towards the mouth of the uterus.  Then, insert a hook or two hooks and pull it out with them.
If the mouth of the uterus has closed due to a hot, inflamed tumor, one should not be forceful. Instead, one should use applications of oily, moist substances, use compresses, and sit in relaxing and moistening waters.
If the fetus is on its side, if it can be positioned, use the methods mentioned for a living fetus.  If this is not possible, cut and remove it.  Absolutely nothing of the placenta should be left inside.  Once you have finished your treatment, use the methods we previously described for hot tumors that appear in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, sit in constricting water and use other treatments.  I have witnessed a woman who became pregnant, the fetus died in her womb, and she subsequently became pregnant again.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared in her abdomen, swelling until it opened and discharged pus.  I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time.  The wound did not heal, so I applied some strong, drawing ointments to it.
A bone then emerged from the area, and after a few days another bone came out.  I was surprised by this, as the abdomen is not a location where bones are found. I concluded that these were bones from the deceased fetus. I probed the wound and removed many bones from the fetus's head.
I continued this procedure until I removed many bones, and the woman was in the best of health. She lived like this for a time, with a small amount of pus continuing to discharge from the area. I have presented this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to achieve by hand in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.

This binder, with which books are bound, has two spirals at the ends of the planks, but this spiral should be gentler than the binder, and should be either ebony or boxwood. The width of each plank should be the thickness of two fingers.
Their edges are about a finger's width, and their length is a and a half spans. In the middle of the planks are two rods made of the same wood as the planks, secured in them, with a length of half a span or a little more, and a width of about two fingers or a little more.  These two rods.
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals.  Image of another tool like this, gentler and lighter.
