Chapter seventy-eight.
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, the woman should be told to hold herself, then be made to sneeze with a censer, and you should press your hand over her mouth and nostrils.  If it comes out this way, do so. Otherwise, take a certain amount.
Pierce a hole in the cover, place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as fennel, rue, dill, chamomile, chamomile, rue, and centaury, all or some of these medicines.  Soak them in water, and place the container over the fire.  Then place a silver tube over the hole in the cover, and the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
Hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the uterus, then make her sneeze, as we said.  Then the placenta will come out quickly.  If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in deer's fat or the saliva of a rhinoceros.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta.  If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out slowly, little by little.  If it is stuck deep within the uterus, insert the hand as described until the placenta is located and pull it out gently, little by little, with care.
So that the uterus does not fall when pulled too hard, rather it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left.  Then the amount of pulling can be increased.  Then it will respond and detach from the adhesion.  If the cervix is closed, we have already described the treatment with sneezing, the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, avoid force when pulling it again.  Instead, tie what has come out to the woman's thigh, near the opening of the uterus. Then inject her with the four-ingredient ointment, for it will cause it to fester after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out.  However, if it festers, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, suitable incense should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried incense of frankincense and dried figs; the image of the device used to fumigate the woman when menses and the placenta are retained, and similar situations.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper. The narrow end is placed at the vulva, and the wider end is placed on the fire. Incense is placed on the embers, held until it is gone, and then replaced with more.
The seventy-ninth chapter.
In treating the unperforated rectum.
Many boys may be born with unperforated rectums, blocked by thin peritoneum.  The midwife should pierce this peritoneum with her finger, or otherwise incise it with a sharp scalpel, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and treat with ointments until healed. If you fear that it will close again, insert a lead tube into the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
The rectum may also be blocked by a scar or a tumor.  One should incise the scar tissue, and then treat it with the remedies mentioned before and cauterize it.
Eighty-eighth Chapter.
On the treatment of warts that occur on the lower parts of the body.
Warts that occur on the lower parts are a thickening and hardening that develops near the anus, either externally or in the space on one side, and may be one or more in number.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge a whitish watery fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate to the rectum and intestines.  Those that do not penetrate are identified by what is discharged through the stool and flatus when the patient uses the restroom.
Sometimes worms may be discharged from them.  Some warts, if located in the space and penetrating the bladder or urethra, might be found; others might penetrate the hip joint or the sacrum.  Warts that penetrate the anus can be identified in the same way as non-penetrating ones.
Insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe of copper or iron into the wart.  If there is no deviation in the wart, proceed.  If there is a deviation, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.  If you feel nothing at all, and no stool, flatus, or worms emerge from the opening, as we said, then know it is not penetrating.  Proceed to treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, its sign is the discharge of urine from it and the inability of the affected area to heal with medications. If it penetrates the hip joint or sacrum, the sign is the probe reaching those areas.
If there is no deviation towards the bone, and pain is localized to one side, and pus continuously emerges, and if treatment is ineffective and the wound does not heal with ointment, these penetrating warts have no cure at all.
Treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt it.  However, for those warts that are not penetrating and not chronic, healing is expected with iron, as I describe and have experienced.  The patient should lie on their back before you.
Raise the patient's legs upward, with the thighs tilted toward the abdomen.  Then, if the wart has a deviation, insert the copper or lead probe until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the buttocks, you should then insert your index finger into the buttock.
If you feel the probe has penetrated your finger, exposed without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrating. Do not expend effort on it, for there is no cure, as we have said.
Some claim that it heals in rare cases.  A treatment that might be helpful is to heat a thin cautery iron, according to the size of the wart, as previously mentioned.  Insert the heated iron into the wart until it reaches the area near the buttock, then repeat this two or three times.
So that you know that all excess, clotted flesh, resembling a bird's quill, has been burned.  Then treat it with a flax thread soaked in clarified butter to remove the burned flesh, and then treat it with healing salves, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will not heal with any other treatment.  If you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger in the buttock, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the wart below the skin surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. As you pass the incision with the probe, while it is in the wart, until the incision reaches where the end of the probe ends, and the probe is removed and falls out.  Then cleanse the clotted flesh, which resembles a bird's feather shaft, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing salves until it heals. If blood obstructs your work in cutting away the flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good effects: burning the excess flesh.
And stop the bleeding, and dry the moisture, then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil, so that the area becomes purulent and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing salves that promote the growth of firm flesh.
These are the medicines that cause constriction and drying.  If it is set right, it has healed, and do not return to it. If the wart has reached deep into the buttock and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the buttock and search with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetrating, then there is no way to work on it except through hope and expectation.  That is to use one of three methods: either cauterize it, as we said.
Or to cut it open until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the thread and treatment from near.  Do not continue the incision too far lest you sever the muscle surrounding the buttock, causing the patient to involuntarily pass stool.  Then treat it as we mentioned, and perhaps it will heal, as we said.
Or to pierce that membrane and puncture it after cutting it open to near the buttock, as we said, either with a probe or another sharp-pointed instrument. Then, remove all the hardened flesh that is in the incision as far as you can.
Then sew up the entire wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed, leaving the wart open inside the buttock, which will be less burdensome on the patient.  The wart may heal in this manner.
If, when inserting the probe into the wart and it is on the side of the buttock, near the body surface, with skin and the buttock's circumference, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's awl in this manner:
And insert a twisted thread of five threads or so into it.  Then, insert the probe with the thread into the wart until it reaches its bottom. If it is punctured in the margin of the buttock from the inside, close by.
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the buttock and pulling out the end of the thread.  Tie both ends together firmly and leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls away.  Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the wart is not punctured, puncture it as best you can, except if it is very deep, in which case you must not do this due to the muscle, lest you cut it. Then, do with it as we described until it heals.
The shape of the lancet's spine, with which you puncture warts, should have a very sharp curve on one side and a blunt side on the other, so that you do not cut what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning anal fissures that bleed and their incision and treatment.
Hemorrhoids come in two types: either they are within the anus, resembling red blisters or like grapes, and they are small or large, and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or multiple, and some are external, at the edges of the anus. However, those that are external are usually less moist, dripping yellow water or a small amount of blood constantly, and are the color of the body.
The treatment for those within the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus is stretched and the polyps are exposed. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or your fingernail and cut them at their roots. If the forceps do not hold them due to their moisture and looseness,
Then grab it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened wood, or pull it with your fingers, and cut it. Then, after cutting, sprinkle some sharp powders on it to act as cautery, or cauterize it as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  If the anus does not come out, administer an enema to the patient with a small amount of irritant to wash out what is in the anus and cause it to come out quickly when the patient strains.  As for polyps outside the anus, their treatment is easy and simple.
To treat them, grasp them with your fingernail or forceps and cut them. Then treat them with the methods mentioned until they heal.  If cutting with metal is undesirable, then it is appropriate to use a perforation method.  Take a twisted thread and thread it through a needle.
Then pull the polyp upward, and pierce it with the needle at its root from the other side. Wrap the ends of the thread under the needle which is positioned across it, and pull the polyp firmly. Tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the polyps, leaving one unpierced.
To cause blood to flow from it, then place a cloth soaked in rose or cypress oil on the anus.  Have the patient rest, and leave it until the polyps fall off.  If the polyps fall off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
As for treating fissures, they frequently arise from dryness, accumulation of waste matter, or impacted humors.  If they persist and are not helped by treatment, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist and the upper crust that prevents adhesion disappears.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more forcefully than before until it bleeds well and the skin is peeled off. Then treat it, and it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and non-ingrown, dry warts, and the pimple.
A corn is a rounded, flesh-colored nodule, resembling the head of a nail, that appears throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, causing pain when walking.
One should dissect the area around the corn, grasp it with a lancet or a hook, and pull it out from its root. Then treat it. Or if you prefer, cauterize it as described in the relevant section.  As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, hardened, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They often appear on children's hands.  Treatment is to enlarge the wart and cut it or pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off.  Or, if you wish, cauterize it as before with fire or a sharp medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts can be removed with the instrument mentioned in the treatment of the pimple.  A pimple is also a small, hardened, thick protrusion on the skin, deep-seated.  If exposed to cold, a sensation similar to the sting of ants is felt.
It appears throughout the body, but most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a tube of strong goose or eagle feather and placing it around the pimple so that the tube encircles it completely. Then, rotate your hand with the tube until it breaks the skin surrounding the pimple.
And place it deep within the flesh, and the pimple is pulled out from its root.  Or, if you wish, do this with a brass or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted, to allow the fingers to grip and turn it easily.  Or, if you wish, cauterize the pimple as described previously in the section on cauterizing warts.
Chapter Eighty-three.
Images of instruments used for injections in conditions of the rectum, diarrhea, and colic.
The enema syringe may be made of silver, or of china, or of hollow or hammered copper.  Small and large versions of this instrument can be made, depending on the users. Those used to treat young children or those with narrow or painful rectums should be very delicate.  Image of a large enema syringe:.
The upper funnel, where the bag is attached, should be wide in this manner, having a partition where the bag is attached, as you see.  The lower end, which enters the rectum, should be very smooth and tight, with two small holes on one side and a single hole on the other.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a reed, or slightly thicker. The bag for holding the medications, made of an animal's bladder or a sheep's intestine, should be shaped like a small plate, with a diameter of a span.
Many holes are pierced in it, in a circular pattern, with the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger.  Then a strong thread made of doubled thread, of ten or so strands, is inserted into these holes, and the bag is gathered together like a plate. Once the medicine is inside, the top of the syringe should be tightly squeezed between the stones, then it is used to administer the enema.  This is an image of the bag and the holes:.
The method for administering the enema is to fill the bag or bladder with the medication, warmed to the patient's tolerance.  Be careful not to use a medication that is too hot or too cold. The patient should lie on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed beneath the patient.  The tip of the syringe is smeared with ointment, egg white, or licorice juice, or similar.  Then, it is gently inserted into the anus.  The attendant holding the bag should squeeze it firmly with both hands until all the contents of the syringe have emptied into the intestines.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient lowers their legs. If the medicine stimulates expulsion, the patient should hold it back as much as possible.  If they sleep with it in all night, it will be more effective in alleviating the ailment.  If the enema is used for diarrheal conditions and the expulsion is hastened, repeat the enema until the patient is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the anus, the syringe should be extremely gentle, smoother than the syringes used for children.  This is an image of a gentle, smooth-skinned syringe:.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have previously discussed the treatment of conditions similar to burns in the classification.  Here, I will discuss their treatment with the hands and medicines in detail.  I say that wounds differ according to the object that causes the wound and according to the location of the wound.
The objects causing wounds are numerous, such as blows from a stone, cuts from a sword or knife, stab wounds from a spear, stick, or arrow, and similar numerous objects.
And as for wounds according to locations on the body, they are like wounds that occur on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
I will describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a standard and rule for all wounds. I will begin with simple head wounds, especially because the compound ones will be mentioned in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a bone fracture, then if it was caused by a blow from a stone or similar, and only the skin was bruised, and the wound was large, and you feared the occurrence of a hot swelling, then immediately lance the scalp at the site. Do not delay this, and draw blood from him according to his strength, unless there is a reason to prevent the lancing, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little.  Let the lancing be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and thick foods. If there is a reason to prevent lancing, then reduce the food, avoid meat and drinks.  If a hot swelling occurs, apply a linen cloth dipped in rose oil, alone or with a drink that has a binding effect, to the wound.
If you are not concerned about a hot swelling, apply to the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and the air has not altered it (the air-alteration I will describe to you shortly), some ointments, if the air has altered it.
Until pus is produced, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and caused by a sword or similar, and its edges have not come together through natural healing, bring them together with stitches, as I will describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its attachment is only in a slight area, then cut it away. Then treat the wound with medications to build strong flesh in place of the skin. Instruct the patient to eat strengthening foods, such as heads and tails, and similar foods.
If the skin develops gangrene, and it is not attached to the bone, cut away all the gangrenous tissue and discard it. Then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding, and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, then locate the artery.
If you strike it and it does not stop, cut it with the scalpel and tie it off. If the previously mentioned methods are ineffective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops.  If these wounds are small and uncomplicated, their treatment is easily accomplished simply by applying this powder while the wound is still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is: one part frankincense, two parts cinnamon, and three parts slaked or un-slaked lime.  Grind all of these together, sieve them, and fill the wound with it, securing it firmly so that it adheres well.
It will clot with blood, then it should be secured from above with bandages.  Do not remove it as long as it does not swell.  The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  Lime alone can sometimes achieve this effect if frankincense and cinnamon are unavailable.
This can also be done with the lime in many large wounds, if it is carefully applied and secured.  This works for wounds that are fresh with blood, as long as the air has not changed them. However, if the air has altered the wound somewhat, especially during the summer.
There is no effective treatment in applying this powder.  Instead, treat it with some ripening ointments, or apply a poultice of barley flour mixed with water and honey to encourage the pus to drain. Then treat with other remedies until healed.
If a minor bone fracture occurs in the wound, remove it with a cloth.  The treatment of skull fractures is mentioned elsewhere, in the section on neck injuries.  If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it's a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris, alum, and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause spasms.
Never apply anything cold to the wound, because the nerve's essence is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs. Use gentle remedies such as washed rose petals, repeatedly moistened with fresh water or oil, or rose ointment and zinc oxide.
If washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this way, and with moist poultices.  If the wound is large, use stitches or bring the edges together with splints, then treat until healed.
If the wound has a cavity and pus has collected at the bottom, drain it at the lowest point. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see blood flowing, tie it off or cauterize it, if necessary.
If some or all of the cartilages of the larynx are severed, but the vessels are intact, stitch the skin edges together around the larynx. Do not touch the larynx itself, but smooth and restore it to its natural shape, then firmly bind it and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with astringent and drying ointments until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it. If there are fragments of bone, remove them all or as many as possible. If not all can be removed, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described.  If bone in the wound becomes necrotic and forms an abscess, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you see a cavity, observe if air is expelled when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a fatal wound. If it has no cavity and is fresh with blood, do not immediately apply the powder nor tighten it, lest the blood be trapped in the cavity.
It may recede to the heart and kill the patient. However, apply a drawing ointment twice a day. If ointment is unavailable, place a worn linen pad in the wound to absorb the moisture, and let the patient sleep with the wound uppermost.
To drain what collects there.  If three days or more have passed, and the patient shows no spasms, poor palpitations, or shortness of breath, and you find all other conditions good, know the wound is sound. Treat it with bandages and other therapies until it heals.
If the wound fails to heal and suppuration persists, know it has become an abscess. Treat it as such.  The patient should lie on their wound to drain what collects there.  If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the stitching described earlier.
If large, or with the powder if small, and if it has affected the bone, breaking off fragments, then probe the wound and remove those fragments if they are loose. If not loose, leave them.
The wound will fester, then they will be easier to remove.  As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment is governed by the principles we have described.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On wounds of the abdomen, the expulsion of intestines, and their suturing.
A wound appearing in the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. A large wound may expose intestines, or several intestines, making their replacement and suturing more difficult and arduous.
A small wound, too, may present difficulties in another way, making its repair more challenging.  If the intestines are not returned to their place promptly, they swell and thicken, making their replacement more difficult.  For this reason, the most favorable wound is the medium one, as it does not present the same difficulty in returning the intestines as the other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen can be done in four ways. Two of the methods are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and other bodily wounds. The other two are specific, applying to all wounds but especially suited to suturing the abdomen.
I will describe them to you one by one, with explanation. If the wound is small and something of the intestines protrudes, and its return is difficult, it is due to one of two reasons: either the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestines were previously inflated by a cold air.
If this is the case, you should warm it by dipping a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, alone or with cooked herbs such as izhkhur, sa'di, and sanbal, and applying it to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with a binding quality may also achieve this, and is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.  If you see the swelling subside, moisten the intestines with water in which khitmi or khabazi herbs have been cooked; this will facilitate their easier return.
If, after this treatment, the intestines still do not return, make a small incision in the wound using the instrument used for lancing abscesses.  The initial treatment, if successful, is preferable to incision.  Incision is only resorted to after necessity and the failure of other remedies.  Here is a depiction of the instrument.
Its one curved side is limited, the other side unlimited. The thin end is not as thin as a scalpel, but slightly blunt, resembling a small, narrow knife, as you see.  If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, the intestines should be returned to their natural position.
Its specific location, if possible, is preferable. However, if the wound is large and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is located in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be raised higher than the lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is in one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the wound elevated higher than the other. This approach should also be used in major and minor surgical procedures.
For minor surgeries, position the patient as best as possible. Then, have a helpful attendant hold the entire wound with both hands, bringing the edges together, and gradually exposing portions to the surgeon.
This is a common method for suturing: Take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound. Leave an area equal to the thickness of the little finger at the edge of the wound and pierce a single needle into both skin edges without thread. Piercing through the skin's edge and the underlying peritoneum from the inside. This brings the skin and peritoneum edges together, forming four layers.  Then, use a double-threaded needle and wrap the thread around the needle multiple times on both sides to firmly close the wound edges.
Then leave an area equal to the finger's thickness and insert another needle.  Tie it off with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Continue this process with as many needles as needed to close the entire wound.  Use needles of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut through the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin, the needles should be of medium thickness.  Also, the needle should not be inserted too close to the edge of the skin.
To prevent the flesh from tearing quickly and the wound from reopening before healing, do not pull the stitches too tight, so as not to prevent the wound from healing.  Then, cut the needle points to avoid injuring the patient while sleeping.  Use linen strips on each side of the needles to secure them.
Leave them until you know the wound is healed. This type of stitching with needles is most suitable for minor surgeries, as one or two needles may suffice.
For the general method of stitching, gather the four edges—that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—all at once with a needle carrying a thread of moderate thickness.  Then, after the needle has traversed all four edges, return the needle to the starting point.
The thread will be interwoven from above the wound, creating stitches similar to those used for securing containers.  Leave a space between each stitch the width of a little finger.  This is a stitch known to everyone.
With this stitch, a surgical procedure was performed on a man who had a knife wound to his abdomen.  The surgical opening was more than a span in width, and nearly two spans of the small intestine had protruded through the opening in the middle of his abdomen.
He returned the intestines after they had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered.  He lived many years afterward, functioning normally.  Doctors had previously deemed him incurable.
Surprisingly, I did not treat it with ointment, as I was in a place lacking medicine. Instead, I placed used cotton on the wound twice daily and ensured it was washed with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specific types of stitches, I mention it in accordance with Galen's writings: begin the stitching from the skin, inserting the needle from outside to inside.  Once the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle extending straight along the length of the abdomen.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle.  Then insert the needle into its opposite edge from inside to out, at the edge of the peritoneum.  If you insert it there, insert it again into the same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and insert the needle into its other edge, from inside to outside.  While inserting it into the peritoneum, also insert it into the edge of the peritoneum on that side, until it passes completely through.
Then, starting from the same side again, trace along the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side, and bring the needle out of the skin nearby, then return it into that skin, and trace along the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side with this edge of the peritoneum.
Bring it out of the skin on its side, and do this again and again until you stitch the entire surgical wound in this manner.  The general description of this stitching is that it should be like the stitching used by furriers for furs.
By stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leaving it once from the other side until it is empty.  The second type of specific stitching, which Galen also mentioned, is his words verbatim.
It is to stitch it in the manner that some healers stitch, by bringing each part to its corresponding part, naturally, by bringing the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge.  This is as I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the peritoneum closest to you from the outside, penetrating it inward alone, and leaving the edges of the peritoneum. Then, the needle should be withdrawn and inserted from the outside to the inside of both edges of the peritoneum, and then withdrawn again.
And penetrate it from inside to outside in the edge of the peritoneum on the other side.  This type of stitching is better than the general, simple stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's statement verbatim. Some have said that abdominal stitching may be accomplished with two needles. That is, inserting a single thread into both, beginning by inserting the needle from your side and penetrating to the other side, and inserting the other needle from the opposite side, penetrating from your side, according to the stitching of the saqqafa (leatherworkers) equally.
Know that a rupture in the middle of the abdomen is more difficult to stitch than other parts of the abdomen.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is like other wounds. If you encounter the wound fresh with its blood, before air has altered it, and you have returned the intestines to their proper places and stitched and secured them, then apply the medicated poultice.
If the wound has been altered by air, apply some of the ointments available until it suppurates, the sutures come out, and the peritoneum and peritoneum are joined together.  Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If no remedies are available, apply a piece of worn-out cotton as soon as suppuration begins, and change it twice a day, as I instructed you, until it heals.  If the wounds are minor, you generally do not need further treatment. However, if you fear the wound might cause pain in major organs.
Therefore, dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits.  If he feels pain or a foul odor in his bowels, which often occurs, then irrigate with a lukewarm, dark, astringent liquid.
Especially if the foul odor has reached the intestines and has become a penetrating wound into the abdomen. Know that the thicker portions of the intestines are easier to heal than the thinner parts.
The jejunum, however, does not heal from any wound that occurs in it, because of its many large blood vessels, its thin structure, and its proximity to the nerves.  If the extruded intestinal tissue is fresh, return it to its place as you would do for the other intestines.
If a period of time has passed and the extruded tissue has turned green or black, it should be tied off with thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, as the extruded tissue contains blood vessels and arteries.  Then cut what is below the ligature.
Tie the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound opening so that it is easy to pull it out when the extruded tissue falls away and the wound becomes suppurated.  Description of a wound that occurs in the intestines.  Now, if a small perforation occurs in the intestines...
Sometimes a person can be cured, for I have seen a man who had a wound in his abdomen from a spear thrust, to the right of the stomach. The wound persisted and became a fistula, discharging feces and air.  I treated him, though I had little hope of a cure. I continued to treat him until he recovered and the area healed.  After seeing the area healed, I feared that the patient might experience a bad event in his abdomen, but he experienced no such problem and remained in the best of health. Indeed, he ate, drank, had intercourse, bathed, and exercised, serving him. Some experienced practitioners have stated that if a small wound occurs in the intestines, it should be treated in this manner.
Take large ants by the head, then bring the edges of the wound together. Place one ant, its mouth open, on the wound edges. When it closes and bites, sever its head. It will adhere and not separate. Then place another ant near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then close and stitch the wound. Those heads remain stuck to the intestines until the intestines heal and the patient recovers completely without any complications. The intestines can also be stitched with thin thread.
To extract the animal adhesive from the Egyptian adhesive, one takes the end of this thread from the adhesive, and unties it. Then, a thin, spun linen thread is tied to its end, and that thread is then inserted into the needle. The Egyptian adhesive thread is used to stitch the intestines, then returned to the cavity.  This type of stitching, with ants and Egyptian adhesive, is only a matter of hope and expectation.
If the rupture is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy and no cure at all.
Chapter Eighty-six.
On the treatment of colds and rhinitis.
Know that any wound or tumor, if it becomes chronic, old, and ulcerated, and if it constantly discharges pus without stopping, is called, in general, in any organ, a fistula or a cold sore, and we call it a cold sore, or fistula.
It is, in fact, a hard, dense, white mass without pain, with a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some called it a feather.  Sometimes it is moist, continually discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may stop.  This moisture may be abundant, or scant, thick, or thin.
I previously stated in the classification of surgical cases that any ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes; one of them is either due to the lack of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges preventing the growth of good flesh. Or, it is due to its severe damage and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is foul, and the matter discharging from it is of poor quality; or because the treatment is inappropriate; or because a general disease has affected the town; or due to a local malady.
Or, it is due to its severe damage and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is foul, and the matter discharging from it is of poor quality; or because the treatment is inappropriate; or because a general disease has affected the town; or due to a local malady. As was observed in the city of Saragossa, where the ripening of diseases is difficult, and the healing of tumors is slow naturally; or because it contains one or more bones.  All these causes, their signs, and treatments with remedies have been explained in the classification.
Here, I must introduce you to the treatment of boils and nasal discharge, which is done by hand.  Know that boils can occur in all parts of the body, some leading to large veins and arteries, or to nerves.
or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of many complex joints like the hand and foot.  And some lead to the vicinity of a major organ and the like.
Those boils of this nature are difficult to heal, do not respond to treatment, and working on them is arduous and fruitless.  If there are boils that have not reached these mentioned locations, and you hope and expect to treat them...
If you want to find this out, take a probe of copper or iron.  If the boil runs straight, probe it with that. If the boil has a curve, probe it with a thin lead probe, for lead is soft.
It will slide when entering and turn toward the curve.  If the boil has many openings that you cannot detect with a probe, then inject one of its openings.  The moisture you inject will travel to the other openings and flow out.
Then, continue your search in any way possible to determine if there is a bone or nerve, whether the bottom of the boil is deep or close to the surface, or if it is a single boil with many openings.  Understand all this to the best of your ability, based on careful observation and the pain felt when touching the area.
And in a similar manner from the signs and causes of the swelling.  If you have truly ascertained all of this, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  Look, if...
If the boil is superficial, close to the surface, or in a healthy area far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the locations I have mentioned, then, as described previously, incise the boil and remove the hardened tissue, the corrupted flesh, and the excess flesh.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its opening, until it heals.  If the boil is deep and straight, you should incise it as deeply as possible and then clean it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then, use a twisted, medicated cloth soaked in strong medicine and insert it into the bottom of the boil, which you locate with the instrument.  Do this repeatedly until the strong medicine consumes all remaining corruption at the base of the boil.
Then, treat it with ointments that promote healthy flesh growth until it heals.  If it does not heal with that, cauterize it as previously described.  If the cause of the boil is bone, and you are sure of this, probe and incise it as I told you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
Or a vein, nerve, or vital organ, as we said.  If the bone is exposed to you and it has some corruption and blackness, remove the corruption until it is gone, then treat it with something that will heal it until it is cured. If it does not heal and the pus continues as before.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of the corruption.  Expose all of it again, thoroughly remove and cleanse it with your utmost effort, then treat it.  If it heals, well and good.  Otherwise, know that the corruption is in a deep, hidden place.  Expose all of it again.
Thoroughly remove and cleanse it with your utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, pull it out with the finest forceps suitable for that.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove them all, leaving nothing behind.  If you encounter ligaments that obstruct them, and cutting them poses no risk, cut those ligaments and any flesh present, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones in your time, apply something that will rot the wound and leave it for a number of days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones rot. Protect the wound, so that the opening you created during your work does not heal.
But if you fear this, apply a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur and oil, or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment, or one of the green ointments.  If these ligaments rot and the bones become loose, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone, and only its surface has become corrupted, thoroughly clean it until the blackness and corruption are gone, and then close the wound.  If a large part has become corrupted, and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it is necessary to break it up.
Cut it all the way to where the corruption ends.  Then treat it until it heals.  I tell you about a case of a young man with a disease in his leg to use as an example and aid in your treatment.
Around thirty years ago, he developed pain in his leg due to material moving from inside the body to the leg, swelling into a large tumor.  There was no external cause.  Then, time passed with incorrect treatments by the physicians until the tumor opened.
Much material drained from it, and he was poorly treated, until the leg became diseased and had many openings, all draining pus and bodily fluids.  A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them was skillful in the art of the hand.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, and large amounts of material were flowing from those openings. The man's body had wasted away, and his color had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them connected to others from all sides of the leg.  I then cut open one of those openings, exposing some of the bone. I found it corrupted, decayed, blackened, rotten, and perforated, with the hole reaching into the marrow.  I reported what I discovered.
I removed the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was nothing but this corruption that I excised and reported.  Then, I applied healing remedies to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.  I then re-exposed the bone, going beyond the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone.  I reported what I saw of this corruption as well.  Then, I tried to heal it, but it did not heal or close.  I then exposed it again.  I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempt to mend it, but it would not heal, until I had removed about a span of bone.
I removed it with its marrow, and then healed it with remedies. It healed quickly and recovered.  This repeated procedure of cutting and probing was necessary due to the patient's weakness, their limited tolerance, and my fear for their life, as they frequently suffered fainting spells due to excessive vomiting.  They recovered completely.
Firm flesh grew in place of the bone, and their condition in the body improved.  Their strength returned, and they resumed their activities.  No impairment of walking developed that would harm them at all.  If a bone in any part of the body is broken.
Therefore, it is necessary to expose it in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the protruding end of the bone, and instruct someone to pull it upward. Make another bandage of thicker wool than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone, and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards.  While exposing the flesh in the area you wish to expose, so that the saw does not injure the flesh, place a piece of wood or a plank beneath the bone, securely fastened.
For if you do this, the saw will not fail to cut the corrupted bone.  The incision should be slightly above the area of corruption, so that there is no corruption within the cavity of the bone that is not apparent on the surface, and you are forced to repeat the incision.
If the bone is corrupted but not protruding, and instead the parts are joined together with the corruption within its center or in a part of it, expose the flesh from all sides. Then place the plank beneath, and make the incision from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished the first incision, make an incision on the other side.  Let the incision be a short distance from the corruption, as we said.  If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and make an incision on the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision.  If the corruption is in the hand or foot, the treatment is very difficult, but you must scrape away the corruption as it appears to you and remove and cleanse it in any way you can.
By whatever means it works for you, as long as you do not encounter a vein or nerve. Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the spreading of bones.
If the limbs become putrid, either from an external or internal cause, and if you treat the putrefaction with medicinal remedies but the treatment is not effective and you see the putrefaction spreading in the limb, unchecked by anything.
Then you should amputate that limb at the point where the putrefaction has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great affliction greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign of this affliction is that the limb will turn black, so that you would think fire had burned it.
Or it may putrefy after turning black, so that this putrefaction spreads to the limb next to it and affects the rest of the body.  Proceed with amputation in this case as well, and similarly if the cause of the putrefaction is from the sting of some venomous creatures, like the sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and the like.
If the putrefaction or the sting is in the tip of the finger, amputate the finger and do not delay until the putrefaction spreads to the hand.  Likewise, if the putrefaction has reached the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm and do not delay until the putrefaction spreads to the elbow of the arm.
Likewise, if it has reached the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow, at the joint itself.  If the putrefaction progresses and you see it spreading towards the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for that would result in the patient's death.  Use other treatments according to your ability.
Likewise, do the same for the leg. If the putrefaction reaches the toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints. If it reaches the foot, amputate the entire leg.  If it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the putrefaction has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and allow the patient to succumb to death. The procedure for amputating or dissecting a limb is to tie a ligature below the location you want to cut and tie another ligature above the location.
One attendant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another attendant pulls the upper ligature upwards. You then use a broad scalpel to dissect the flesh between the ligatures until all the flesh is exposed. Then, cut or dissect it.  Cotton cloths should be placed on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct area and cause the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling.  If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the area quickly or apply some hemostatic material, then return to your treatment until finished.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until healed.  I will tell you about an example of this condition occurring in a man's leg.  It was precisely the same condition as I am describing to you, in that a blackness appeared on his leg accompanied by a burning sensation like fire.
This decay initially occurred in his finger, eventually affecting his entire foot. The man despaired when he saw the decay spreading through the limb, along with the intense pain and burning.  He cut it off at the joint, and he was cured.
After a long time, this same decay appeared again in his index finger. He came to me, and I applied the remedies I had on hand to the affected hand, after cleaning his body.  But the decay did not recede, and it began to spread to the other finger.
Until the decay spread to his entire hand, and he asked me to amputate his hand.  I refused, hoping to arrest the decay, and also fearing his death from the amputation, because his strength was failing. When he despaired of me, he left for his home.
I was later told that he eventually cut off his entire hand and was cured.  I have related this story to help understand this type of illness, and to serve as a guide.
Eighty-eighth Chapter.
On treating abscesses and how to inject remedies into them.
If a tumor forms in some fleshy organ and persists for a while, then bursts or breaks open, and all the matter within it is discharged, leaving the area empty as a vessel.
The skin over it is like a tattered cloth, thinned out, and not sufficiently corrupt to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament.  For this reason, it is called an abscess, and not a fistula, unless its duration extends so far that the corruption affects one of these structures.
In that case, it is called a fistula or ulcer. Its treatment is to cut away all the skin in a circular manner, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain it does not adhere to the affected area because of its corruption.
If you anticipate the skin might adhere, and the corruption has not reached that extent, and there is thick flesh in the skin, treat it with injections.  Examine the abscess; if it is large and the pus it discharges has a foul odor, inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
This is vinegar, oil, honey, and alum, equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens to the consistency of honey. Then, take the amount needed, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Bind the opening and leave the medicine in place for about two hours.
Then, express it out.  Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  If this medicine is unavailable, it can be injected with ash water.  Take the ash of the vine tree's wood, or oak wood, and pour water over it.
Then, strain it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed out.  If the patient is not likely to tolerate Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject with water and honey mixed with some ground alum.
Or inject with honey and drink mixed together, for honey is capable of cleansing and washing, and the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has an astringent and drying quality.  If you do this repeatedly and are certain the abscess's corruption is gone, then inject with something that promotes the growth of flesh there.
For example, take some terebinth ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and an astringent drink, or inject with some other ointments and remedies whose properties we have demonstrated in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and the injecting tube cannot be inserted,
If the opening is narrow, widen it slightly with an instrument, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or Cerigon ointment within it until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is wide, bring its edges together by stitching and leave just enough space for the syringe to pass through, but no more.
Also, if the opening from which pus is dripping is elevated, split it at the lowest point so that the pus drains downwards, because if pus is retained in the depths of the abscess, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you are unable to split the abscess downwards as desired, then consider positioning the affected part so that pus drains easily, as convenient, and ensure that no pus remains trapped within it at all.
As for the cohesive medicines to be applied to the abscess from the outside, and how to apply them: take one of the cohesive ointments suitable for your purpose, spread it on a linen cloth, and make the cloth large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut a hole slightly larger than the abscess opening opposite the abscess mouth.  Spread a soft ointment on another cloth, sized to fit the hole, and place it in a spaced-out manner on top of the hole so that pus can drain out easily. Do not remove the large cloth until later.
For several days, the one over the abscess opening is the one you remove at all times to drain any accumulated pus.  To know if the pus draining is abundant, little, mature, or unripe.
And with this also, examine the location of the abscess itself: does the patient feel pain there, or not? Is there swelling, or not?  If your treatment continues in this manner and you see the material draining from the abscess opening is scant.
Know then that the abscess has adhered or is close to adhering.  In that case, treat it again tomorrow or on the third day.  When you treat the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment.  If, after a long time, thin pus comes out of the abscess, do not despair of its adhering.
Then its healing is near, because some people have slow wound healing and some have fast.  If, after many days, unripe pus comes out of the abscess, know that the abscess has not adhered. If the abscess adhesion is delayed.
Then increase the drying medicines.  Let the medicines be moist in consistency and dry in strength, such as the palm ointment if more colocynth has been added to it.  The most effective remedy is to use palm ointment.
It is treated by applying rose ointment, and sprinkling moderate-strength, aged wine over it, and then mixing it with it and using it.  A similar method may be used if you do not have palm ointment: honey, boiled until it thickens, may be used; or take myrrh, frankincense, and kender, powder them all, and sprinkle them onto the honey while it is on the fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it to the abscess, or apply the boiled, thickened honey, sprinkle the medicaments over it, place cloths on it, and bind it.
Long zera, the root of the asphodel, crushed and sifted carnation flour, and the herb dittany, whether used singly or in combination, can also be used. Powder and sift these, and sprinkle them on the honey that I have described and apply it. If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption deep within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment for abscesses as previously described.
Eighty-ninth chapter.
In the treatment of the ingrown toenail, the injured fingernail, the removal of excess finger, and the separation of adhered fingers.
An ingrown toenail is excess flesh that grows under the fingernail of the hand or foot, and possibly on other fingers. If it is allowed to persist and its treatment is neglected, it will swell into a hot tumor.
Or it may fester and become gangrenous, even eating away the nail root and perhaps destroying the entire nail.  The decay may even reach the bone, causing a foul odor, broadening the end of the finger, and making it dark in color.  If you treat it as described in the classification, and your treatment is unsuccessful,
Then it is necessary to cut away all the excess flesh that grows from the nail with a scalpel, then cauterize the wound.  Cauterization is very beneficial in this case.  However, if the bone is healthy and the nail also healthy,
If the nail's corner has pressed the flesh inward, causing it to be pierced and irritated, a thin strip of material should be placed under the corner of the nail that is piercing the flesh, raising it upward, and the flesh should be gently cut away.
Apply cauterizing and corrosive medications to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat with ointments until healed.  If the decay has affected the bone, it is necessary to remove the affected bone, as it will not heal as long as there is diseased bone present.
If you see decay and corruption spreading in the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as previously described, and then treat it until it heals.  If the nail is injured or bruised, causing severe pain, it is necessary to first apply a blood-letting procedure.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, slit the nail with a slanted cut from top to bottom, taking care not to cut into the flesh beneath the nail.  Doing so will cause intense pain in the affected area and create excess tissue growth in the area.  Then treat the area with remedies that relieve pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may contain some bone, or even a nail, with some of its growth arising from the joint of another finger.
Or its growth may be in some of the phalanges of the finger; those growing in the phalanges of the finger will not move.  Those growing at the finger joint might move.  Any that are merely fleshy, are easily severed, by cutting them at their origin with a wide scalpel.
However, those growing at the origin of the joint are difficult to treat and should not be cut;  but those that sprout on a finger at one of the phalanges should first be cut, removing the flesh in a circular fashion towards the bone, and then the bone should be sliced with one of the appropriate saws.
Then treat it until it heals; as for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this is quite common, and may be congenital or result from the closing of a wound, burn, or similar injury. It is necessary to separate such adhesions.
So that the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them a piece of cloth or gauze soaked in rose oil, so that they do not quickly adhere again, or place a thin lead plate between them, until the area heals properly.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs between a finger and the palm, separate the adhesion in a way that best restores the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
In cutting the cords.
The cords are twisted, thick veins filled with dark, black phlegm, which occur in most parts of the body and are most frequent in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers and carriers.  First, the body should be thoroughly purged of the black bile through vigorous shaking.
Then, bleed the affected person using a lancet.  Treatment with iron can be of two kinds: one is to cut and remove the black blood, the other is to cut the vein and remove it entirely.  As for cutting, first, the leg should be soaked in hot water, indeed.
Until the thick, murky blood dissolves, then the leg of the patient is tightly bandaged from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage.  Then, the vein is cut in one, or two, or three wide cuts.
Then, remove the black blood from the bottom of the leg to the top, and from the top to the bottom, until you remove the amount of blood you deem sufficient, or what the strength of the patient can tolerate.  Then bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black bile, and repeat the purging and bleeding.
When the veins become filled and that harms the patient, as for cutting, it should be done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then have him enter a bath and soak his leg in hot water until it turns red and sweats, or he does a strenuous exercise.
If a bath is not available to heat the limb, then cut the skin lengthwise opposite the vein, either near the knee or below at the heel.  Then open the skin with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it is visible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and shiny.  When freed from the skin, it also appears like a tendon.  Then insert a probe underneath it until it rises and emerges from the skin.  Hook it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then cut another incision near that incision, about the width of three fingers.
Then scrape the skin off the vein until it is exposed. Then lift it with the probe as you did before and hook it with another hook, as you did at first. Then cut another incision or many incisions if necessary.
Then cut and sever it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull and cut it until it emerges from the second incision.  Then pull it toward the incision above it. Do this until you pull it out from the third incision above all the others, until when it is all out, then cut it.
If pulling and cutting does not work, insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Insert the probe underneath and twist your hand in all directions until it comes out, being careful not to break it, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult to remove.
This causes harm to the patient.  When you have removed it completely, place wool soaked in a drink and rose ointment or oil on the surgical site and treat it until it heals. If the vein is constricted, with twists and turns, curving in directions.
If it is not straight, as we said, you should make an incision on each twist and turn. Then hook it with hooks until you remove it completely. Be careful when making incisions not to cut or injure the vein, as this will make it harder to remove, so exert your effort carefully.

Chapter Ninety-one.
In the vein of the civilian.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries such as the Hijaz and Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely populated, infertile countries.  It may also arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from festering under the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and the scabs of ulcers, and the worms that develop between the skin and flesh.  A sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is that a severe burning sensation occurs in the leg, then the area becomes blistered, and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of the blister.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal.  When its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing one to two dirhams, and tied. The lead should be left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it outward, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows extensively, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it from its source before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a swelling, festering, and a bad ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it should be treated and pulled out little by little until it emerges completely from the end, leaving nothing of it in the body. In some people, this vein may reach five to ten cubits in length.
I have heard that it has emerged from a man to a length of twenty cubits. If it breaks off during your treatment, insert a sponge into the hole, and press it firmly against the body for a long time until all material inside it is emptied.  Try to heal the area with medicines for a number of days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or the leg itself, creating many openings and branches emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the classification and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the ulcerated worm that grows under the tendon, called the "cow's disease.
This disease is called "cow's disease" in some of our countries because it frequently affects cows.  It is a single, small worm that develops between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, ascending and descending.
Its movement from one part of the body to another is noticeable to the touch, until it pierces the skin at a point where it emerges, forming an ulcer from the putrefaction of some humors, similar to what occurs with worms, snakes, and the disease of the gourd in the abdomen.
However, the danger from it is that if it crawls throughout the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it might break through the eye and emerge, thereby destroying it.  This often happens.  If you want to treat it and remove it, it can only be done when it is crawling and visible to the touch.
Therefore, one should tie tightly around the area above and below it with a strong bandage, then lance it and remove it.  If it has burrowed into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cauterization to the area with fire until you burn it out.  The greatest danger is its possible damage to the eye, as we mentioned.
If you see it in the head near the eye, tie tightly under it on the forehead, then lance it and remove it.  The patient should also take care to cleanse the body with medications that relieve putrid, poor-quality humors, and to avoid foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-three.
On lancing the disease known as the "naفر".
This disease, called the "naفر" in our country, is a pain that appears in some organs and then moves from one organ to another.  I have seen it as I describe it to you.
I was summoned to a woman suffering in a remote area. She revealed her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the arm. After an hour, I saw that swelling crawling along the radius, like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain will subside from that spot and settle in the shoulder. Then, after an hour, it raced throughout the rest of the body until it was in the other arm, and she told me that it was circulating throughout her entire body, as I observed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one organ to another.  Before, I had only heard of this disease moving from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes like this, in this woman.
I surmised that this was because the woman was from the desert, her body lean and her veins exposed.  Therefore, this moving sensation was more apparent to the senses.  It is likely that this wouldn't appear in the same way in those who live in comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the sufferer feels the pain visibly, as we have described, then apply pressure above and below it quickly, and puncture it until the trapped wind/air emerges and is released from the affected area.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and remedies that expel or disperse wind, such as rotten-seed and scab-seed and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the removal of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the locations of their impact on the body.  Regarding their variation in types, some are large, some small; some are hollow glass, some solid glass; some have three angles, some four.
Some have tongues, some have shards.  As for their impact on the organs, they fall into two categories: either the organs are major hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, and bladder, and the like. If an arrow strikes one of these organs, and the signs of death that I will describe to you later appear, then you should avoid removing the arrow, for death will befall the person in most cases. But if these unfavorable signs do not appear, and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of a head injury from an arrow piercing the bone and wounding the membrane covering the brain, there is severe headache, dizziness, and fainting.
But if these unfavorable signs do not appear, and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of a head injury from an arrow piercing the bone and wounding the membrane covering the brain, there is severe headache, dizziness, and fainting. Redness in the eyes and tongue, spasms, confusion, vomiting, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears; possibly the loss of speech and voice, and the discharge of a white, porridge-like substance from the wound, resembling watery flesh.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not yet been removed.  Now, if the arrow strikes the heart, near the left breast, and it feels as if it has lodged in something solid, not hollow...
The arrow might also move like a pulse, and black blood flows from the wound.  This is followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting.  Know then that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels following the neck swell, the patient's color changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking inhaled cold air.  If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be heavy with severe pain.
He sighs and all the shoulder muscles move. If the arrow strikes the liver, intense pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in color flows from the wound.  If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food might be discharged from the wound.
His condition is obvious. If the arrow strikes the abdomen, pierces it, and something from the bowels, intestines, or anus is discharged from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or removal of the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder, urine will be discharged.
If something protrudes outward and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are more likely to survive the event.
Unless the arrow struck an artery or nerve, and was not poisoned, I will tell you some observations of these arrows to help you with treatment.  A man had an arrow strike the root of his nose, piercing his eye.
I extracted an arrow from the opposite side, under the earlobe, and he recovered without eye damage.  I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had lodged in his eye's lower eyelid,  and only the small tip touching the shaft remained.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, with a square, smooth iron head, and no barbs. The Jew recovered without further harm to his eye.  I removed another arrow from a Christian's throat; it was an Arabic arrow.
It had barbs, and I cut between the windpipes, as it was lodged in his throat.  I gently worked it out, and the Christian recovered.  I also removed another arrow from a man's stomach, and we judged he would die from it.
After about thirty days, with no change in his condition, I carefully worked on the arrow and extracted it. He recovered without further incident. I saw a man with an arrow in his back, and the wound had become closed over.
Seven years later, the arrow came out from the base of his thigh.  I saw a woman with an arrow in her stomach; the wound had healed over, and the arrow remained. Her condition did not change, nor did she experience any impairment in her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face; the wound had closed, and he felt little pain.  Many such cases exist. I removed an arrow from a sultan's commander who had been struck in the middle of his nose, slightly deviating toward the right side.
The arrow had completely disappeared. I was called to treat him three days after the injury.  I found the arrow wound very small.  I probed it with a fine instrument, but felt nothing.  He was experiencing a prickling and pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that the prickling was the arrowhead, so I applied a dressing with drawing and ripening properties, hoping the area would swell and reveal the arrowhead's location, so I could then open it.  But no sign appeared to indicate the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound eventually closed. The patient despaired of extracting the arrow for a long time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. He informed me, and I applied strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it. I felt the thin arrowhead, which was adhering to the nasal bone.  Then I continued to enlarge the opening with that strong medicine until the arrowhead became visible.  This took me about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I could insert the forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it wouldn't come out. I continued to manipulate it with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with the forceps, a description of which is given in the final part of this chapter.
The physicians had judged that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it. The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely, with no further problems.  I tell you this, to show how some arrows are extracted, as an example and guide for those cases that I have not yet described.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be grasped through description or contained within a book.  Instead, the skillful artisan judges the many from the few, the present from the past, and devises a new method and new tools for unusual cases.
If I now turn to this aspect of the craft, I say that arrows are removed from the organs where they lodge in two ways: either by pulling them out from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, the same side from which they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding into the fleshy tissue, and may be pulled out. If it does not come out immediately, you should leave it for a few days, so that the surrounding flesh rots, and extraction becomes easier.  Likewise, if it is lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for several days and continue pulling and manipulating it daily until it is extracted. If it does not emerge after several days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a fine awl to enlarge the opening for the arrow, then pull it out.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull bone, deep within one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms I have mentioned appear, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it for several days until its condition improves.
Then continue to pull and extract the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and cannot be extracted, use the awls around the arrow as I described before, then treat the affected area until it heals. Now, if the arrow is hidden in a part of the body, and it is hidden from sight, probe with a sounding instrument. If you feel it, pull it out with instruments suitable for the task. If you cannot do so because the wound is narrow and the arrow is deeply embedded, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way,
then widen the wound to gain access to the arrow and remove it. If it has two barbs, grasp them and remove the flesh entangled in each from all sides by whatever method you can.  If you cannot release the flesh, break and twist the barbs until you extract them.
And when trying to remove the arrow in any place, use twisting with forceps in all directions to extract it. Be extremely gentle so the arrow does not break, making extraction more difficult. If you cannot remove it in this time,
leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh rots. Then try again, for it will be easier to remove. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments described in their respective sections.  Be careful not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use any method possible.
By all means possible, extract the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly as previously described.  When pulling the arrow, the patient's position should return to the position it was in when the arrow was lodged, this is the best approach.
If this is not possible, use whatever form you can.  An arrow protruding from the opposite side either has part of it visible externally, or you can feel the tip of the arrow near the skin surface, protruding.
Cut into it, making the incision just large enough for the forceps to fit. Then pull it out, as this will make removal easier. If it is lodged in bone, twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow affects the bone and widens a space for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days, then try again until it comes out. If the arrowhead is still attached, push it out with it. If the arrowhead has come loose and you wish to use pushing, insert a hollow instrument into the arrow shaft, so that its cavity fits the shaft end, then push it out with the instrument.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits into the hollow, for this will make its removal easier. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the affected flesh, if possible, and then treat it with appropriate remedies.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is close enough to be probed, and you can make an incision, cut and be careful not to sever a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then stitch the wound if necessary, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:.
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted as though a file, so that when it grips an arrow or something, it will not release it.  Different sizes, large, small, and medium, are made, depending on the size of the arrow, the size of the wound, and its width.
Image of the hollowed-out cannon:.
Image of the solid cannon.
This one is solid at the tip, like a rod, to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-Five.
In the opening of veins.
The veins customarily opened in the body are thirty. Of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *ḥasīsīn*; the two veins in the temples, visible; and the two veins in the eye sockets, known as the *nāẓirīn*.
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins, known as the *jayārak*, and the two veins under the tongue.
And as for the veins opened in the arm and hand, there are five veins. One of them is the *qayfāl*, which is from the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the *akḥal*, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the *bāsiliq* and a branch of the *qayfāl*, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the *bāsiliq*, which is located on the inner side, and is also called the *ibṭiyya*, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen and the cord of the arm.
