It is located on the *zand* (forearm).  It is the one that adheres to it, and it is the one that appears clearly above the thumb, and the *ʾasīlīm*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, having two branches.  In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one beneath the bend of the knee on the outer side; the second, the *ṣāfin*, located at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the *nisā* (women), located at the ankle on the outer side.
And in the other leg, there are three veins similar to them.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening benefits chronic colds, migraine, shingles, and chronic, bad head sores; and their method of opening will be described as I state.
This is done by shaving the patient's head and rubbing its back in the location of the veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well. Then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban, so that the veins appear behind the ears in the two depressed areas of the head.  Then, you should probe them with your finger.  Where you feel a pulsation under your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then, take a scalpel, known as a *nishal*, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand, with the vein and skin, upwards, and cut the vein, together with the skin, in a precise cut. The length of the cut should be the length of two clenched fingers.
Or similar.  Release the desired amount of blood, then tie them off with bandages, and leave them until healed.  Their incision and cauterization are mentioned earlier in the book.  As for the arteries in the temples, their opening benefits chronic migraines, severe headaches, chronic eye inflammation, and acute, intense watering of the eyes.
Their method of opening, as I describe to you, is to tie the patient's neck with a turban until the veins become clearly visible, and their pulsation is apparent under your finger.  Then, mark the spots with ink.  Afterwards, lift the skin above the vein with your index finger.
Insert the *nishal* scalpel underneath and lift the vein upwards, and sever it as you did with the other veins.  Release the blood according to your need. Then, loosen the patient's neck constriction and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then, apply a compress and bandage, tying securely from above. Leave it until healed. Their incision, cauterization, and lancing are mentioned previously in the book.  As for opening the forehead vein, its benefit follows the opening of the temporal veins for chronic facial conditions like shingles, sores, and inflamed redness.
The method of opening it, as I describe to you, is to have the patient tie their neck with a turban until the vein is visible.  Then, take the instrument called the *fāʾs* (axe). Here is its picture:.
Place the pointed tip of the axe on the vein. Strike from above with a chisel or similar tool. Allow the blood to flow as desired.  Then, loosen the patient's neck constriction and tie it securely until healed.
It may be opened with a broad scalpel, but the scalpel should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels.  Rather, it should be slightly broad-bladed and opened against the grain because the bone is close by.  Otherwise, the scalpel might break if it is thin.
As for the veins in the eye sockets, their benefit is for eye ailments like scabies, redness, and discharge, and facial diseases.  As for their opening, the patient should tie their neck with a turban.  Then, open them while standing over their head.  The opening should be done with a small, slightly broad-bladed scalpel, against the grain, slightly lengthwise.
If the area has little flesh, the scalpel may break if its tip is thin.  Then, take the necessary amount of blood, place a bandage over the veins, and tie them tightly for one night.  Then, loosen them.  As for opening the nasal vein, it is helpful for acute fevers.
Severe headaches and facial diseases, such as the red inflammation that appears on the nose, especially if chronic, are treated this way: The patient's neck should be tied.  Then, hold the nose with your left hand and take a long, thin scalpel.
Insert it into the middle of the nostril itself, between the nasal passages, in a straight line, because the vein is not noticeable there. The blood will immediately flow. You should push the scalpel in slightly and take the needed blood, then tie it up for a night.  It will clot quickly.
As for the veins of the armpits, opening them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and black humoral diseases that appear on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, and foul ulcers and sores.  Their opening is done by tying the patient's neck beneath them with a bandage. The surgeon should stand at the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair.  Then, open a wide but shallow incision along the vein.  Draw off the moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed.  Do the same with the other vein. Then, loosen the bandage.
Tie the two veins with a moderate tightness so that the patient does not suffocate. Leave them until the next day. The wound will heal.  As for the veins of the temples, opening them after opening the cephalic vein is beneficial for mouth sores, gum diseases, foul ulcers, cracked lips, and foul sores.
Which are in the nose and surrounding it.  The method for opening them is to have the patient sit in front of you, and tie their neck with a turban.  Then, turn their lips outward, and look for the two veins, one on the right of the lip, and the other on the left.  Observe their blackness.
Also, there are thin, black veins around them.  Cut these veins completely off.  If it is unclear to you, and you do not know which to take, then aim at cutting the largest and most obvious ones.  Similarly, do the same with the veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, opening them after opening the cephalic vein is beneficial for the obstructions that occur in the throat from diseases of the tongue and diseases of the mouth.  The method for opening them is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, raise their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side and the other, where you will find a vein on each side. Their color is dark.
Open them, but take care not to cut them deeply, as there are arteries beneath them, and bleeding from these arteries may occur. The three veins that are opened at the elbow are the ones that have been customarily opened in all people. Their opening is in two ways.
Either with a wide, fennel-shaped lancet, or an olive-shaped lancet, to the point of softness; or with a lancet knife, which is a puncture [and this is its image]. Image of the wide fennel-shaped lancet:
It should be wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, obvious, thick veins that contain thick, dark blood.  And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a finer tip, suitable for opening thin veins that contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is the image of the puncture lancet:
This puncture, which is suitable for incision, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width or narrowness of the veins. It can be used as a guide for other similar instruments. It is well known among craftsmen.
Regarding the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the ills that are beneath the throat and neck, from the chest and abdomen.  The phlebotomist should be cautious when making the incision, and should be aware of its location. If there is an artery beneath, an error in the lancet's insertion will sever that artery.
Bleeding will result, therefore it is necessary that its incision not be made with a pointed lancet, but rather with a puncture. If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, one should avoid it and turn to another vein or seek one of its branches.
Or, one can incise the brachial artery, which is visible, with the puncture lancet, as we have mentioned. If one desires to incise the basilic vein itself, then before tightening the arm, one should palpate the area until the pulse point is located, then mark it with ink.
Then, the arm is bound, and the vein is incised with a deflected puncture, with the lancet as we mentioned, carefully ensuring the blow is away from the artery's location.  If, upon tightening the bandage, you see a swelling in the marked area, this swelling is the expansion of the artery, so avoid it.
If, during the phlebotomy, you see the blood spurt like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. In that case, quickly place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger.  If the bleeding stops, which often happens, tighten the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it.  Let it be on a pillow, and do not move it for days until healed.  If the bleeding does not stop and you are overcome, and no remedy is available to you at the time, sever the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract, and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, take one half, and tightly bind it to the vein with bandages until the following day.  If the bleeding stops, do so.  Otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned application of cauterizing agents to stop the bleeding, and in most cases, severing the blood vessel is not difficult.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the secure binding of the bandage to the arm, know this.  As for the black vein, the benefit of its incision is to draw blood from the top of the head and the bottom of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we said.
The phlebotomist should be careful, for beneath the vein are nerves.  If he increases the incision of the lancet and strikes a nerve, numbness will occur that is difficult to see, and it may not heal at all.  This nerve is often noticeable to the touch.
If in some people it is hidden and delicate, not readily apparent, then your incision should be made with a gentle pulling motion, avoiding the nerve as much as possible. If the vein lies between two nerves, incise the vein lengthwise.  As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein especially, if you wish to incise it, use the olive-shaped or the wide, rue-shaped lancet, as it is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when performing the phlebotomy, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft area.  If the vein is not struck on the first attempt, it is not harmful to repeat the incision several times.  However, it may swell in some people if not struck on the first attempt.
But that swelling will cause no harm.  As for the method of phlebotomy, its symptoms, and what needs to be done for its repair, first of all, you must know that phlebotomy is used to maintain health.
Or it is used for illnesses.  The type used to maintain health and prevent illness should be in one of the three veins of the elbow, namely the cephalic, the acubal, and the basilic.  Phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy should be performed on Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours of daylight have passed.  The phlebotomy used for illnesses does not have a limited time; it is done whenever need and necessity demand, whether by night or by day.
At all hours and times.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should old men who have passed sixty years.  If anyone intends to perform phlebotomy for any reason...
Before phlebotomy, he should cleanse his bowels with a gentle enema if there is much accumulated mucus, so that foul matter from the intestines will not be drawn into the veins during phlebotomy, harming the vital organs.  The feverish, the drunk, and the intoxicated should not be bled until these conditions have passed.
Phlebotomy should also be avoided following dysentery, vomiting, childbirth, excessive sexual activity, exertion, athletic activity, late nights, and fasting, and any physical or mental exertion that weakens the body.  Then, one should consider thinning the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
Through foods, drinks, and medications if possible, then he should enter a bath if nothing prevents it, or perform some light exercise to thin the blood.  Let his phlebotomy be in the middle of the day, as we have said, and he should clear his mind of all bad thoughts that day.
He should avoid worries, anger, fear, and all physical ailments such as excessive fatigue, exertion, and sexual activity, and so forth.  He should have present the things which people commonly use for their enjoyment, such as perfumes, flowers, amusements, and the like, as much as possible for each individual.
Then the phlebotomist should sit on a higher cushion than the patient, then extend the arm and rub it twice or thrice with his hand, then tighten the bandage with the ligature, twisting it twice or thrice, maintaining a moderate tension.
Because if the tension is not moderate, either excessive tightening will stop the flow of blood, or too much looseness will also prevent the flow of blood.  Then, after tightening, it is necessary for the patient to rub both of his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then the phlebotomist should wipe the lancet with a little bit of old oil, especially, and then place his left index finger on the same vein slightly below the point where he intends to phlebotomize, so that the vein will not move and avoid the strike. For some veins resemble tendons, moving when lanced, and some are filled with air, so when the lancet is placed on them, they recede under the lancet, deceiving the phlebotomist and failing to open the vein, and if it does open, the opening is narrow.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be careful and deliberate in all these matters, and then lower the lancet; if the vein opens at that spot, well and good.  Otherwise, he should return to it again slightly below or above that spot, quickly if the area has not swelled.
If the patient swells or trembles, leave it a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it may bring about a hot swelling.  He should not enter a bath, and then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes.  If the lancet was inserted and the opening was small and the blood flow was thin.
And fearing that the desired amount of blood may not be drawn, reinsert the lancet in the same puncture, gently and straight, and slightly enlarge the opening.  Do this quickly, before the area swells, for in many people, the area may swell when the opening is small.
If you see that it has swollen, do not return to it at all; it will not help you. Apply some clarified oil to it; it facilitates blood flow and is better in this location than oil itself or any other ointments.
Use clarified oil in all your phlebotomies when blood flow is difficult.  This remedy, and the *shukhzania*, may also achieve this if applied to the area.  The blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the phlebotomy, which often happens, especially for someone who has not been bled before, or if the vein opening was small, act quickly. Apply a sponge soaked in warm salt water to the swelling and compress it for an hour. It will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood has completely flowed from the vein itself or from another vein. If any discoloration, black or green, remains in the area after a few days, this does not harm. If desired, apply a poultice of bruised and dissolved myrrh.
Or something from the juice of *fudunj* and the like.  A swelling and a protuberance often occur when bleeding basil.  Place your hand on it. If you feel it throbbing when you prod it, it is a bad protuberance. Beware of applying any of the mentioned remedies to it.
For it may bleed arterial blood.  Bandage it with something constricting to harden the area, then treat it with other remedies until healed.  Each person's blood extraction should be according to their strength and the predominant color of their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red.  Also, if you see it thick, allow it to thin out.  Likewise, if it is sharp, let its sharpness diminish.  For those who are strong and full and require a single bloodletting, this is appropriate.
For the strong, widen the vein incision and use a broad lancet. For the weak, do the opposite.  The blood should be drawn in multiple stages.  The puncture should be narrow.  The best tool for vein incision is a slanted, oblique blade, not a piercing one.
This type of bleeding is free from bleeding and nerve damage.  It is better and safer than transverse or longitudinal bleeding.  If the patient faints during bleeding, it is necessary to give them some bread soaked in pomegranate juice or rosewater before the procedure.
If he is feverish and the blood is drawn in three or four stages, if he has a cool temperament, let him eat bread soaked in pomegranate juice, or honey-based syrup infused with spices, or a fragrant herbal drink before the procedure.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and it is caused by excessive blood loss, then the patient should be given meat broth and a thin herbal drink.  Fragrant essence should be used to perfume the chest, and all the remedies mentioned in the section on fainting should be applied.
If the bloodletting is for vomiting, and if someone wants to further relieve the arm and draw more blood, for someone whose bloodletting was for excessive vomiting and whose strength is weak, one should release the blood a little at a time, according to the strength, over several days.
If someone wants to relieve their arm and draw blood again, and if their body is strong, then they should do it after seven or nine hours from the first bloodletting.  If one wants to draw blood from the body to the opposite side from which it has collected, then this should be done on the second or third day.
If a person has a lot of blood in their body that has become hot and intense, causing a fever, then the blood should be drawn in a single, large quantity, widening the opening until fainting occurs, after ensuring all bloodletting prerequisites have been met. And place your hand on his pulse while the blood flows, lest death occurs instead of fainting, for this often happens if the bloodletter is ignorant and careless.  This should not be done if you intend to release the arm and draw blood again.
If the vein opening closes and the blood flow becomes difficult, do not forcefully poke or twist it, for that is very bad.  Rather, either leave it until you can open it again, or use the lancet blade to remove any clotted blood from the vein opening.
Or apply something dissolved in water, like salt, or a potent antidote like faruq or shakhznaya, and gently poke the vein until blood flows. If the vein has swollen, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If, despite this, there is a need to draw blood again, then either draw from the same spot above the swelling, or from the other arm, or another vein.
For drawing blood from the vein in the upper arm, use it in place of the akhal and basaliq if those are not available or hidden, because it combines their properties.  To perform the procedure, the patient should put their hand into hot water until their forearm is red and the vein clearly visible.  Then, gently but firmly bind the area with a bandage.
Then, draw blood from the vein slightly off-center, neither horizontally nor vertically.  Make a wide opening, and puncture slightly above the wrist joint. If the blood flow is difficult, put the arm back in the hot water, and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Until you have obtained the desired amount. If it is summer, you may not need to return the arm to the hot water.  The use of hot water is mainly needed during winter to facilitate blood flow.  Drawing from this vein is safer than from any other vein. Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it.  Drawing blood from the asylem vein in the right arm is beneficial for liver ailments.  To do this, bind the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after having immersed the arm in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it.  Drawing blood from the asylem vein in the right arm is beneficial for liver ailments.  To do this, bind the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after having immersed the arm in hot water until the vein swells. The vein will be very apparent.  Then, puncture the vein slightly off-center.  If you sever it completely, it will not harm him.  Be cautious; do not use the lancet too forcefully.  Beneath the vein is the nerve for the fingers, and the area is exposed flesh. Then return the arm to the hot water.
Leave it to flow.  If you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein, and stop flowing.  Once you have drawn the required amount of blood, apply a mixture of oil and salt to the vein to prevent rapid clotting.
Likewise, you should do this with every narrow vein.  Drawing blood from the left arm is beneficial for spleen ailments.  Do the same procedure as you did with the second vein.  As for drawing from the saphenous vein, it is beneficial for illnesses in the lower body, such as problems with the uterus.
And for menstrual retention, kidney ailments, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.  The procedure for drawing blood from this vein is to immerse the leg in hot water and massage it until the veins swell.  Then, bind above the joint of the leg with a bandage. The vein is located near the heel, visible towards the big toe.
Many branches extend from it on the side of the leg.  Draw blood from the widest branch or near the heel, where the branches meet. This is the best and safest approach. If you draw blood on the side of the leg, be careful of the nerves beneath the skin on the side of the leg.
Make your incision with a twisting motion, as if you were trying to sever it, and the lancet should be sharp. If the blood is difficult to draw, return the leg to hot water and let the blood flow until it is empty.  If the surgeon misses the vein on the first attempt.
Then, move the incision slightly upwards.  The area is safe, and there is no danger if you are careful about the nerve, as we have mentioned.  Do the same for the saphenous vein on the other leg.  As for the vein of the woman, its location is, as we stated, at the heel on the outer side.
Its use is for hip pain if it is caused by hot blood.  The method for drawing blood is to have the patient enter a bath and quickly bind their leg from the hip to above the heel with a thin, long bandage.  Only then will it appear.
If it appears, draw blood, in any case.  Either by twisting (which is best), or cutting off a piece, or by slicing it. The area is safe. In most people, it is very hidden; if you do not find it.
If it does not appear at all to the touch, draw blood from some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot near the little and ring fingers.  Be mindful of the nerves and draw the amount of blood desired.
Then release the bandage. Place a cotton pad on the incision site and bandage the area.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Cupping and its Application.
Cupping vessels can be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping can be done in two ways: one is cupping with a ligature, drawing out blood; the other is cupping without a ligature.
This cupping without a ligature is also twofold.
The cupping vessels of the sides of the neck, both sides, the chin cupping under the lower jaw of the mouth, the cupping of the shoulders, the cupping of the coccyx on the tailbone, the cupping of the forearms in the middle of the arms, the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the thin veins dispersed in the flesh, and for this reason it does not weaken the strength as much as venesection.  Cupping, whether by fire or otherwise, should not be used for any illness caused by fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If cupping is needed, either for illness or for habitual use, we employ it at any time, at the beginning, middle, and end of the month.
For some, when there is an excess of blood requiring cupping, they experience heaviness and headaches in their heads; others experience fullness, redness in their face, head, and neck; some experience itching in their face, temples, and dark circles and swelling around their eyes; and others itch the site of their cupping.
Some laugh excessively; some taste blood in their mouths, and their gums bleed; some spit up blood; some sleep excessively; some see blood, redness, battles, and wounds, and the like, in their dreams.  Whenever we observe any of this,
Especially if it occurs in the middle third of the month, we then prescribe cupping after two or three hours have passed of the day.  The benefit of cupping (at that time) is for heaviness of the head and watery discharge from the eyes.
However, this should be done after the whole body has been emptied. This cupping can be a substitute for phlebotomy of the temples.  Caution should be taken not to use it on those with a cold head or cold, as it will greatly harm them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold conditions in their head.  Those who habitually use it may suffer forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cupping vessel slightly, for fear of inducing forgetfulness.  As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the temples and the vein on the scalp.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, disjunction of the breathing apparatus, coughing and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be raised slightly, for if it is positioned too low, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for pains in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, choking, and toothache.  It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the scalp.  The cupper should be instructed not to insert the cupping vessel too deeply into the skin, lest a vessel be cut and excessive bleeding result.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments.  It can take the place of venesection of the vessels in the lips.  As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the hollows of the forearms, it is beneficial for what is beneficial in the venesection of the three veins: the scalp vein, the temple vein, and the vein on the neck.  Because it draws blood from all the thin veins in the flesh and draws those thin veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press too deeply into the skin, for the area is lacking flesh and has nerves and blood vessels beneath.  As for a single cupping vessel applied to the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower rectum.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, copper cupping vessel, for the area requires strong suction and glass cupping vessels might break.  A great deal of pressure is needed.  As for cupping the legs, it clearly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body and benefits chronic ailments of the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, it induces menstruation, benefits boils and abscesses, and serves as a substitute for venesection of the saphenous and popliteal veins. However, it greatly exhausts the body and causes fainting in most people. Cupping the popliteal veins is similar in benefit to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place the empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction. Do not prolong the application of cupping, but quickly place and quickly remove the cups to allow the humors to flow to the area evenly.  Continue repeating this until the area reddens and opens.
When the redness of blood appears, then apply pressure and resume suction gradually.  Then observe the condition of the bodies.  For those with loose, porous flesh, a single pressure should be applied, lest the area become ulcerated.
The cupper should widen the pressure, deepening it slightly, and adjust the suction with gentle movement.  If the blood is thick, it should be pressed twice. In the first instance, to open a path for the thin, watery blood; and in the second, to completely remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very opaque, the pressure should be repeated a third time to achieve the desired outcome. In general, if we wish to remove a small amount of blood, a single pressure suffices; if we want to remove a large amount, we apply more pressure.  If we judge the blood to be thick.
A deep pressure should be applied, the moderate limit being the depth of the skin.  What ointments and waters should be used when applying cupping, and what the cupper should avoid?  As for those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores...
The areas for cupping should be anointed with softening, dissolving, opening ointments.  In summer, for example, ointments of frankincense, violet, sweet almond, or pumpkin seed.
In winter, for example, ointments of narcissus, lily, chamomile, or similar hyacinth. If the impurities are thick and cold, then use ointments of marjoram, rue, benzoin, or dill, and the like.
If the cupper has wide pores and soft flesh, then ointment should be avoided.  These people should wash their cupping vessels after cupping with rosewater, cold water, elderberry water, pumpkin water, or similar, and as for those whose blood is excessively moist...
They should wash their cupping vessels with vinegar, or with rue and sumac water, and the like.  As for those with thick impurities, they should wash their cupping vessels with old wine, or marjoram water, or dill or chamomile broth, and the like.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath and immediately after the bath.
Rather, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the leeches should consider before and after cupping.
First, one must consider if the cupper or leech is bilious, and if their blood is predominantly hot and inflamed, then they should take cooling foods such as pomegranate, dandelion, vinegar, lettuce, sour wine, and the like.  Their food should consist of chicken, lamb, and other similar lean meats.
If one's temperament is cold, they should be given honeyed drinks, or quince drinks, or basil sour wine.  They should take medium-aged, fragrant wine, and be instructed to eat sparingly.  Their food should consist of chicken, quail, sparrows, and doves.  The drink should be more than the food on the day of cupping and bloodletting.
Sometimes, certain people may be given a dose of the Faruq antidote, musk medicine, or sheeltha before cupping and bloodletting, or afterwards, to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood.  However, those who are feverish should not be given these.
As for cupping without any specific condition, these are cupping applications to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidneys, and hip joints, because these organs cannot tolerate specific conditions.  The goal is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping vessel on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or to remove a cold wind that has affected an organ, as when placing a cupping vessel on the abdomen and navel; this loosens the organ, warms it, and relieves the pain by dissolving that wind.
Cupping can also be applied to the kidneys if a blockage or stones are present, as its strong drawing power may open the blockage or dislodge the stones.  Similarly, it can be used on the liver and spleen when wind affects them.  These cupping vessels may be used empty, by suction only.
Cupping may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water for conditions like sores.  This involves filling a large cupping vessel with hot water alone, or water in which some suitable herbs have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed.  This is the method of cupping using fire.
Its opening is the width of two open fingers, as depicted, and its depth is half a span.  A small hole, just large enough for a needle to pass through, is situated near the middle on the side. It is made from thick-rimmed Chinese or yellow brass.
It is smooth, flat, and polished to avoid injuring the organ when placed on it.  A cross-shaped piece of brass or iron is in the center, where the candle is placed in the fire.  Such a cupping vessel can be made large or smaller than described, according to the illness.
The appropriate cupping vessel depends on the user.  Cupping vessels for children and thin individuals are different from those for men and those with larger bodies.  The method for applying fire to the affected area is to light a tightly wound cotton wick or small pitch candle, and place it on the central rod within the cupping vessel.
So that the fire rises from the top towards the bottom of the cupping vessel to prevent burning the patient's body. Then it is placed on the affected area, and the finger is placed on the hole we mentioned, so that when holding the cupping vessel, the finger does not need to be removed.
Steam will emerge from that hole, and the cupping vessel will adhere to the location. Then the wick is re-lit as needed.  However, the cupping vessel used for water-based scabies does not have a fixed rod or a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it directly on the affected area.  This is its image/form.
The larger the cupping vessel, the better, as it can hold more water.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On the application of leeches.
Leeches are used mostly on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either due to their small size, such as the lip, gums, and the like, or because the area is exposed without flesh, such as the finger and nose, and the like.
To use them, select leeches from clean fresh water, free from impurities, and leave them in fresh water for a day and a night so they will be hungry and have nothing in their stomachs. Then the body is first evacuated by bloodletting or cupping, and then the affected area is cleansed.
Until it reddens, then a leech is placed upon it.  If it becomes full, it falls off.  If the affected area can be cupped, this is more beneficial.  Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze it.  If bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and it is a oozing, then soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the oozing stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or astringent or similar coagulants on the area until the bleeding stops, or place peeled halves of chickpeas on the area.
Leave them until the chickpeas adhere to the area; then the bleeding will stop.  If leeches need to be reapplied, if possible, do not use the same leeches; if leeches refuse to attach, then clean the area with fresh blood.
Or pierce the area with a needle until some blood comes out, then place [a leech] there.  If it feels some blood, it will attach to the spot. If you want it to fall off, sprinkle some sorrel, salt, or ash on it, and it will fall off.
Chapter Three.
On Fractures.
This chapter also, from the subtleties of the medical art, is concerned with the setting of fractures and dislocations of the bones.
Know, my children, that many physicians and laypeople, those who have never reviewed the writings of the ancients on this topic or read a single letter from them, claim expertise in this chapter.
For this reason, this field of knowledge has become nonexistent in our land, and I have never encountered a practitioner who excels in it. Rather, I have gained what I have gained through prolonged study of the books of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, so that I extracted knowledge from them.  Then, I devoted my entire life to practice and skill.
I have depicted for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. My experience has been applied to it, after I brought it closer to you, purged it of unnecessary detail, and made it extremely concise, elucidated it fully, and presented numerous images of the instruments used in it.
This is an addition to the explanation, as I did in the previous two chapters.  There is no strength except from God, the Most High, the Greatest.
Chapter One.
It contains summaries and essential points concerning the fracture of bones.  Before we begin to describe the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary to mention some general statements and chapters at the beginning of this chapter. This will first compel you, and anyone else eager to learn this noble craft, to understand and grasp its truth.
If a person suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one should first apply treatment to set the fracture or reduce the dislocation, or both. Unless there is a hindrance such as weakness of the body, or the person affected is a child or very old person, or the time is exceptionally hot or cold.
Then, his diet should be restricted to cold legumes, poultry, and venison. He should avoid drinking, fatty meats, overeating, and any food that causes blood to flood the veins until the swelling subsides and there is no further anticipation of material leaking into the area.
Then the patient should return to his original regimen, which he habitually followed. If the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should eat foods that are nutritious, thick, strong, and viscous, such as lentils, rice, heads of grain, and the ribs and bellies of cattle. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and similar foods, will accelerate and improve the healing of the fracture.  Know that fractures in strong men and elderly people will never completely reconnect and heal to their original state.
Because of the dryness and hardness of their bones, those in the most flexible state, like the bones of young children, may reconnect. However, nature produces a substance resembling glue, thick and adhesive, around the fractured bone from all sides, to hold the bone fragments together firmly.
It binds them together until they reach the same strength and security they originally had, so nothing interferes with their function.
Therefore, the patient's diet should consist of strong, viscous, and thick foods, as previously mentioned.  Know that fractures vary according to the affected body part, as a fracture in the leg differs from one in the head, and a fracture in the chest differs from one in the spine.
Similarly, all other parts differ from one another. We will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its respective section, and the type of fracture may also differ within the same bone.
For the fracture might be a simple break without splinters, or it might be a lengthwise fracture with splinters, detached or not detached, and there may be a fracture with a wound and a tear in the skin, or a minor crack.
Each type requires a specific method for its repair, as will be explained in detail in its place.  Signs of a fracture include the bone's crookedness, its unevenness, its visibility to touch, and its grating sound when pressed with your hand.
If there is no visible distortion, no grating, no perceptible disturbance when palpating the bone, and the patient does not experience much pain, then it is not a fracture, but possibly a contusion, a minor fracture, or a minor crack.  The injured part should not be moved or prodded at all.
Instead, apply the medicines to be described later, suitable for the affected area, then apply a gentle bandage.  And know that when a bone is fractured and splintered into two pieces, though no splinters are formed, every part has been displaced from its companion.
You should immediately attempt to reposition and align it before a hot swelling develops.  If a hot swelling does appear, leave it for a few days until the swelling subsides, then realign it by any gentle and skillful means possible.
Know that setting and aligning a bone is easier when no splinters are present.  Then, it is bandaged as will be described.  However, if there are splinters, the fractured limb (whether an arm or a leg) must be stretched from both sides.
Either by your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both ropes and hands.  Place the limb on a flat surface in its natural position.  When the fractured bone parts have been pulled apart, carefully and gently return the protrusions to their proper places.
Strive to cause no pain or discomfort to the patient through your actions.  Do your utmost to reunite the two bone fragments in the best possible alignment.  At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hand.  If you perceive anything out of place, correct and realign it to the best of your ability.
Avoid excessive stretching and forceful pulling, as many unskilled practitioners do.  Often, their actions cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have repeatedly observed.
After the alignment, meticulous work, and bandaging, keep the limb still and at rest. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness, sleep, turning, restlessness, bowel movements, and all other activities to the best of their ability.  Ensure that the limb's position provides pain-free support.
If, while maintaining that position, the patient feels pain or discomfort, he should shift to a different position in which he experiences no pain.  He should ensure that this new position is straight and aligned.
To prevent the limb from becoming deformed if it is set, and regarding how to bandage a fractured limb, I am describing it as follows:  Know that fractured limbs vary in size, condition, and structure.  Smaller limbs like the arm, finger, forearm, and similar, ...
The bandages should be gentle and light for smaller limbs like the arm or finger.  For larger limbs, such as the thigh, back, or chest, the bandages should be firm and substantial, since a wide bandage is required to support and firmly bind the larger limb on all sides equally without gaps.
Then, after completing the alignment, apply the appropriate plaster. Apply a soft bandage to the fracture site.  Then, bandage the fracture site three or four times, depending on the limb's requirements. Gently tighten the bandage and move it up towards the upper portion of the fracture.
Apply less tension than you applied to the fractured area. Gradually increase the distance between the bandages and the fracture site, easing the tension slightly until a satisfactory position is achieved. Then, take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap further, then move the bandage down towards the lower part of the fracture.  Maintain the tension and looseness of the bandages as described for the initial (upper) wrapping.  Place padding, such as soft bandages or cloth, between the layers of bandages to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, correct it. Otherwise, do not add anything. Then wrap another bandage over this.  Apply firm splints over these layers, if there is no swelling or inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to relieve the swelling and reduce the puffiness. Leave it for a few days, then apply the splints. The splints should be made from broad, skillfully-cut pieces of cane or from the wood of sieves.
Made from pine, palm leaves, reeds, or other similar readily available materials.  The size of each splint should be precisely the same.
However, the splint placed directly over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The length of the splint should be appropriate to the size of the fractured limb.  Image of the splint:
Then, wrap the splints with another bandage, using the same tightness as the first layer.  Secure from above with firm stitches, ensuring the tightening is more pronounced at the fractured area. The tighter the wrapping, the closer to the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and not too thin, preferably made of damp linen.  If the threads are thick, like those used by ignorant practitioners, made from twisted linen strips, this is a grave error.
Because the tension will be uneven.  Thin threads are also unsuitable, as they will not allow the desired tension.  There should be at least one finger's width between the splints.
If the patient is injured by the edges of the splints after proper tightening, place soft padding or fluffy wool under them to avoid further injury.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin is broken, we will address that separately.
Know that not every fractured limb needs splinting from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five, seven, or more days later, depending on the risk of inflammation.
This is the description of the dressings used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It's a general, concise dressing that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is not affected by either heat or cold.
Take the flour dust, the fine particles that cling to the millstones during grinding. Knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white.  Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then bake it.
Another dressing for fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  Take ten dirhams each of mastic, ladanum, acacia, raisin, and mugwort, five dirhams each of myrrh and mastic tree, and twenty dirhams of Armenian/Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients and mix them with acacia water, or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  Use this dressing, as it is well-combined; it quickly sets broken bones and is suitable for most people due to its balance.
Another dressing useful for fractures and sprains: take ten dirhams each of mugwort, mastic, and white hellebore, five dirhams each of myrrh and mastic tree, six dirhams of acacia, and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all thoroughly, sift, knead with water or egg white, and apply.
A dressing for joints and displaced bones, relieving pain and bone fractures and fissures.  Take prepared wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply to the affected area.  This dressing does not have the strength of a binding agent.
However, it excels in relieving hot swelling and pain. Another dressing for broken bones: take leaves of the fig tree and wild poppy, grind them both together, and apply as a poultice. Another concise dressing is used when a broken bone is displaced.
To analyze the remaining swelling, take parts of hellebore root, chamomile, violet, and quince flour, grind all together, and knead with a paste. If the limb is not inflamed, knead with the paste. If inflamed, knead with coriander water or water, and apply.
Another stronger dressing for resolving the swelling that occurs when a bone is displaced: take equal parts of hellebore root, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, violet, and chamomile.
Grind all together and knead with the water of a certain plant, or with fresh water, or with a paste, according to the heat of the limb and the subsidence of its heat.  Hippocrates did not mention in his book that it should be applied to a fractured limb during its reduction.
Except for the plaster made from wax and oil, nothing else. He described it to be medium thickness, neither thick nor thin.  Galen believed that things with dryness and some heat, like myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, should be applied to the injured limb during its reduction.
A dressing for weakness and pain: Take equal parts of mugwort, roasted chickpeas, shaved human hair or bird feathers, hellebore, and salt. Grind, sieve, knead, and apply as a dressing.  And how much of the ligament should remain...
Then, consider: if the injured person experiences no pain, no rubbing, and no displacement of the broken bone, do not remove it for many days.  However, if there is severe rubbing, troublesome pain, or swelling in the affected area, remove it.
Immediately remove it and do not delay. Remove the dressing. Then, take a soft cloth or a damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the rubbing subsides and the pain lessens.  Then, allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then, apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bandage it overnight, to alleviate inflammation, swelling of the limb, and pain.  Then, return to a gentle bandage and light dressing. Do not apply the initial tight bandage.
Care for it until it heals.  If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and swelling have completely subsided, and the dressing and bandage are needed, reapply them as you did at first,  in the same manner. If nothing of what we mentioned occurs to the limb, [do nothing further].
Do not remove it until three, four, five, or seven days, or even twenty days; all according to the condition of the limb, as we said, until the fracture is firmly set and the flesh around it has healed.
Then, increase the bandage's tightness even further than the initial application.  Also increase the patient's nourishment, as previously mentioned. If you see the fracture site has dried out and wasted more than it should, know that the nourishment is not reaching it.
Unwrap it with lukewarm water each time you remove it, every three days.  Ease up on the bandage slightly.  By doing this, nourishment flows to the limb and it heals quickly.  As for what the ignorant practitioners of fracture setting do, to break the limb again, ...
If it is not set properly the first time, and is set crookedly, it is an error on their part and a great deception. Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and employed it. I have seen no trace of this in any of them. The correct approach is not to use it.
Chapter Two.
On fractures of the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their shapes varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and they may be such that the entire bone is fractured down to the peritoneum beneath the bone. As is done to wood by the carpenter.
In this way, this type of fracture is called a carpenter's fracture; or the sword may have cut only a part of the bone and fractured only one surface, not penetrating through it completely; this type of fracture is called a simple fracture. The wound from either of these fractures may be large or small.
Some fractures are caused by contusion or impact, such as a blow from a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar.  This fracture may be penetrating, having approached the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be superficial, on the surface of the bone.
The wounds from these fractures also may be large or small.  Some fractures are hidden within the bone, subtle like a hairline crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  Some are caused by falls or blows from stones or similar objects, where a part of the bone surface penetrates inward, creating a depression, like when a blow damages copper and a part of it sinks in.
This is most common in the soft, bony skulls of children.  All these types of fractures may have displaced or undisplaced fragments.  We will describe the treatment of each in its place.  All these types of fractures can be identified by examination.
Examining them with probes and removing putrefied flesh from them.  The hairline fracture is identified by examining the bone, wiping and staining it with ink.  The fracture appears black.  Treatment of the fracture first considers the patient's symptoms.
If you observe clear signs of danger, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bloodshot eyes, redness, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat them.  In most cases, death is inevitable with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for his recovery, then proceed with treatment.  If the injured person comes to you immediately after the injury, and this is during the winter months, ...
In any case, you should diligently remove the bone before the 14th day.  If it is during the summer months, you should quickly remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane beneath the bone rots, causing the symptoms we described.
If the fractured bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is a crush and injury, you should cut away the crushed and injured part, as I describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the shape of the wound and what is tolerable for the patient.  If during the exposure of the bone, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, address that accordingly.
Fill the area with rags soaked in a mixture of potion, rose oil, and astringent.  Clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops.  Then begin to manipulate and remove the bone.  This can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a narrow, delicate incision.  This is its depiction:
Then, use another incision, slightly wider than the first.  This is its depiction:
Then, use yet another incision, wider still than the second.  It is necessary to have a set of various incisions, some wider than others, some shorter than others, with very sharp edges, made of Indian steel or good quality steel.
Use gentleness when striking the incision, so as not to dislodge the head and injure it.  If the bone is strong and hard, it is advisable to bore around it before using the incisions, with awls called "awls not submerged.
Because they do not exceed the limit of the skull bone, going beyond it.  The awl has a rounded edge below its sharp head, resembling a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should prepare a large number of these awls, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that you have, for each skull, an awl whose sharp tip is appropriate in length and shortness for the thickness of that skull.  Here are the images of three types. Of awls, large, medium, and small.
To pierce around the fractured bone, place the awl on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been pierced. Then, move the awl to another location, leaving a distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a rod, or similar.
Then, with the forceps, cut between each pair of holes, using as much gentleness as possible, as we said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or with another tool that you have prepared for that purpose.
Such as fine splints and forceps.  You must be extremely careful that the awl or forceps do not touch the peritoneum. If you have straightened the bone and freed it from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, and removed it, then it must be cleaned.
And smooth out any roughness remaining on the bone with another instrument similar to the forceps, but it must be finer and gentler than the other forceps.  If any small pieces or fragments of bone remain, remove them gently with the tools you have.
Then, treat the wound afterwards with bandages and ointments, which will be described later.  The other approach to this work is exceptionally simple and free from risk.  Galen described it and praised it highly.  This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lenticular forceps beneath it.  This is its image:
The lenticular part of it should be smooth, so as not to cut anything, and the sharp part should have its long edges as you see.  The lenticular part should rest against the peritoneum, and the sharp edge of the forceps should be on the bone.  Then strike the forceps from one side.
With a small hammer, so as to break all the bone gently, as it turns, and you are safe from injuring the membrane, with no terrible accident happening at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were asleep.
If any membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular forceps itself, and remove it gently.  It will come off without injury or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not penetrate the membrane and the broken part of the bone was rough and had splinters, then you should remove that roughness and those splinters with small rasps of varying sizes, so that you can use the most appropriate one for each place, according to what the work requires.
With the broken bone and its shape, you should begin by using the widest rasps for cleaning the bone, then use narrower ones, and continue this way until you use the smallest and finest of them all.
As for the smaller, hairline fractures and minor breaks, you should apply treatment to each according to its requirements.  This is something not hidden from those who have the slightest skill in this craft and have studied what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the wound's opening.  Then take another cloth, folded or triangular. Soak it in rosewater and rose oil, place it on top of the first cloth, and do this gently so as not to weigh down the peritoneum.  Then apply a wide bandage over it, but only tighten it enough to hold the wound closed.
Leave it with this treatment for one or two days until the hot swelling subsides.  Then remove it and use afterwards the drying medications such as iris root, carob flour, kandar, and zarawand, and similar substances.  Make these into powders.
Dust the wound with these dry powders.  In general, use a medicine that cleanses without stinging.  Make every effort during the treatment of the wound to keep it clean, free of grease, dirt, or pus; never allow pus to accumulate in it.
Because if pus collects on the meninges, it corrupts and putrifies them, causing a great affliction to the patient.  This can occur in the skull's peritoneum when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
A dark discoloration on its surface.  If the patient displays the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is doomed.  If the discoloration occurred due to a medication applied, and the medication's strength was such that it caused this discoloration.
Then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, thoroughly mix them, and apply the mixture to a cloth. Place the cloth on the peritoneum and continue treating it with the necessary remedies until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In setting a broken nose.
Know that only the upper nasal bones, either both or one, can break, for they are bones. The lower part is cartilaginous, and does not break, but can become bruised, bent, or deformed.
If one of the nasal bones is broken, you should insert the little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from the inside with your index and thumb from the outside, restoring the nose to its natural shape.  Be gentle and careful to avoid causing pain to the patient.
If the fracture is high up on the nose and cannot be reached by the finger, it should be straightened with a blunt, slightly thickened instrument. If the fracture is in both sides, do the same.  He should undertake the reduction on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise, after seven or ten days when the acute swelling subsides, insert a linen thread into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if the fracture is on both sides.
The thread should be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some earlier practitioners of fracture reduction recommended soaking the thread in ghee and changing it every day. I, however, do not find this necessary. Instead, the thread should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the thread until the bone is set and the cartilage is firm.  After wrapping soft linen around them, goose quill tubes can be inserted into the nostril where the thread is, making the nasal support more secure, and preventing the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not essential.  If you wish, you may do it, or if you wish, you may use the threads.  If during your work a hot swelling appears on the nose, bandage the nose with linen cloth or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or a diachylon plaster.
If no hot swelling appears, it is necessary to bandage it externally with semolina and crushed kandar (a spice) mixed with beaten egg white, then place a soft bandage on top.  Do not tie the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it is fragmented, it is necessary to split it open and remove it with a suitable instrument, then suture the split and treat it with healing and suppurative ointments appropriate for that. If a wound occurs inside the nose, it is necessary to treat it with thread and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Fourth Chapter.
On the setting of a fractured lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is fractured, and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, if the fracture is only from the outside and does not break into two pieces and does not curve inward, then its identification is easy.
If the fracture is in the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth.  Similarly, if the fracture is in the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted and gently lift the bump of the fracture from inside to outside.
With your other hand outside the bone, control its alignment.  If the jaw fracture has broken into two pieces, then traction must be applied from both sides in a straight line until alignment is achieved. If it has occurred in the teeth, it should be trimmed.
Or, separate what you expect to remain, securing it with a gold, silver, or silk thread.  Then place a splint on the fractured jaw, followed by a folded cloth, and finally place a large, tight bandage or a piece of leather the same length as the jaw over the cloth. Then, tie it from above in whatever way seems appropriate for its closure, so it does not dislocate.  Instruct the patient to rest and abstain from strenuous activity. Provide the patient with soft, digestible food. If you suspect a change in its condition in any way, reopen it on the third day.
Then, tie it from above in whatever way seems appropriate for its closure, so it does not dislocate.  Instruct the patient to rest and abstain from strenuous activity. Provide the patient with soft, digestible food. If you suspect a change in its condition in any way, reopen it on the third day. Then, mend what you bandaged with mill dust and egg white or semolina flour after removing the splint. Place a soft, supple cloth over the bandage. As long as the bandage adheres and the bone shows no change, leave it in place.
Do not reopen it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  This fracture often strengthens in three weeks.  If, during that time, a hot swelling appears, use the methods previously described to alleviate it until it subsides.
If the fracture is accompanied by an open wound, and if a piece or pieces of bone have become detached, then carefully remove these pieces with appropriate instruments. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a surgical saw as needed.
Then, if you have removed all the bone fragments, if the wound is wide, suture it; otherwise, apply a suitable ointment. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of a fractured clavicle.
The clavicle is most often fractured from the front, at the acromial end.  The fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it fractures and splinters into two pieces without splinters, which is easiest to set.
Or, the fracture may involve splinters, which is harder to set. Or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one should hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, the other should extend the neck to the opposite side.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers until it is correctly shaped, with neither a protrusion nor a deformity. If further traction is needed, place a ball of linen or wool under the patient's armpit.
Hold the bone as needed, and stretch and lift the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot draw the clavicle outward because it has sunk inward, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a medium-sized pillow under his shoulder.  The assistant should press down on his shoulder so that the deep-seated clavicle bone rises up.  Then correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, you should carefully remove it.  If the splinter is embedded in the bone, use one of the surgical instruments prepared for this purpose to carefully remove it. After placing the instrument that protects the diaphragm (an instrument made of wood or iron) under the clavicle, this is its image.
It resembles a spoon, without any embellishments. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, and its length should also be according to what you need for the work.  It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you made when removing the bone splinter is wide and you fear inflammation, then close the edges of the wound with sutures.  If the opening is small or you fear inflammation, then close the wound with stitches and bandages proportionate to the wound's size.
If a hot inflammation appears, soak the bandages in rose oil and vinegar or potion and apply them.  As for strengthening the bone, if there is no wound or incision, apply a bandage made from mill dust and egg white to the bone. Place a soft splint on it, then place a ball under his armpit if needed.  Then take a very long turban, its width about a span or so, and apply a folded bandage over the splint and dressing.  Then take a splint made of thin wood. Its width should be three fingers and its length likewise. Then place it in a cloth, then place that cloth with the splint in the part of the turban that lies over the fracture.  Then tightly wrap the turban around the fracture as you would wrap a turban. And wrap it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, and repeat it several times on each side.  How you see that the tension secures the fracture firmly, is something that is clear to you.
The whole purpose is that the splint must not move from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligaments relax and you see the splint has shifted, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then ensure the patient sleeps on their back and place a small pillow under their armpit at night.
To raise his arm away from his side so that the fractured collarbone is raised with the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs at the site, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Apply pressure and leave it until the fractured collarbone heals and becomes firm.  It will strengthen and become more solid within twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the setting of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures at its widest point, but rather at its projections.  Therefore, whenever a portion is fractured, or if it is fractured mid-shoulder, this can only be discerned by palpation.  Then, based on the nature of the fracture, apply a splint.
Reduce it [the fracture] to its natural shape by every possible means, then apply to the area ground-up millstone dust, egg white, and soft linen. Place over this a bandage made of folded cloth and then apply a wide splint made of wood.
Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder, or slightly wider.  If there is a protrusion under the splint in any part of the shoulder, smooth this protrusion with soft linen until the splint lies flat.  Then bind it from above with a long bandage.
Tighten the binding and secure the splint so that it does not shift.  Inspect the bandage each day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it shifts.  The patient should lie on their healthy side.  The shoulder heals in twenty days.
(One) or twenty-five days. When this period is complete, remove the bandage; you are safe.  For it is one of those bones where one does not fear its splintering or displacement.  If a fragment of bone protrudes from the skin and is irritating beneath the skin, make an incision and remove it. Do what was mentioned regarding the treatment of a swollen clavicle if such a thing occurs.
Chapter Seven.
In the setting of a fractured rib.
The ribs can fracture in the middle, but this is infrequent.  The ends are more prone to fracture and fragmentation.  Symptoms of a fractured midsection include tilting downwards, severe pain, and difficulty breathing and coughing.
There may also be expectorated blood, and the depression in the fractured bone is evident and palpable.  Treatment involves the patient lying on their back, with a pillow placed between their shoulders.  Then, the patient's sides are pressed down, and the ribs are brought together with the hands from the sides.  The fractured area should be gently aligned in all possible directions.
Maintain this alignment until the bone returns to its proper shape, then apply a bandage and splints. Place a splint made of thin wood or similar lightweight material such as poplar or willow over this, wrapped in cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone to prevent displacement.  Wrap the bandage around the back in a circular motion several times, tightening it firmly.  Regularly check the bandage, and tighten it whenever it loosens.
If the situation requires removing the splint due to inflammation, pain, or swelling at the site, immediately remove it and the bandage, and address any issues with appropriate treatment.  If appropriate, reapply the bandage. And apply pressure and maintain the dressing until healing occurs.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures typically occur in the thick portions near the spine and their anterior extremities.  This is because they are cartilaginous, and their injury is easily discernible from feeling with fingers.
To set the fracture, align the broken pieces with the fingers, ensuring a proper shape.  Then bandage and apply a splint if needed to secure the fractured bone. If the rib fracture is angled inward, it causes the patient severe pain.
And the pain is like that of a person with a hernia, because the bone pierces the diaphragm, causing difficulty breathing, coughing, and profuse spitting of blood. This is a difficult condition to treat.  The ancients devised many methods for it.
Some have said that the patient should eat foods that produce gas and wind, to inflate the abdomen and push the fracture outward.  We discourage this, lest it worsen any inflammation.  If there is no inflammation, it will not cause it. If it already exists, it will increase and exacerbate it.
Some suggest applying a cupping device to the area, then forcefully drawing it.  This seems logical, though one worries that the cupping device might draw more fluid to the area due to its weakness. Others say the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The space between the ribs is filled, so that the ligament is smooth if wrapped around, then the patient is treated for a hernia, through diet and medicine.  If the patient is exhausted by the severe condition, he cannot bear it. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, we should incise the area, expose the fractured rib, then place the instrument that protects the peritoneum (as described previously) underneath, carefully cut the bone, and remove it.
Then we close the wound edges, if large, with sutures, and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If inflammation develops during this time, quickly apply poultices made from rose oil to the area, and treat the patient internally with something to reduce inflammation.
He should lie on the side where he sleeps more easily until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the spine and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if fractured, this is rare.  More often, there is bruising.  The same is true for the vertebrae of the spine. If this occurs to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not... Then look: if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he cannot move, extend, or clench them; and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels no sensation or pain, then know that in most cases, he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking, know that the marrow of the bone is intact, and the patient will recover with treatment. If the spine is injured in the same way, and you wish to know whether it will also recover or not...
Then look at his legs.  If you see that they are relaxed and the same condition as the hands has occurred, and then when he lies on his back, wind and stool come out involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily... And if he lies on his back and tries to urinate, he cannot.  Then know that he is doomed, so do not concern yourself with treating him. If none of this happens to him, the matter is less serious.  The treatment for what has occurred is to attempt to relieve the hot swelling.
By placing rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, on the affected vertebrae three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides.  Then apply one of the strengthening, dry dressings to the area and bind it with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should sleep only on the side where he feels no pain until he recovers. If a splinter or something similar has formed at the site of the injury, the skin should be cut open and the bone fragment removed. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with stitches and treat with healing ointments until healed. If the last bone of the coccyx is broken, then the little finger of the left hand should be inserted into the rectum.
Then, the broken bone should be aligned as much as possible with the other hand.  After alignment, apply a bandage and splint if necessary, and then bind it.
If you sense a broken splinter within, cut open the area and remove it. Treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a broken hip.
The bones of the hips rarely break. If they do, the break typically involves fragmentation at the ends, a longitudinal crack, and inward tilting. The patient experiences pain, pricking, and numbness in the affected leg.
To set it, run your hand over it until you grasp the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, adjust the ends to resemble their normal shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal or has tilted inward, lay the patient on his stomach so you can adjust the fracture. Once adjusted, apply a bandage and then a splint of wood or leather, securing it tightly.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or the splint coming loose.  Fill any hollows in the hips to create a smooth, even surface for the splint.  Tell the patient to sleep on their back or healthy side.  If a hot swelling develops, treat it.
Refrain from stretching or manipulating it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply appropriate remedies as described previously.  Then resume adjusting and securing the splint as needed. If fragments or splinters of bone are found at the ends, do not remove them.
Do not touch the affected area, but adjust it from the outside as previously mentioned.  Leave it secured until healed.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it using one of two methods. One method is to take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness, like this:
Tie ligaments to both ends of the stick, then suspend it from a high point.  The patient sits in a chair, and then places their broken arm over the stick, so that their armpit is positioned at the center of the stick's curve.  Then, hang a heavy object from above.
Or, an attendant stretches it downwards, and then the physician straightens the fracture with their hands until the fracture is restored properly. The other method is for the patient to lie on their back, and their arm is suspended from their neck by a bandage.
Then, order two attendants to hold, one with his hands, the part above the fracture, and the other, the part below it, and each stretches it towards their respective sides.  If you want the stretching to be stronger, tie a bandage below the fracture and another above, and each attendant pulls towards their side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the bandage should be placed under the armpit and the other one below the fracture, near the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the bandage should be placed at that location and at the elbow itself.
Then, gently straighten the fracture without force, until it is properly aligned and fits well. Then, tie it tight, unless a hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling appears, refrain from tightening it until the seventh day. Apply moistened cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil to it.
Until the swelling subsides.  Then, tighten it.  The method of tightening is to place the bandage on the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth around the bandage.  Then, bring the arm to the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
Hold the cloths and tightening on the upper arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes about the fractured bone.  If you fear that something may change about it, then use splints.
It is to place a splint on the fracture itself, one wider and stronger than the other splints.  Let there be a finger's width between each splint.  The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an additional three fingers on each side.
Then, tighten the splints with the tightening described in the first section.
It is that your tightening should be stronger at the fracture site.
And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see the application of splints and tightening as we have mentioned in the time of your setting of the limb, then do so.
And if you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.  Then examine the ligament every three days, lest a burning sensation, swelling, or the cessation of nourishment to the limb from excessive tightening occurs.  Then rectify all of that according to what we have mentioned.
If you are confident that none of that will happen, do not loosen the bandage until after many days. The patient should lie on their back, their hand on their stomach, and a padded, moderate-sized arm sling of wool should be placed under the arm.
