And examine at all times, night or day, lest the fractured limb's shape shifts or the bandage loosens. Correct all of this with your effort, and ensure the patient's diet is as we previously mentioned, that the food is initially gentle, until the bone is to solidify.
Then one should increase the strength of their diet, for it is customary for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days.  At that time, the bandage should be loosened, baths should be taken, and remedies applied for that purpose.  If the fracture is severe and extensive.
Do not remove the bandage until fifty days, or even two months.
Chapter Twelve.
In the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, and they are called radii. One is small, and it is the one next to the thumb, and the other is large, and it is positioned beneath the smaller one from below.  Thus, the larger radius might break alone, or the smaller radius might break alone, or they might both break.
If the upper small radius is fractured, its setting is easier and its healing is quicker.  If the lower radius is fractured, the fracture is bad, and healing is difficult.  The worst case is when both bones are fractured. If the upper small radius was fractured...
Then the physician must, in setting it, make the pulling gentle and slight to align it.  If the larger radius is fractured, the pulling should be stronger.  If both radii are fractured, the pulling must be very strong.
The hand should be positioned, during setting and pulling, extended on a cushion.  The thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers. The injured person should be seated.
The cushion should be at a height so as not to cause the patient any exertion.
Then, an assistant supports the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage, and another assistant supports it from above.  Then the physician aligns the bone, returning it to the best possible shape. If the fracture has splinters, they should be removed.
Return each splinter to its place with all your effort. If a splinter appears detached, piercing the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, cut it open and remove it, as described above. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate section is dedicated to it.
If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of the setting, apply a cloth soaked in rose-infused ointment, made with white wax and rosin, of a medium thickness. Apply the cloth with gentle pressure until the swelling subsides.
Remove the ointment cloth and apply a dressing prepared from mill dust mixed with egg white. Then apply splints. The splint for the fracture itself should be slightly wider and stronger.  Know that, in most cases, six splints will be needed for the arm.
The fracture could be in one forearm bone or both.  Then, apply stronger traction to the fracture site.  The further the traction extends up or down the arm, the gentler the traction should be, as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
The cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not overly stiff.  The thread used for tying should be linen, of medium thickness as described before.  Check the limb and ligaments after several days.
If a problem arises, such as irritation of the limb, it should be bathed in warm water until the irritation subsides. Leave the limb unstrapped overnight to allow rest, and then resume traction.
If the traction has become loose, and the bone has shifted or similar, rectify it with all your effort.  Also, if the limb is not receiving nourishment due to excessive traction, you should loosen it somewhat, leave it for several days to allow nourishment to flow, and then re-apply traction.
If the patient experiences none of the problems mentioned, the dressing should not be removed until twenty days or so.  Then, suspend the patient's arm around his neck, keeping the arm straight.  He should avoid strenuous movement, and sleep on his back.
Know that this arm fracture is set within thirty, or thirty-two, days, and perhaps in twenty-eight days, all depending on the individual's constitution and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On setting the fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but are often injured.  So, when a fracture or injury occurs to the hand...
The patient should sit in a crouched position, with a chair in front of him at a level height.
Then he places his hand on it, outstretched, and the attendant extends the fractured bones, and the physician straightens them until they are well-aligned. Then, if no hot swelling occurs, a bandage and splints should be applied; then a splint is to be placed over the area.
And it should be wrapped in soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball shape from cloth, and instruct the patient to grip it with his fractured hand, then secure it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of soft leather so that the skin may heal along with the curvature of the entire hand, and secure it as needed. If the fracture is outward, then a splint should be placed on top and another splint underneath in the hand to maintain the hand open and upright.
Then, the bandage is applied as the hand rotates, and the fingers are bound together with a bandage. If the fracture is on one of the fingers, if it is the thumb, then it should be straightened and secured with the hand. Or, if desired, a small, upright splint may be used to support the fracture.
And he must not move. If the fracture is to any of the other fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or pinky finger, it should be straightened and tied to the adjacent, healthy finger, or all the fingers should be tied together.  This is better.  Or a small upright splint can be placed on them.
As we said about the thumb, and examine during and after the setting of the fracture, and address any inflammatory swelling that arises, according to the previously described method.
Chapter Fourteen.
In setting a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone often breaks and is easily felt because it moves back and forth.  It is set by applying a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture, with the patient lying on their face.
Then each attendant stretches the bandage in their direction to equalize it, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  But if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material, should be used.
At the origin of the thigh, towards the pubic area, the pull should be upward, and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee, with the pull downward. Then the physician should align the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well.  Then, the bandage and pressure should be maintained unless the limb develops a hot swelling. If swelling does occur, leave it for several days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to treatment.
As for the pressure, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture twice or three times, leaving excess material. Then, the leg should be wrapped until the heel is at the origin of the thigh.  A long thread should be inserted between the thigh and the leg, near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread should extend above on both sides.
Then, wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Then place splints on the thigh, at the site of the fracture itself, and make one splint on the leg bone.  Fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft cloths, so the pressure is equalized.
Then begin with firm pressure from the center on the fracture site, three or four wraps.  As the bandage moves away from the fracture site, the pressure should become less firm, softer, and looser.
Then, take the ends of the thread you inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie the adjacent splints above to it. Then, take the ends of the thread down to the heel of the leg, and also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side with them.
So that the bandage does not move from its place.  Then, maintain the pressure as long as no sores, swelling, or puffiness occur on the limb.  If any of this happens, quickly loosen it and repair whatever happened, as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter in the bone that pierces the skin, you should smooth it out if possible. Otherwise, cut over it and remove it. Treat the wound as previously described until healed.  This fracture of the thigh may be treated without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned in the arm and forearm, except that setting it this way does not cause lameness for the patient.  If it is set alone, without being joined to the leg, the patient will invariably become lame.
Know that the thigh is set for fifty days, or a little more, or a little less, all depending on the variations in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
In the fracture of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely fractures, but is often bruised. If it does fracture, it is either a split or a fragmentation of its parts, and this may occur with or without a wound.
Treatment depends on careful examination, and involves aligning the fractured pieces with the fingers until they are joined together, according to the ability to carefully and gently reposition them. Then, apply a bandage and a circular splint if necessary.
Then, apply further binding that corresponds to this, and then all cases described previously for other fractures, such as a hot swelling, are treated, addressing each symptom appropriately until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
In setting fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones; one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the ulna.  They are susceptible to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, its setting is like that of the arm, and the procedure is the same.  If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, especially from below, this is evident to you.
Therefore, use traction and straightening, and bind the splints accordingly, just as in the arm.  However, if the leg fracture is a severe one with many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and exert care in setting it.
There is something extra in the procedure for the leg compared to the arm.  After you have straightened the splints and completed all your work, take two pieces of juniper wood, used for smoothing the walls of rooms, or palm fronds, or something similar.
Choose pieces of moderate thickness, not thin, and have them the length of the leg, from the knee down.  Then wrap two layers of cloth around each, lengthwise. Place one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them be from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the pieces of wood in three places, at the ends and the middle. With this support, the leg will be prevented from tilting to the right or left and will be properly secured. A wooden trough may also be used along the length of the leg.
Place it [the trough/support] inside to prevent movement, especially if there is a wound accompanying the fracture.  Carefully examine the leg every day, closely observing for swelling or inflammation or any other such condition. If anything of that sort occurs, address it accordingly until it heals. The leg bone will be set in approximately thirty days.
Chapter Seventeen.
Fractures of the leg and fingers.
The heelbone is never fractured.  Leg bones may be fractured, and fingers are rarely fractured, but rather are more often bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bone occurs and you see the bones are overlapping, the patient should place their foot flat on the ground, standing as they normally would. Then, place your foot on the protruding bone and press down, straightening it.
Until the bones are in their proper places. Then, take the bandage and splint, placing a small, flat board with two flat ends underneath the sole of the foot.  Tightly bind the foot with cloth and anything else needed.
After three or four days, release the bandage.  You will find the bones are straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The treatment is as described.  If some of the fingers are broken, realign and straighten them as described in the treatment for finger fractures.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger, extending the length of the finger and slightly wider than it.  Place the board, which I described earlier, under the foot, and bind it tightly.  If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Make a splint for each finger, sized accordingly, and wrapped in soft cloth. Secure the board beneath the foot, and position each end of the board extending beyond the sole of the foot to provide proper support.
You should not forget to apply all that we have described regarding fractures and treat each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
Fractures of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, position her squatting, then gently lean her back slightly, supporting her from behind.  Then, the midwife should pack her vulva with cotton until it is full, like a ball.
Then, have the woman shake and lift her pelvis slightly, and the cotton will come out until it forms a ball at the entrance of the vulva, and the fracture will be healed.  Then place a support on her back.  When she wishes to urinate, remove the cotton gently so she may urinate.
Return it to its original position, as it was inserted initially, then return to the initial support.  Do this for seven days or so, and it will heal.  If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, attach a short tube to its opening, and place the entire bladder inside her vulva.
Then, blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, and the fracture will return.  Then pack it with cotton and maintain the described care for the necessary number of days until healed.  And if the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this method is used to heal it.
And the treatment is the same as described for the hip bone.  The correct approach to these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, is clear to you, especially if you possess some expertise and understand my book well.  Indeed, the fracture itself will often guide you.
For correct treatment, understand the method of applying pressure for healing.  Regarding a fractured penis in a man, take a goose's esophagus, insert the penis into it, and then place a bandage made of cloth over it and bind it tightly.  Leave it for three days or so until healed.
Chapter Nineteen.
On setting fractures of bones if there is a wound.
If a fracture is accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, one should immediately perform venesection if the conditions for it, as previously described, permit.
If the wound bleeds, you should immediately cauterize it with pulverized alum if nothing else is available.  Then, proceed with setting the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, if a hot swelling does occur, then postpone setting the fracture until the ninth day, until the hot swelling subsides. Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for it exposes one to unfavorable symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, exposed, then you should gently and carefully attempt to return it to its proper position and smooth it out with your hands, with a slight stretching motion.  If you cannot return it and smooth it with your hands, return it with this instrument, which is an instrument made of iron, seven or eight fingers in length. And its width should match the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of these, according to the treatment needed for each type of fracture.  Let them be rounded, with a slight thickness so they will not bend when used.
And they should be sharp at the ends, with a bend at one end. The top should be thick, and thinner from the middle to the bottom.  This is its image/form:.
It is called in Greek "perem," meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent end should be placed on the protruding bone end and pushed with it once.  When the bone is returned and somewhat positioned, then the broken ends are carefully aligned.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can hold it. If the bone cannot be repositioned as described, then cut it with a similar tool as we have mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, as best you can. Then, remove any remaining roughness or thin scales from the bone.  If, after repositioning the bone, the patient experiences severe, painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its proper position.
If you can reposition it to its natural position, do so, as you will greatly benefit the patient.  When the bone setting is complete, soak a cloth in a dark, astringent liquid, especially if it is summer. Do not apply lint or anything oily to the wound.
So that putrefaction and decay do not occur. Then, use splints when you are finished setting the bone. Leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages, using scissors, to the size of the wound.
Beware, with great caution, of tightening the wound with the fracture. Often, the ignorant physicians do this, causing their patients either death or an ulcer or a cold. Let your tightening be gentle and relaxed, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or if it is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and if the patient experiences disturbing pain in the area, you should not apply splints. Instead, make bandages of strong cloths in the place of splints and tighten them.
If, after a day or two, you see pus starting to form in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the liquid that you placed on it.  Then use the bandages and ointments that we customarily use to treat wounds, like the four-ingredient ointment, etc.  You should loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  You should position the limb so that pus drains downwards easily. If the wound takes many days to heal, and the pus does not stop, know that there are small pieces of bone fragments.
You should probe the wound with a probe. Any bone fragments that have come loose, remove them.  If any remain lodged and are causing pain, cut through them and remove them by any means possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or ulceration, or another form of decay or putrefaction, you should counter each symptom with the corresponding treatment described in its section.
If a large, prominent bone such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar large bones, are broken, do not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often death results from this.  Leave it until it rots; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the complications that arise after some fractures.
This complication often arises after the fracture has healed, especially near the joints.  It disfigures the limb and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
If the complication is fresh, use astringent medications such as myrrh, frankincense, aloes, acacia, and similar ones, by taking some or all of these and mixing them with an astringent drink or egg white or vinegar.
Apply this to the complication, firmly bandaging it.  Leave the bandage for many days, then remove it and apply another until the swelling goes away, God willing; or apply a tight lead plate.
Lead has the property of removing any swelling in the limbs.  If the swelling has hardened and become severe, and necessity demands its removal, cut it open from above, and remove the protruding excess or scrape it with instruments until it disappears, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb, after being set, remains thin and not as its original form.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either excessive loosening and improper tying of the ligaments, or excessive tightening of the ligaments preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or excessive and improper use of splints; or excessive movements at improper times; or insufficient and weak blood supply in the patient's body.
Treatment for this includes nourishing the patient and enriching his body so that the blood increases, using baths, bringing joy and happiness, and similar measures.  Then, applying pitch to the limb to draw nourishment, or continuing to splint it with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On treating fractured bones that are forcibly straightened and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after the healing of a limb that has been set, a crookedness or projection of the fractured bone appears or the limb is deformed and disfigured. However, if the limb has not been prevented from its natural function, one should not accept the claim of those who assert that the limb should be broken from its head, as many ignorant physicians and setters do in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great risk, at best resulting in injury. But if the crookedness and deformity are recent, then it should be treated with water in which relaxing herbs such as the leaves, roots, and crown imperial, and the like, have been boiled.
And it should be bandaged with relaxing bandages, such as well-crafted diachylon, or the juice of the root of the plantains should be taken, mixed with chicken fat and she-camel fat, and bandaged with it, or thick figs should be pounded with pigeon droppings and similar medications called resolvents.
And the deformity may be resolved by continuous, gentle massage with the hands, and the limb should be moved in every direction at all times.  If the crookedness has progressed, intensified, and become hard, and the necessity arises to treat it with iron.
Then the affected area should be split open, the bone connection released, and any excess deformity or bone cut away with fine incisions.  Care and diligence should be used in this process.  Then the wound should be treated with the previously mentioned methods until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocation of a joint from its place, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the afflicted intense pain and suffering.  If a person experiences a dislocated jaw, they should promptly attempt to realign it.
Delaying treatment is absolutely forbidden.  If delayed, the affected area will swell, and a tumor will form, making jaw realignment difficult.  Therefore, one should not delay, nor move nor stretch the jaw while it is swollen, as this often results in painful spasms and suffering for the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should first bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat.  Then, apply warm water and ointment to the limb.  Then, gently realign the jaw, and treat each affected area as described in its respective location.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged according to what was previously discussed regarding fractures, from the upper to the lower body.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely become dislocated, and their displacement occurs in one of two ways: either they slip slightly from their positions and relax somewhat, or they completely dislocate and relax entirely. To the extent of the chest, so that the patient's saliva drips and he cannot contain it, nor can he close his jaw or speak, articulating his tongue.  However, if the dislocation is slight, it will in most cases return to its place with the least effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to quickly return it, and not delay at all.  A servant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw within the patient's mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or, if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, the physician should insert both thumbs, and the rest of their fingers from outside, to adjust the jaw.  The physician instructs the patient to relax and open their jaw completely, and while the patient moves the jaw in all directions, the physician adjusts and pushes it back into place.
If it is difficult to return them, especially if both jaws are dislocated, use warm water and ointment compresses to make returning them easier. Do not delay their return, as we have said.  If they have returned to their proper position, the patient's mouth is closed, and they are not relaxed, then place bandages with a mixture of wax and rose ointment on them.
Then gently tie with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back, with their head propped up between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left, and do not attempt to chew anything. Their food should be soft and easily swallowed.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is healed, the patient may eat whatever they like.  They should use gentle care, and not force their mouth open for eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw is healed.
If the jaws are difficult to return to their proper position after dislocation, there is often fever, persistent headache, and the patient's stomach may be upset and they may vomit repeatedly.  If you see this, know that the condition is serious.
Often, those who experience this condition for ten days die.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On repositioning the clavicle and shoulder joint.
The clavicle does not dislocate from the inside due to its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate outwards, and this is apparent to the touch.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back and extend their arms.
Then, press the area firmly with your hand; this will reposition it.  Then apply a bandage and splints, securing them.  The part of the clavicle adjacent to the shoulder rarely dislocates.  If it does, it should be repositioned as described, using whatever method is available.
Then apply a bandage, splints, and dressings, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  Using this same treatment, the shoulder joint can also be repositioned if it becomes dislocated.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In reducing a shoulder dislocation.
Know that the shoulder joint dislocates in three ways: one is downward toward the armpit, the second is toward the chest, and the third, rarely, is upward towards the shoulder.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade, nor in front of the nerve.  Most often, it dislocates and comes out downward toward the armpit, especially in those with thin musculature, because it emerges and re-enters quickly in them.
However, in those with thick musculature, it is the opposite; it emerges and re-enters with difficulty.  Sometimes, a person suffers a blow or a fall and the shoulder swells, with a hot, inflamed lump, and they suspect it is dislocated. Therefore, it is necessary to examine this thoroughly to determine its true state.
Then, in treating it, if the dislocation is downward toward the armpit, when you compare the dislocated shoulder to the healthy one, you will find a visible difference.  You will find a prominence on the head of the dislocated shoulder, and under the armpit, when palpated, the head of the shoulder feels like an egg.
And the patient will not be able to raise their arm to their ear, nor perform any other arm movements.  Similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upward, this will be evident on palpation.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have an attendant raise the arm upward. Then, place your thumbs under the armpit and lift the joint forcefully upward to its proper position.  The attendant should raise and extend the arm upward.
Then bring it downward, and it will return quickly.  However, if it does not return as described, and the dislocation has been present for a considerable time, the patient should take a warm bath and use remedies that relax and soften the muscles.  Examples include cooked fennel, fenugreek, and king's crown in water.
Then, the patient lies on their back, and a ball of moderately soft wool is placed under their armpit.  The physician then positions their heel on the ball, and grasps the patient's arm, extending it downward.
And the physician pushes the ball with their foot, and the arm will return to its place.  Or, if you prefer, another method is to have a taller person stand beside the patient, positioning their shoulder under the patient's armpit.  Then they raise the patient's arm upward so that the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant pulls the patient's arm downward towards their abdomen. If the patient is light, something should be attached to weigh them down.  This will cause the arm to return immediately.  And the dislocation may also be reduced in another way.
Another method is to place a long, round-headed piece of wood on the ground, similar to the pestle head of a mortar, neither thick nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on top of the wood.  The patient stands along the length of the wooden rod, with the rod placed under their armpit.
Then the patient extends their hand downward on the other side, and also extends their body forcefully on the other side. The joint will quickly return to its proper place.  If, despite all the methods described, the reduction is difficult, use this treatment: take a piece of wood approximately two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a rounded head, so that it can easily enter the armpit in this way.
Then a soft cloth is tied to the rounded head to prevent the wood from injuring the patient.  The wood is placed under the armpit. The entire arm and forearm are extended along the wood downward. The wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a support beam.  The hand is extended downward, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. The joint will then return to its place.  Once the joint has been reduced in any way possible, a moderately-sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then, a dressing prepared from mill dust, frankincense, and egg white, is applied to the entire shoulder, wrapping around from above.  The ball is firmly secured under the armpit, and the bandage is wrapped around the dressing from above.  The patient's arm is suspended from their neck.
The patient's arm is left undisturbed for seven days.  The patient's diet should be light to strengthen the affected limb, as this speeds recovery.  After seven days, or five, the bandage is removed, and the patient is encouraged to move the limb. If the limb is firm and doesn't relax, then healing is complete.
If the joint repeatedly dislocates due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three layers of thin cloth should be used, as described in the chapter on cauterization.  If all of this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint still does not remain stable,
If the dressing and binding are repeated several times but the joint does not hold, and it slips and relaxes, and the patient is unable to raise the limb, then know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or relaxed.  Understand then that the joint will never remain in place.
For a dislocated jaw, near the chest and breast, and behind, reposition it by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  All binding and treatment should be applied until it heals. If, after healing, there is a sensation of stiffness in the limb and slow movement,
The patient should use a bath repeatedly until the stiffness subsides and returns to its original state.
The twenty-seventh chapter.
On the treatment of a dislocated elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty. It dislocates in all directions, particularly forward or backward.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is apparent to sight and touch, no matter how it has dislocated.
When the dislocated elbow is placed alongside the healthy one, its dislocation becomes clearly apparent by the depression in the joint. The arm cannot be bent, and the shoulder cannot be brought to it.  One must act quickly and relocate the dislocation before a hot swelling occurs.
If a hot swelling appears, relocation becomes difficult, and it may never heal.  Especially if the dislocation is backward, this is the worst type of dislocation, the most painful, and often results in death.
To reduce it, if it is reducible, a servant should extend his arm fully with both hands, and the physician's hands should be placed above and below the elbow.  He should push the joint back into place with both thumbs or the base of his hands.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once until the base of the palm strikes the shoulder it faces.  If the dislocation does not return, use very strong and forceful extension, achieved by two servants extending the arm.
Two servants also hold the patient to prevent movement during the extension.  Then the arm is rotated in all directions after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around it.  If the physician manipulates the joint with his hands, he should first lubricate it with ointment to facilitate the smooth movement of the joint.
Then the joint is pushed firmly until it returns to place.  After it returns, the injured area should be bandaged with a compress containing a drying agent mixed with egg white. The bandage should be applied tightly and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then it is removed.  If the joint is firmly in place, remove the bandage and leave it.  If you see that the joint has not solidified, reapply the bandage and leave it for several days until it does strengthen.  Then remove the bandage.  If there is swelling after the joint has returned and been bandaged.
If movement is difficult, use warm baths, gentle massage, and kneading until it becomes supple.  Or, place a thick, fat lamb's fleece over the joint, bandage it, and leave it in place for a day and a night.  Then remove it and take the patient to a warm bath.  If the patient sweats, gently massage the joint.
Then repeat the fleece application and warm bath three times until the joint becomes flexible. If you prefer, apply dampened, finely ground cow hide mixed with ghee, and apply it several times. This will also soften the joint and return it to its normal condition.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than other joints, but it is essential to quickly reduce the dislocation at the time of its occurrence, before inflammation or a hot swelling develops in the area.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, have an attendant extend the patient's arm, and the physician should place his hand over the bony prominence of the joint and push until it returns. However, it is necessary to check if the dislocation has gone towards the palm of the hand.
Let the patient place their palm on the board when extending and reducing.  If the dislocation is outwards (on the back of the hand), then let the patient's palm be on the board so the physician's hand falls upon the same bony prominence of the joint.  If it returns immediately, then do so; otherwise, apply a bandage that soothes the inflammation.
Leave it alone, do not repeat the procedure; it cannot withstand it, and you cannot reduce it after days have passed, except that the joint will remain deformed and will not harm the patient, unless the hand weakens and cannot grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve is severed or bruised, and there is no remedy for it except to treat with cautery. Sometimes it helps, sometimes it does not.  Once the wrist is reduced, apply the bandage we described, then tightly bind it and leave it for five days.
Then, it is released, and the hand is exercised.  If its movement is difficult and something painful is present, soften it with heated water and rubbing repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  If a finger dislocates to the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to its place. Then bandage the fingertip and support it towards the direction in which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight that day.  If it is nighttime, bandage it in the same way. Continue to release it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bandage it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Likewise, do the same if it dislocates to the palm of the hand; bandage it towards the same direction and follow your initial procedure until it is healed. Do likewise if it dislocates to any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On treating the dislocation of the spine.
When a vertebra in the spine or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae have been displaced from their positions, there is no treatment because death quickly comes to the afflicted.
A sign of this is that the afflicted's stool comes out involuntarily, and he cannot retain it. Often, some of his limbs, either his legs or arms, or one of them, become relaxed.  If a single vertebra is displaced, it often gets displaced.
Its displacement can occur in four directions.  That which moves backward is called a hump.  Its treatment is that you should observe; if the hump was caused by childhood disease, there is no treatment for it, and no recovery whatsoever.  However, if it was caused by a fall, injury, or something similar...
The ancients described various treatments with lengthy explanations, much of which is ultimately of little use.  I have condensed this to what is sufficient, avoiding much of their lengthy explanations and descriptions. I have depicted the mechanism differently from what they explained.
I say that a hump occurring in the front of the chest has no cure, nor recovery.  The same applies to those occurring to the sides.  Only those occurring in the back are treatable, specifically by the method I am describing:  have the afflicted lie on their face.
On a flat platform near a wall, spread a damp cloth underneath to avoid injuring his chest. Then place a vertical board, planted in a hole in the ground, near his head at one edge of the platform, and another board near his feet at the other edge of the platform.
A servant holds one board, ensuring it's not firmly fixed in the hole, and another servant holds the other in the same manner.  Then a soft, strong bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits.  The end of the bandage is extended to the board near the patient's head and tied to it.
Then, a second bandage is tightened around his hips, knees, and ankles.  All the bandages are gathered and tied to the other board, near the patient's feet.  Each servant pulls the board towards their side with the bandage, and the boards remain in their embedded positions.
However, the boards are not secured, as we said. The physician places his hands firmly on the hump until it returns, or places a board on top of it, and then leans on the board with his feet until the hump returns.  If this treatment does not work, take a board about three cubits long.
A cavity is carved into the wall, which we mentioned should be near the patient, where the end of the board can be inserted. Then, the middle of the board is placed on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end, pulling firmly to apply pressure to the hump.
It will return to its place. If you wish, create this using a hand-wound torsion spring by placing two boards, each a cubit long, in the ground at the head of the patient, and another two at their feet, at the far end of the stall.
Let there be a span between each board.  A hole is made in each board, through which the torsion spring passes. The boards are firmly secured to the ground so that they do not move at all. A twisted stick, the torsion spring, is inserted, with the bandage being wound in the holes of both boards.
At its end is a hole secured by a stick a span long, twisted accordingly. The same is done with the other two boards. Then, the bandage tightened at the patient's chest is attached to the torsion spring at their head, and the bandages tightened on the patient's legs are attached to the torsion spring at their feet.
Then a servant stands by each torsion spring, twisting the spring with the twisting tool, while the physician adjusts the hump as previously described. This is a depiction of the torsion spring, the stall, and the patient.
Then, after the vertebrae return to their proper place and the area is aligned, the dried bandage should be applied, first with egg white, then with a plaster.  A splint made of wood, about three fingers wide, and long enough to cover the hump, should then be placed over the bandage.
And on some sound bead, then it is tied with the appropriate bandage. The patient should use soft food until recovery. If some protrusion remains in the area after healing, then the softening and relaxing medicines should be used, along with the splint described for a long time.
A lead sheet may also be used in this case.  A protrusion may appear in the final vertebrae of the back, which may be mistaken for a dislocation.  It could be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not subject it to this treatment, as death may result.
Chapter Thirty-one.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and deformity that afflicts other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward.  It dislocates inward most often, and rarely forward or backward.  A sign of dislocation inward is that if you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected leg, the healthy leg will be longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend their leg at the groin.  The area below the groin will be swollen and noticeably distended, indicating that the head of the thigh has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outward are the opposite of these signs.  The sign of dislocation forward is that the leg is fully extended but cannot be bent without pain in the knee, and if attempting to walk, the patient cannot move forward.
The patient will have urinary retention and groin pain.  When walking, the patient will place the heel on the ground.  The sign of dislocation backward is that the knee cannot be straightened or bent before bending the groin, and the leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the thigh is clearly visible near the hip.  As for the reduction of these dislocations: observe.  If the dislocation is old and has persisted without attempts at reduction, there is no treatment at all.
Therefore, it should not be attempted.  As for a recent dislocation, regardless of the four types of dislocation, quickly manipulate the joint, extending it inward and outward, and moving it left and right.  Perhaps...
If it returns without further treatment, then if it does not return this way, a strong, capable assistant should hold the leg from below, either with their hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant should pull from above.
By inserting their hands under the armpits, and then applying a soft bandage to the root of the thigh, and a third assistant holding the end of the bandage, pulling either from the front, from the groin towards the collarbone, or from behind, towards the back.
All the assistants should pull simultaneously until the patient rises from the ground and hangs suspended.  This type of traction is common to all four types. If the dislocation is reduced as described, otherwise, each type requires its specific treatment.
For its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their healthy side, then a bandage should be placed on the root of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area beneath the groin.
Then, the bandage is pulled upward from the groin toward the upper body and collarbone.  A strong assistant then grasps the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and pulls forcefully outward; this will return it to its proper position.
This method is easier than other methods for reducing this limb. If this type of treatment is unsuccessful, then you should bind the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage around the heels and knees.
Each should be a distance of about four fingers from its companion, and the affected leg should be extended farther than the other by about two fingers.  The patient should then be suspended from the head by a wooden beam in the house, a distance of approximately two arms from the ground.
Then, a strong servant is instructed to grasp the head of the thigh while another servant is to support the patient; the servant grasping the thigh should push with force. The joint will quickly return to its place.  As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is outward...
The patient should lie on a raised surface, as described in the case of a hump, and the bandage is tightened on the affected leg, specifically around the chest.  Then, one plank is placed by the patient's feet, and another by the head, and an additional plank is placed firmly in the middle of the raised surface.
Wet cloths should be wrapped around it to prevent injury to the patient.  The plank should be placed between the patient's thighs, to prevent downward movement during stretching.  Then each attendant should pull in their direction, and the physician should adjust the hip with their hands, if it returns to its place.
Otherwise, place the board on him, and compress as described for the hump, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side.  If the dislocation is forward, the affected leg should be extended once while in this same position on the raised surface.
The physician places the right hand on the affected hip and presses it with the other hand.  At the same time, the pressure should be extended downward toward the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be stretched downward.
He is elevated from the ground, but should be placed on a firm surface.  Also, the hip and knee should be outward, as described in his position on the raised surface, lying on his face, with the bandages tightened.
As we said earlier, compression with the board should also be used on the area where the joint has dislocated.  Once the hip joint returns correctly, a sign of its return, which should be clear to you, is that the patient's leg extends.
If you see them even and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned correctly.  Then, join the thighs, apply the bandage, and secure it with a turban in such a way that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain at rest for three or four days. Then, loosen the bandage and bandage, and compare the legs.  If you find them equal in size, know that the fracture has set. Release the patient to walk. If you notice any weakness, however.
Return and bandage it, tightening as you did at first. Leave it for three days, then loosen it.  Let the patient walk slowly for several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of a fractured knee.
The knee fractures in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards (i.e., backwards).  It does not fracture forwards.  A sign of a fracture is to ask the patient to draw the leg towards the thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured.  The method of reduction for all types of fractures is that the patient sit with legs extended, if able.  A servant sits behind him, holding his waist and gently bending it backwards.  Then, you sit on his thighs.
Then, place your back against his, and put his leg between your legs.  Then grasp his knee with both hands, interlacing your fingers on his knee. Then, press firmly with both hands on the sides of his knee. Another attendant should extend the leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign that it has returned to its place is that the leg adheres to the thigh, softly and without force. Then bandage it, and fasten the leg to the thigh, and bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them.  Only minimal walking should be permitted for several days until strength returns.
If you cannot return it using the methods described, then use strong traction with the bandages described previously for treating the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be dislocated slightly, or completely.  Dislocation may be inward or outward.  A sign of dislocation is to see the heel swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
As for treating a slight dislocation, it is easily returned by gently pulling with the hands and aligning it until it returns.  However, if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit, and a strong assistant should hold it from behind the middle of the patient's back.
Then, with your right hand, grasp the foot from above, and with your left hand, grasp below the foot, at the heel.  Then pull the foot toward you with your right hand, then with your left hand, twice.  Then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, without force.
Repeat this twice as described.  Then, on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling on the heel. If it returns in one or two attempts with this method and you see the foot is aligned, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the procedure; it will return.
If it does not return with the methods described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground, and firmly plant and secure a firmly fixed object in the ground, positioned between their legs. A cloth should be wrapped around it to prevent injury to the patient. Then, an assistant should hold the patient's thigh.
Then another assistant extends the leg, either with their hands or with a bandage tied around the patient's leg.  Each assistant should pull in the opposite direction, and the stake should be firmly positioned between the patient's legs to prevent the body from pulling downwards during the stretching. Then the physician straightens the joint with their hands.
Another assistant holds the healthy leg downwards.  If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splint, and secure it with firm bandages. Bind the foot downwards with a bandage.  Care must be taken to avoid the nerve located behind the heel.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes injury. Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again. Then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If the patient attempts to walk before this time, there is a risk that the joint will become dislocated and treatment will be unsuccessful. If a hot swelling appears, the methods previously described in other parts of the treatment and poultice section should be used to reduce it until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocated toes.
One should gently straighten the dislocated toe by a slight stretch, without force. This is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the toes on the top of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or, while standing as if walking, on a board.  Then place your foot on the protruding joints and press firmly with your foot until they return to their normal position and no protrusion is visible.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, encompassing the entire foot and having two ends.  Bind it tightly for three days. Then release it and keep them from walking for many days until it strengthens and a recurrence is unlikely, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of dislocation that occurs with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both.
When something of this kind occurs and you attempt treatment and reduction, death frequently follows.
Therefore, one should not undertake the treatment of such a case unless they are skilled in the art, long-practiced, gentle, compassionate, cautious, and not rash or reckless.  In the beginning, they should only use remedies that soothe hot tumors and let the patient rest.
Except by God's grace, unless you hope for the patient's safe recovery with slight illness and some hope appears to you, then return it from the very beginning, before a hot tumor arises.
If the limb returns to its proper place, use the treatment that relieves hot tumors and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments.  If the jaw is fractured and splinters of bone have detached, remove them. Follow in this regard what we have mentioned regarding simple illnesses in their appropriate places previously.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering into a path of recklessness, as I have previously advised.  This will preserve your reputation and safeguard your standing, God willing.
